             FIFTHPOVERTYREDUCTIONSUPPORTCREDIT (PRSC-5)


I Borrower           United ReDublic Of Tanzania
                     Ministryo fFinance
L                    SDR 127.6 Million (US$ 190million equivalent)
                     Standard IDA terms: 40-year maturity with a 10-year grace period
I Description
  Tranchin           Single Tranche
                     The proposed poverty reduction support operation (PRSC-5) i s the
                     second in a series o f five annual operations (PRSC-4 - PRSC-8)
                     supporting the implementation o f Tanzania's National Strategy for
                     Growth and Reduction o f Poverty (MKUKUTA). It covers six
                     focus areas. These are the three clusters of the MKUKUTA (ie.,
                     growth and the reduction o f income poverty, improvements of
                     quality o f life and social well being, and governance and
                     accountability), macroeconomic stability, resource allocation and
                     budget consistency, and public financial management.
  Benefits           The proposed operation would support the government to pursue its
                     policy objectives laid out inthe MKUKUTAby providing financing
                     for the execution o f the government's FY08 budget. It would also
                     provide the incentives to implement key economic, social and
                     institutional refoms to strengthen the overall performance o f the
                     economy and contribute to poverty alleviation. Over the period
                     2005-2010, it i s expected that the reform program under the series
                     o f five annual PRSCs would help the government to sustain growth
                     o f around 6-8 percent annually, which i s projected to result in a
                     significant reduction in poverty. It would also help the government
                     in making progress toward reaching the MDGs, namely, increase
                     school enrollment and literacy rates, reduce child and maternal
                     mortality, increase access to safe water, and strengthen the
                     sustainability o f development efforts.
                     (i) Tanzania remains vulnerable to external shocks such as droughts,
                     changes in commodity prices, or fluctuations in aid flows.           The
                     credit encourages the        diversification o f the economy          and
                     institutional reforms that will reduce this vulnerability. This also
                     includes measures to strengthen Tanzania's power sector, where in
                     2006 drought related power shortages highlighted significant
                     structural weaknesses related to the management o f the sector, the
                     financial soundness o f the state owned power utility company, and
                     the structure and technical efficiency o f Tanzania's power
                     generation, distribution, and transmission systems. (ii)As the
                     utilization o f PRSC-5 funds i s determined by the budget process,
                     there i s a risk that resource allocation and use i s not fully consistent
                     with the MKUKUTA. However, government has adopted an open



                                            i

                   I and  participative budget process including an annual PER process
                    which helps to align and monitor resource allocation and its
                     consistency with the MKUKUTA. (iii)The 2006 PEFAR
                     assessment suggests that Tanzania's financial management system i s
                     among the best in Africa, but significant fiduciary risks in the areas
                     o f financial management and procurement remain.           The Bank
                     supports reforms that are expected to reduce these risks.         (iv)
                     Capacity for program implementation and service delivery at both
                    the central and district level i s weak; however, in key areas

Operation IDNumber Iprograms   are inplace to strengthen capacity.

                              IDA PROGRAMDOCUMENT FORA
    PROPOSEDFIFTH POVERTYREDUCTION SUPPORT CREDIT (PRSC-5)
                                          TO TANZANIA




                                    I. INTRODUCTION

1.      The proposed poverty reduction support credit (PRSC-5) inthe amount o f US$190 million
would be the second in a series o f five annual operations supporting the implementation o f
Tanzania's National Strategy for Growth and Reduction o f Poverty (NSGRP or MKUKUTA in
Kiswahili).

2.      PRSC-4, which was approved by the World Bank's Board on M a y 9, 2006, presented the
five year PRSC program in support of the MKUKUTA. The Bank's PRSC support to Tanzania i s
provided within a framework for general budget support (GBS), comprising a Performance
Assessment Framework (PAF), a Partnership Framework Memorandum (PFM) for GBS for the
implementation o f MKUKUTA, and a streamlined review process, in common with 13 other
development partners (DPs) that provide GBS. The framework i s designed to support domestic
accountability, further reduce transaction costs (especially for the government) and encourage the
further development and use o f Tanzania's own systems - rather than setting up and usingparallel
donor systems.

3.      Discussion o f the country context in Section 11 focuses on progress in realizing key
outcomes targeted by the MKUKUTA and supported by the PRSC, namely (1) sustained high
economic growth and accelerated reduction o f income poverty, (2) improvements in non-income
dimensions o f poverty, especially education, health, and water, and (3) strengthened governance.
Section Illoutlines the government's Program followed by the discussion o f the Bank's support to
the government strategy in section IV. Section V then provides details o f the program supported by
the PRSC, highlighting specific reforms implemented by government and progress towards
specific MKUKUTA objectives.

                                 11.      COUNTRY CONTEXT

RECENTECONOMIC DEVELOPMENTSINTANZANIA
4.      A drought and an extended and poorly managed energy crisis resulted in lower than
projected growth in 2006. Tanzania's economy grew by 6.8 percent in 2005. Mining and
construction sustained high growth, with growth rates o f 15.7 and 11.9 percent in 2005,
respectively. The agriculture sector grew by 5.2 percent in 2005. A prolonged drought reduced
growth o f the agriculture sector to about 3.7 percent in 2006.' The drought also led to a sharp
decline in hydro-generation capacity, which in combination with severe structural problems in the
energy sector, delayed procurement o f alternative energy, and high world fuel prices caused a
severe and sustained energy crisis. Load shedding o f up to 12 hours in Dar es Salaam affected
primarily households, but also to a more limitedextent industryand the service sector, with growth
in the manufacturing sector slowing down to an estimated 7.5 percent in 2006 compared to 9
percent in2005. Overall GDP growth for 2006 i s estimated to be around 5.8 percent.

1 GDP estimates for 2006 are based onpreliminaryestimates for the frst three quarters of 2006.

5.      Accelerated private and public sector investment lay a strong foundation for
sustained economic growth. The investment to GDP ratio increased from 17 percent in 2001 to
21.9 percent in 2005. Both public and the private investment grew substantially to 7.5 and 14.4
percent in 2005, respectively. Foreign direct investment (FDI) continues to play an important role
inTanzania, with an estimated inflow ofUS$473 million in2005. A large share ofthe FDIgoes
into the mining sector, but manufacturing, tourism, agriculture, the financial sector, and trade are
also benefiting from sustainedFDIinflows.

6.      Raising Tanzania's export competitiveness remains a challenge. Tanzania's exports of
goods more than doubled between 2000 and 2005, mostly due to gold, which grew from U S 9 1
million in2000 to US$ 1,322 million in2005, while traditional exports increased at a much slower
pace growing from US$292 million in 2000 to US$355 million in2005. Tourism i s another major
source o f foreign exchange for Tanzania, with foreign exchange receipts from travel increasing
from US$377 million in 2000 to US$824 million in 2005. Since imports o f goods and services
have also been rising, the current account deficit (excluding current official transfers) increased
from 10 percent o f GDP in 2000 to 11.8 percent in 2005. However, most o f the increase in the
current account deficit represents absorption o f foreign aid.

7.      Fiscal policy remains consistent with macro-economic stability objectives - however
Tanzania's government owned power sector utility TANESCO has a large operational deficit
that needs to be addressed urgently. Total central government expenditures increased from 22
percent o f GDP in 2003/04 to 25.6 percent o f GDP in 2005/06. This increase was made possible
by improved domestic revenue performance, which increased from 13.3 to 14.1 percent o f GDP,
and increased borrowing, both from domestic and concessional external sources. Domestic
financing o f the budget amounted to 2.3 percent of GDP in 2005/06. High oil prices combined
with increased use o f thermal power generation have resulted inhigher cost o f energy production
and sharply increased transfer payments by the Treasury to TANESCO.

8.      Monetary policy and financial sector development support continued low inflation
and increased credit to the private sector. Relative rapid growth o f the money supply (M2) by
31.6 percent during2005/06 was consistent with continued low inflation, which was 5.0 percent in
the same year (end o f period), despite a significant increase in the price o f oil. Growth o f credit to
the private sector continued to grow rapidly in 2005106 by 35.9 percent with the stock o f credit
reaching 10.9 percent o f GDP. Strong demand for credit and increasing competition led to an
increase in interest rates on time deposits from 5.3 percent in December 2005 to 7.2 percent in
December 2006. As a result o f increased competition, the increase in posted lending rates from
14.9 percent to 16.4 was more modest and the interest spread continued to narrow.

9.      In summary, Tanzania continues to implement sound macro-economic policies,
which provide a conducive environment for progress towards the MKUKUTA objectives of
high growth and reduced poverty. The drought and high energy prices led to a projected
increase in inflation to 5.8 percent in 2006. However, as inflationary pressures are expected to
subside in 2007, inflation i s expected to fall to about 5 percent. The implementation o f a financial
recovery plan for TANESCO i s critical to avoid budgetary pressures which could result in an
increased fiscal deficit or the crowding out o f growth and poverty related expenditures.
Tanzania's high aid dependency makes it vulnerable to fluctuations in aid flows. It i s accordingly
important that the structure o f Tanzania's expenditure remains flexible enough to be able to
accommodate changes in the resource envelope without creating macro-economic distortions.
Power sector reforms to reduce the fiscal drain from that sector are critical to ensure continued
fiscal sustainability o f the government's reform program. Monetary policy has been successful in


                                                    2

sustaining low inflation. Financial deepening of the economy and structural reforms that will
strengthen the international competitiveness of the economy are critical to mitigate potential
negative impacts o f large aid inflows on macro-economic stability and on Tanzania's international
competitiveness.

MACROECONOMIC OUTLOOK AND DEBT SUSTAINABILITY
10.        Growth for 2007 is projected to increase to above 7 percent. Heavy rains in December
2006 and early 2007 have filled the dams and restored Tanzania's hydro power generation
capacity. At the same time, thermal back up capacity i s being put in place, which significantly
reduces the prospects for a repeated energy crisis in 2007. However, heavy rains have also
damaged the transport infrastructure.               The impact o f heavy rains on the growth prospects o f
agricultural production has yet to be fully evaluated. The secondary and tertiary sectors as well as
miningare projectedto remainthe primary drivers o feconomic growth.




                                                           3

11.      The recent growth acceleration i s grounded in macro-economic stabilization and far
reaching structural reforms.     However, it also benefited substantially from the demand side
impulses o f increasing government spending financed by increasing aid flows. Once growth in
government spending slows down, it will be critical that exports and demand by the private
sector for domestically produced goods becomethe locomotives of growth. This will require a
strengthening o f the international competitiveness o f the Tanzanian economy, but also measures
that accelerate structural change and allow the Tanzanian economy to produce a wider range o f
products and services. Improving international competitiveness requires increased attention to the
provision o f infrastructure, especially in the energy and transport sectors to keep up with high
GDP growth rates in order to prevent bottlenecks that could constrain economic growth. Other
measures to reduce the cost o f doing business in Tanzania relate to the regulatory and licensing
regime, the justice sector which has direct implications for access to credit, and macro-economic
policies that are consistent with exchange and interest rates that support the competitiveness o f the
Tanzanian economy.         Accelerated structural change requires foremost the expansion and
strengthening o f post-primary education, but also an increased focus on improving the information
and communication infrastructure and an improved innovation regime, including the promotion o f
foreign direct investment, access to and adaptation o f new technologies, as well as an appropriate
incentive regime for entrepreneurial activities that result in a widening o f the range o f products
and services produced inTanzania.

12.      The CEM underlines that continued fast growth of the manufacturing sector is
critical for Tanzania's sustained economic growth and structural transformation. However,
in order to generate shared growth that benefits the poor, greater attention to the
development of rural areas i s necessary. To date, the impact o f reforms undertaken has been
visible primarily in urban areas, especially Dar es Salaam, while rural areas have seen much less
systemic improvements in growth performance and poverty reduction. The C E M highlights five
areas where reform programs should scale up the focus on rural areas. First, improved access to
markets i s central to increasing real incomes o f the rural population. This requires investments in
road infrastructure, but also the removal o f constraints to the establishment o f storage facilities and
market facilitation (standards, business climate). The second are financial sector reforms, where
despite the overall progress made, rural areas remain largely cut o f f from access to financial
services, which in turn has been identified as a key constraint to both farm and off-farm activities
inrural areas. Third are public expenditure reforms, where the increases in the overall resource
envelope have primarily accrued to central government agencies and to a much lesser extent to
local governments. Further progress in the development o f a sound intergovernmental fiscal
framework i s a necessary precondition to strengthen the development, operation and maintenance
o f local infrastructure and delivery o f local services, which inturn directly impact on rural growth

This is not a blanket license to spend on agriculture which is essentially a private sector activity -
and poverty reduction. Fourth are government policies and expenditures to support agriculture.

rather it suggests that public expenditures on research and extension and irrigation play an
important role insupporting the sector.

13.      The CEM also highlightsthe importance of paying greater attention to largeregional
differences in growth and poverty reduction performance in Tanzania. This will require a
focus on creating conditions that allow the exploitation o f region and district specific growth
opportunities, inaddition to the targeted provision o f social services.

 14.     Debt sustainability Followingthe implementationo f the Enhanced Highly Indebted Poor
Countries (HIPC) and the Multilateral Debt Relief (MDFU) initiatives, Tanzania's debt



                                                     4

sustainability indicators are well below the debt sustainability thresholds. The net present value of
the debt-to-export ratio i s estimated to be 64 percent in2006.

POVERTYAND INEQUALITY
15.       Poverty simulations and trends in asset ownership suggest that poverty has declined
since the last household budget survey carried out in 2001.                          Poverty i s mostly a rural
phenomenon and 87 percent of the poor live in rural areas. Given the lack of recent data on
poverty incidence, we use poverty simulations to project the likely trajectory of ruralpoverty since
the last householdbudget survey in2000/01. Figure 1, suggests that ever since agricultural growth
exceededper capita income growth, ruralpoverty has been on the declinea2

instance, access to electricity in urban areas increased from 24% in 1992 to 38% in 2004. Inrural
areas, on the other hand, access to electricity did not improve. To date 98% of the rural population
lives without access to electricity. Access to piped water appears to have stagnated too; some
declines can even be observed in access to piped water in urban areas. Great progress in service

by the evolution of asset ownership, access to services and other health related welfare indicators obtained from five
different Demographic and Health Surveys that have beenimplemented inTanzania between 1992 and 2004.

18.        The evolution of health related welfare indicators also provides some room for
cautious optimism (Figure 4): the fraction o f malnourished children has declined, in urban as
well as rural areas. Likewise, the share o f orphaned children in rural areas appears to be on the
decline. Unfortunately the rural decline i s offset by an increase in orphaned children in urban
areas, so that for the nation as a whole the proportion o f children who have lost one or bothparents
has remained constant since 1999.

19.      The evolution o f poverty as implied by the poverty projections and the DHS data does not
capture some of the more recent events such as the increase infood, petrol and charcoal prices and
the electricity crisis which makes it likely that progress inpoverty reduction was more modest (or
has stalled) in 2006. Following the delays o f the rains in October 2005, large parts o f Tanzania
have had to face serious drought conditions. This has translated into a serious deterioration in the
condition o f livestock, and sometimes even large scale losses in the main pastoral areas in central
and northern Tanzania. It has also led to food shortages and hunger. Inurban areas food shortages
translated into substantial food price increases. By July 2006 maize prices were 32% higher
relative to a year before (Figure 5). Prices in isolated markets such as Bukoba or Shinyanga more
than doubled relative to the 5 year average due to the double impact o f increased fuel (transport)
cost and drought conditions. Following the normal cereal harvest that started inM a y and June food
prices have begun to decline, though they remain considerably higher than they were a year ago.

20.      To date, the assessment o f the impact o f the recent acceleration in economic growth on
poverty and o f the consequences o f the recent increases in fuel, transport and food prices i s based
on scattered information. Recent survey information i s not yet available but i s being collected
through two initiatives. A new Household Budget Survey i s currently being carried out by NBS. In
2008, a National Panel Survey will be rolled out which would allow to assess poverty dynamics on
a year-to-year basis. Unfortunately it will be well into 2008 until any o f these surveys can start to
provide updates on the poverty situation.

21.      Update on inequaZi@. National data on poverty incidence hide significant inequalities in
Tanzania as already illustrated in the Figures 2 to 4 which show large rural-urban discrepancies.
Large variations across districts also exist with respect to welfare indicators such as poverty
incidence, primary enrollment, under five mortality, adult literacy, and access to improved water.
For instance poverty incidence varies from as low as 11% in Bukoba urban to as much as 68% in
Bunda; under five mortality varies from 40 per 1000 live births in Ngorongoro to 250 in Rwanga
and access to safe water varies from 99% inArusha (urban) to as low as 7% in Sikonge.

22.      There are some geographic concentrations o f districts with poor indicators. Districts with
the worst indicators tend to cluster in the same areas, while districts with the best indicators are
more scattered, though mostly inand around urban centers     - except for under-five mortality where
districts with relatively low mortality rates are also clustered in the North.       Districts in the
Southeast have the worst adult literacy rates, highest under five mortality rates and lowest access



                                                     8

to improved water. Districts in Kilimanjaro and Arusha (Arumeru and Arusha districts) stand out
with strong indicators. (Poverty and HumanDevelopmentReport 2005)

23.      Implications. First, robust and broad based rural income growth i s required to ensure
sustained progress on poverty reduction. The projections suggest that poverty reduction in rural
areas may be substantial, which i s also corroborated by DHS data. However, only the results o f a
new HBS to be fielded inearly 2007 will provide a clear picture o f poverty trends since 2000/01.

24.       Regional inequalities are high and the observed inequalities in poverty incidence are in
part a reflection o f differential opportunities inthe past. Many existing inequalities, and especially
those in access to services, determine opportunities for the next generation. With highinequality
in access to services, those born inthe `wrong' part of the county are exposed to a substantially
lower probability o f survival. And those who survive are more likely to end up poor as they are
more likely to be malnourished, unhealthy and uneducated.

25.       There are difficult choices to be made in addressing such regional inequalities. Cost-
effectiveness considerations suggest coverage be determined by lowest unit costs. To the degree
that unit costs o f interventions aimed at poor isolated households are higher, such a criterion
means that many people inisolated areas would be excluded. Hence a trade-off i s to be considered
between reaching a larger number o f poor people with lower unit costs, versus one where more i s
spent per capita on higher unit-cost services to reach people inmore isolatedareas.

GOVERNANCE
26.       Improved governance has been one o f the key reform objectives since 1995 and i s one o f
the three pillars o f the MKUKUTA. The governance indicators by Kaufmann et. a1(2006) (Figure
6) suggest significant improvements in all assessed areas o f governance over the past decade,
except for political stability, for reasons having to do with the situation inZanzibar.

Figure 6. Governance Indicators for Tanzania, 1996-2005

                         r    - -                 (2005)

                         0              25              I s               7 i           iie

27.       Improvements on the dimension "Voice and Accountability" are underpinned by regular
consultations on policy and legislative initiatives, increased access to information, strengthening o f
accountability institutions such as the National Audit Office, media that enjoy more freedom to
carry out investigative journalism, civil society organizations that are active in most areas o f
society and engage the government inpolicy dialogue, and an increasingly assertive Parliament.

28.       The assessment o f political stability i s likely influenced by the political situation in
Zanzibar, where the ruling and the main opposition party are in a close contest for power,
reflecting deep seated historical and ethnic differences inZanzibar's population. However, overall
Tanzania i s one o f the most politically stable countries in Africa, with term limits that have been
respected by all Presidents, and regular elections at the national and local level that are generally
considered to be fair.

29.       The improvements in government effectiveness are the results o f Tanzania's Public
Service Reform Program at the national level and the Local Government Reform Program, which
implement's government's Decentralizationby Devolution policy. Reforms o f public expenditure
management, supported by an annual public expenditure review process, play also an important
role inthe more effective use o f government resources.

30.       The indicator for the quality o f regulatory quality shows only little improvement between
1996 and 2005, despite significant efforts by government. These include the establishment o f a
new legal and institutional framework for regulatory institutions and a variety o f initiatives aimed
at simplifying regulations, ranging from a streamlining o f the tax regime to improvements in the
licensing regime.      However, implementation o f these reforms has been slow and with the
increasing role o f the private sector in the Tanzanian economy, the demands and expectations with
respect to the regulatory regime are likely to have increased. In2006, the regulators for public
utilities (EWUK4) and transport (SUMATRA) have become fully operational.

31.       The main constraint to the effective rule o f law remains weak capacity in the judicial
sector, outdated laws and poor legal infrastructure, such as business and land registries that lack
modem equipment and technology. A variety o f programs, including a Legal Sector Reform
Program, are in place to reform the legal/judicial systems.            Through the BEST program,
government i s also revamping commercialjustice. However, the process o f getting these programs
o f f the ground has been slow and only limited progress has been made in their implementation.
The Commission for Human Rights and Good Governance continues to deal with complaints o f
human rights abuses.

32.       The government-commissioned Warioba report (1996) provided a detailed analysis o f
corruption in Tanzania.      It was followed by the development o f a National Anti-Corruption
Strategy and Action Plan (NACSAP) including individual action plans for ministries, departments
and agencies (MDAs) as well as local government authorities (LGAs). The second National Anti-
corruption Strategy and Action Plan (NACSAP2) was launched in December 2006. MDAs and
LGAs have been mobilized to develop plans to address corruption in their sphere o f activities.
Integrity committees have been set up at both central and local levels to enable a wide range o f
stakeholders to report on corrupt practices and to complain about suspicious behaviors. The
implementation o f the anti-corruption action plans i s monitoredby the Head o f Public Service.

33.       Progress in the implementation o f Tanzania's multi-pronged strategy to reduce corruption
and improve governance has been variable, but led to overall improvements in the control o f
corruption as suggested by the governance indicators. Tanzania's core reforms seek to introduce


                                                   10

more transparency in public financial and human resources management. Reforms o f financial
management and procurement in the public sector are relatively advanced, limiting the scope for
leakages o f public funds. Government has increased funding for both the Preventiono f Corruption
Bureau (PCB) and the Ethics Secretariat which enabled increased presence in the regions and
districts through improved staffing and resources and enhanced capacity to investigate. PCB
continues its program o f community education and public awareness.

34.      A particular weakness o f Tanzania's anti-corruption efforts lies inthe area o fprosecution,
caused by a weak and outdated legal framework for anti-corruption measures as well as a slow and
inefficient judicial system, which itself i s not immune to corruption. A new Anti-Corruption Bill
was submitted to Parliament inFebruary 2007 and will provide an appropriate legal framework for
the fight against corruption and clarify and increase the powers o f the PCB.

35.      The authorities are making a concerted effort to establish an appropriate anti-money
laundering (AML) system, including creation o f a financial intelligence unit and new AML
legislation, passed by Parliament in November 2006. An effective AML regime i s expected to
ensure that it i s not only the underlying offence o f corruption that i s dealt with, but also that
corrupt practices can be monitored and actions taken through systems that track the proceeds of the
corrupt actions. Denying perpetrators o f corruption the financial rewards and eventual recovery of
the proceeds o f corruption i s an effective disincentive to combating corruption.

                         111.     THE GOVERNMENT'S PROGRAM

36.      The National Strategy for Growth and Reduction o f Poverty, known as the MKUKUTA
(Mkakati wa Kukuza Uchumi na Kupunguza Umaskini Tanzania) was completed in June 2005 for
implementation over the period 2005-2010. It i s the successor to Tanzania's Poverty Reduction
Strategy Paper. The MKUKUTA i s informed by Tanzania's vision 2025 and committed to the
achievement o f the Millennium Development Goals (MDGs). It has an increased focus on growth
and governance, and i s an instrument for mobilizing efforts and resources towards targeted poverty
reduction outcomes.

37.      MKUKUTA is based on the principles o f national ownership, political commitment to
democratization and human rights, maintenance o f macroeconomic and structural reforms,
building on sector strategies and cross-sectoral collaboration, building local partnerships for
citizens to engage in policy dialogue, harmonization o f aid, equity and sharing o f benefits,
sustainable development, strengthening o f macroeconomic links and decentralization, and
mainstreamingcross-cutting issues.

38.      The strategy identifies three clusters o f broad outcomes: (i)growth and reduction o f
income poverty; (ii)improvement o f quality o f life and social well being, and (iii)good
governance. Each cluster has a set o f goals and targets. To achieve each target, interventions and
actions are identified. Annex 2 provides a summary o f MKUKUTA goals and targets in each o f
the three clusters.

ClusterI:Growthand Reductionof Poverty
39.      This cluster is guided by the broad outcome o f achieving and sustaining broad based and
equitable growth. The reduction o f income poverty i s estimated to require sustained high GDP
growth o f at least 6-8 percent per annum over the next decade. To achieve this, the focus of the
MKUKUTA is on scaling up investments towards modernizing small, medium, and large scale



                                                   11

agricultural enterprises, promoting o f f farm activities including small and medium size enterprises
with emphasis on ago-processing, andpromoting more sustainableuse o fnaturalresources for the
benefit o f poor communities.       The strategy gives attention to trade, services and markets,
infrastructure, and the creation o f a conducive environment to attract private investment. Support
to the fast growing sectors, tourism and mining, will be geared towards promotion o f employment.
The existing safeguards to protect the environment from degradation will be strengthened. The
cluster also aims at contributing to the quality o f life by ensuring reliable and sustainable energy
supplies.

Cluster 11: Qualityof Life and Social Well-Being
40.      The quality of life and social well beingcluster puts emphasis on outcomes that can reduce
vulnerability, and ensure access to quality services. Improving efficiency in the delivery o f social
services i s therefore given a special focus. Attention will be paid to the promotion o f a clean and
healthy environment and the sustainable use o f natural resources. The disparities between rich and
poor, persons with disabilities, across age groups, between urban and rural citizens in access and
use o f social services will be minimized. The cluster has two broad outcomes: improved quality o f
life and social well being, with a focus on the poorest and vulnerable groups and reduced
inequalities across.regions, income, age, gender, and disability.

Cluster 111: GovernanceandAccountability
41.      Inthe governance and accountability cluster, the MKUKUTA focuses on four broad
outcomes: good governance and the rule o f law, accountability o f leaders and public servants to
the people, deepening democracy, political and social tolerance and cultivating and sustaining
peace, political stability, national unity and social cohesion. The focus on governance centers on
economic structures and use o f public resources, natural resources, personal security, tolerance and
inclusion, and participation in decision making. Human rights and a fair justice system and war
against corruption are key elements in this cluster.          The cluster aims for effective public
administration so that systems o f government are managed openly and inthe interests o f the people
they serve.

42.      The Joint Staff Advisory Note3concludes that the MKUKUTA provides a comprehensive
framework for growth and poverty reduction in Tanzania.             The consistent effort to develop
MKUKUTA implementation and monitoring through integrated national systems is a major
strength o f the program. The parallel efforts to develop results based planning systems,
harmonizationo f external financing and strengtheneddomestic accountability potentially define an
effective and mutually reinforcing approach to implementation. Key gaps exist in the planning
framework, especially the lack o f clarity on growth strategy and the consequent weakness in
coordination o f key infkastructure investments. The integration o f the planning system needs to be
expressed more clearly inthe medium term expenditure plan, and the MTEF also needs to be used
more aggressively as an instrument for achieving the ambitious harmonization goals for external
financing inthe Joint Assistance Strategy.




 IDA/SecM2006-0155




                                                   12

           IV.     BANKSUPPORT TO THE GOVERNMENT'S STRATEGY

LINKTO CAS
43.       The Bank's new CAS i s expected to be presented to the Board jointly with PRSC-5. It
consists o f the government's Joint Assistance Strategy (JAST), the multi-donor Joint Programming
Document (JPD), and a World Bank Group-specific supplement, with details on the WBG
strategy, progress under the previous CAS, and FY06-FY10programs and projects, IDA financing,
and a WBG-specific results matrix, which i s fully aligned and supportive o f MKUKUTA
objectives.

44.       The JAST clearly identifies GBS as the preferred aid modality, as it allows for full
integration o f donor support into government systems and alignment with MKUKUTA
implementation.     The JPD also recognizes the benefits o f GBS in terms o f strengthening
ownership and domestic systems for the management and implementation o f the MKUKUTA.
The CAS thus envisages an increase in budget support from about US$190 million in 2006/07 to
as much as US$400 million in 2009/10.

45.       Contributionof PRSCto achievement of MKUKUTMCASresults. PRSC contributes
to the achievement o fthe MKUKUA objectives in several ways.

46.       Funding of MKUKUTA implementation. PRSC support enhances the resource envelope
available for the implementation o f Tanzania's MKUKUTA across all three clusters o f the
MKUKUTA. The PER process serves as the forum for dialogue on resource allocation (both
domestic and PRSC and other donor resources) in support of the achievement o f MKUKUTA
objectives.

47.       Impact on Policy Reforms and Institutional Changes. PRSC financing i s underpinned by
solid processes for policy dialogue, including the annual GBS review as well as sector reviews for
most areas covered by the PRSC. The PRSC focus on policy reforms and institutional changes
also i s important for the effectiveness o f other forms o f support as well as that o f government's
own revenue.

48.       Sustainability of Results. By supporting the development o f government systems for the
implementation o f MKUKUTA rather than developing donor specific systems, PRSC support
plays an important role in developing government capacity and systems that apply equally to the
use o f domestic and GBS resources.

49.       Maintenance of macro-economic stability. As the cost o f implementing the MKUKUTA
significantly exceeds the resources available from domestic revenue, PRSC-support helps to
reduce the financing gap and reduces the pressure on government to take recourse to other sources
o f funds, such as domestic and commercial borrowing and monetary finance which could threaten
macro-economic stability.




                                                  13

  COLLABORATIONWITH THE IMFAND OTHER DONORS
  50.      The proposed credit i s provided in the context o f a common framework and joint review
  process supported by a group o f fourteen donors45,providing for consistency o f policy dialogue
  and reduction o f transaction cost. The GBS group i s currently chairedby Switzerland.

  51.      The IMF's regular assessments o f Tanzania's macro-economic performance serve as the
  main input for dialogue and assessments on policies related to macroeconomic stability. The 6*
  and last review o f the current PRGF arrangement and a successor Policy Support Instrument (PSI)
  arrangement were endorsedby the IMFBoard on February 16,2007.

  RELATIONSHIP TO OTHERBANKOPERATIONS
  52.      The current portfolio includes support for infrastructure (roads and energy), private sector
  development, agriculture, community based development, financial sector, local government, and
  public sector, and governance reform, education, health, AIDS, water, and environment.

  53.       The Bank's CAS seeks to exploit the comparative advantages and potential synergies o f
  the use o f various aid modalities, ranging from general budget support to specific investment
  credits. The CAS sets out a framework for the use of various aid modalities to maximize aid
  effectiveness.

  54.      With respect to the achievement o f Tanzania's growth objectives, investment project
  support in the areas o f roads and transport, energy, and water plays an important role in supporting
  the government's infrastructure investment program, while the PRSC supports the strengthening o f
  the legal and regulatory environment for the effectiveness o f public infrastructure investments and
  private sector participation. The PER process and analytic work such as the CEM and poverty
  assessment play an important role in providing advice on strategic priorities and appropriate
  government processes for the expansion o f infrastructure.

  55.       The Private Sector Competitiveness Project and the Privatization and Private Sector
  Development Project support institutional reforms and capacity building to help government
  realize its vision o f a private sector led economy. PRSC support i s aimed at leveraging these
  reforms and supporting complementary reform efforts in the area o f economic growth, including
  the pursuito f continuedmacro-economic stability.

  56.       Inthe social sectors, support is provided inthe context o f sector development programs in
  health and secondary education. The Bank's project support has played an important role in
  building capacity and supporting institutional reforms in these sectors which will provide the
  foundation for shifting from sector investment lending to general budget support.

  57.       The Bank also supports several environmental and natural resource management projects.
  The Forest Conservation and Management project seeks to establish institutional reforms for
  sustainable governance in the forest sector and promotes participatory forest management.
  Similarly, the Marine and Coastal Environmental Management Project seeks to establish policy


    African Development Bank, Canada, Denmark, EU, Finland, GermanyKfW, Ireland, JapdJBIC, The Netherlands,

and institutional reforms for sound governance o f offshore and nearshore fishery resources and
promotes collaborative fishery management programs and the setting up o f a marine protected
areas network. The Agriculture Sector Development Project seeks to promote sustainable
agriculture, sustainable land and water management.            The Lake Victoria Environmental
Management Project 2 seeks to promote sustainable governance and environmentalmanagement in
the Lake Victoria and its surroundings. The Water sector Support Project supports government's
2006 Water Sector Development Program. The PRSC has complemented and leveraged project
support through a focus on legal and institutionalreforms.

58.      The Bank's support to community based initiatives i s embedded in various projects such
as the Tanzania Social Action Fund, the Participatory Agriculture Development Project, the Forest
Conservation and Management Project, the Marine Environment Project, and the HIV/AIDS
project. The bottom up approach supported by these projects complements the top-down approach
inherent ingeneral budget support. However, as systems and processes for these community based
initiatives are established and if they prove successful, budget support can play an important role
insustainingandscalingupthese initiatives.

59.      Finally, the Bank also supports several public sector reform projects including the Public
Service Reform Project, the Tax Modernization Project, the Local Government Support Project,
and the Accountability, Transparency, and Integrity Project which provide technical assistance for
the implementation o f reforms supported by the PRSC.

LESSONSLEARNED
60.      As discussed inthe PRSC-4 program document, the design o f the current GBS framework
was grounded ina detailedreview o f previous general budget and adjustment support to Tanzania.
Lessons reflected in the design o f the current GBS program relate to the critical importance o f
government ownership o f the reform agenda and adequate capacity for its implementation, better
integration o f sectoral review processes into the GBS framework, and strengthening o f domestic
accountability. These lessons suggested a monitoring framework that i s less prescriptive and which
does not try to micro manage government's reform agenda. They also underline the importance o f
linking PRSC support more strongly to domestic processes as a means to support government
accountability to local stakeholders.

61.      So far, the experience with the new GBS framework adopted in October 2005 has been
largely positive, especially with respect to achieving greater coherence o f policy dialogue and
better integration o f key processes, including sector review processes, the preparation o f the Joint
Assistance Strategy for Tanzania, and the MKUKUTA monitoring.

62.      The focus on well functioning sector review processes has highlighted several issues.
These include the need to allow for flexibility in the design o f such processes, in line with the
specific institutional characteristics o f each sector. Another issue relates to achieving greater
domestic accountability through participation o f domestic stakeholders in these processes, where
principles for participation and representation have yet to be established. A joint government -
development partner working group i s looking into these issues in order to redefine the core
elements o f what comprises a "satisfactory" sector review process.        A draft definition o f a
satisfactory review process (Annex 9) clarifies that sector review processes depend on effective
processes o f M&E, sector analysis, planning and budget systems, and sector dialogue that
continues throughout the year. Public meetings between government, development partners and
other stakeholders would provide a focal point for bringing together the outcome o f these



                                                 15

underlying processes, but cannot substitute for the continual processes where absent. There has
also been a refinement to the approach to dealing with unsatisfactory review processes. Insuch a
case, key sectoral issues will be elevated above the sector dialogue for and included in the GBS
PAF as temporary process actions and will bemonitored under the GBS.

ANALYTICALUNDERPINNINGS
63.     The PRSC rests on a wide program o f analytic work carried out by government, local and
international academic and research institutes and NGOs, and various donors and the Bank. This
section highlightsfour areas o f analytic work that support the policy dialogue under the PRSC, i.e.,
(i)drivers ofgrowthandpovertyreduction, (ii)sectoral work, (iii)fiduciary issues, and(iv)
poverty and social impact assessments.

64.     Drivers of Growth and Poverty Reduction. A study on "Sustaining and Sharing Growth"
carried out by the World Bank in cooperation with the Ministry for Planning, Economy, and
Empowerment, and the third "Poverty and Human Development Report" prepared by the Research
and Analysis Working Group provide in depth analysis and recommendations related to growth
and poverty reduction in support o f the implementation o f Tanzania's MKUKUTA. The studies
underline the need to focus on rural development and raising agricultural productivity as the most
direct means to reduce poverty in Tanzania. Poor infrastructure, especially the very limited
network o f all-year passable rural roads to facilitate market access and an unreliable power supply
in urban areas, are the main constraints to faster growth. Furthermore the studies highlight the
need for improved governance o f Tanzania's natural resources, to ensure their sustainable
exploitation and that benefits are shared widely with the Tanzanian population. Another important
issue highlighted by these studies i s the need to enhance productivity, innovation, and
technological change as a key driver of structural change in Tanzania. The studies also suggest
measures to facilitate the contribution o f the poor to economic growth, including measures to
reduce vulnerability and risk. The World Bank also prepared a Diagnostic Trade Integration
Study, which provided recommendations for enhancing Tanzania's export competitiveness.
Related work, that takes a closer look at the real exchange rate as a key determinant o f
international competitiveness i s ongoing. A study on rural-urban linkages and the role o f the
informal sector contributes to the debate on how the benefits o f economic growth can be more
widely shared in Tanzania. Work on the tourism sector and sub-national growth strategies i s
ongoing. Investment Climate Assessments have been carried out in 2003 and 2006/07,
complemented by a rural investment climate assessment with a focus on micro and small
enterprises. In support o f the proposed scaling up o f infrastructure, the Bank provided a guide for
the planning and implementation o f public private partnerships in Tanzania. A country specific
"Doing Business in 2007" report for Tanzania helps to set priorities for the implementation o f
Tanzania's BEST program.

65.     Sectoral Work.      Analytic work at the sector level forms an integral part o f sector
development programs and thematic reform programs.            Priority issues for analytic work are
typically discussed during annual sector reviews. Inthe health sector, a recent focus o f analytic
work has been human resources inhealth, health sector financing, and explaining the rapid decline
in under-five mortality. Inthe education sector, analytic work is now focusing increasingly on
secondary, TVET, and higher education as well as on education in Zanzibar, complementing the
regularly prepared education sector status reports. A Water Sector Assistance Strategy prepared in
2006 reviews the current status o f the water sector and identifies strategies for enhancing the
contribution o f the water sector to the achievement o f MKUKUTA objectives and the MDGs.
Detailed analysis o f the agriculture sector i s contained in an agriculture sector review report as



                                                   16

well as in the Country Economic Memorandum, both completed in 2006. Specific studies on the
strategic grain reserve and agricultural input subsidies as well as an agriculture sector public
expenditure review complement analytic work in the agriculture sector. Work on the Transport
Sector and Power Sector Investment Plans provides the basis for a scaling up o f expenditure o f
expenditure in infrastructure.   This is complemented by detailed analytic work that examines
requirements and options for the financing o f Tanzania's large infrastructure needs, including
through public-private partnerships.

66.     Fiduciary Work. In the context o f Tanzania's government led, participatory Public
Expenditure Review process, a wide variety o f analytical activities are carried out every year. This
includes the Bank-led preparation o f the annual Public Expenditure and Financial Accountability
Report (PEFAR), the findings o f which were presented during the PER Consultative Meeting in
M a y 2006. The PEFAR combines analytic work that was previously carried out in the context o f
the annual PER external evaluation and the Country Fiduciary Accountability Assessment. The
FY06 PEFARalso included a fiduciary assessment o f local governments and an update o fthe 2003
CPAR with a detailed review o f the procurement reforms undertaken to date. Technical assistance
for the implementation o f the recommendations o f the PEFAR and the CPAR i s being provided
through a Public Finance Management Reform Program, while policy dialogue takes place in the
context o f the PEFAR and GBS program. The dialogue on governance and anti-corruption i s
underpinnedby a series o f studies on the political economy o f reform, patterns o f accountability,
and the fight against corruption inTanzania.

67.     Poverty and Social Impact Assessments (PSIAs).              The Bank jointly with other
development partners has supported several PSIAs that were instrumental in designing and
reaching consensus on reforms. This includes the analysis o f reforms o f crop boards, business
licensing, and local government revenue reforms. In addition, Tanzania's reform efforts also can
draw on PSIAs carried out by local and international academic and research institutes. The Bank
has recently initiated a program that will support the integration o f PSIA analysis into existing
government planning processes or joint government-DP sector wide processes in the areas o f
macro-economic impacts, agriculture and water sectors.




                                                17

   V.       THE PROPOSEDFIFTHPOVERTY REDUCTIONSUPPORT CREDIT
                                                      (PRSC-5)

OPERATIONDESCRIPTION
68.      The proposed poverty reduction support credit (PRSC-5) i s the second in a series o f five
annual operations supporting the implementation o f Tanzania's MKUJSUTAa6 The GBS
framework includes a Partnership Framework Memorandum (PFM), a Performance Assessment
Framework (PAF), as well as an agreed Joint Review Process.

69.      The P F M (Annex 3) spells out the objectives and underlying principles for the provision
o f general budget support to Tanzania as well as the responsibilities o f government and
development partners. It defines the GBS facility as a five-year program to support the
implementation o f Tanzania's MKUKUTA. The principles set out in the P F M seek to
operationalize various international commitments, including the Monterrey Consensus on
Financing for Development (2002), the Rome Declaration on Aid Harmonization (2003), the
Marrakech Memorandum on Managing for Results (2004), the Paris Declaration on Aid
Effectiveness (2005) and the Tanzania Joint Assistance Strategy (JAST) aiming at providing more
scope for government ownership, better alignment and integration o f donor support with
government priorities and systems, and more predictability o f donor support.


                             Box 1. Good PracticePrinciplesonConditionality

Principle 1:Reinforce Ownership

The program supported by the Bank's PRSC builds on the 2005-2010 MKUKUTA that was prepared in a
participatory process by government. The Bank has actively supported and encouraged the government in this
process. Moreover, analytic work undertaken by the Bank incollaboration with government and other donors and
domestic stakeholders has informed the process o f redesigning the government's framework for budget support,
including work on the political economy o f reforms, growth, and fiduciary issues.

Principle 2: Agree upfront with thegovernment and otherfinancial partners on a coordinated accountability
framework

The performance assessment framework (PAF) for general budget support has been developed under government
leadership. Under a memorandum o f understanding signed by all budget support donors and the government, it
constitutes the single and coherent framework for evaluating progress, setting out results indicators, selected
policy actions, and review processes.

Principle3: Customizethe accountabilityframework and modalities of Bank supportto country circumstances

The PAF and the underlying MKUKUTA represent country circumstances and preferences. Government
leadership in setting up the review process for the PAF also resulted in a review cycle that reflects the country's
own cycles - such as sector reviews, public expenditure reviews and budget preparation cycles - and timing o f
financing needs. Detail and size o f the PAF are a representation of the country's circumstances in terms o f
capacity and maturity o f dialogue, including inthe sectors.




  Support to Zanzibar's Strategy for Growth and Reduction o f Poverty is indirect. As part o f a revenue
sharing agreement between the Union Government of Tanzania and the Revolutionary Government o f
Zanzibar, Zanzibar receives 4.5 percent o f general budget support provided to Tanzania.


                                                      18

Principle4: Chooseonb actions criticalfor achieving results as conditionsfor disbursement                          I
The new PAF focuses o n a few critical actions, outcomes o f sectoral reviews, and outcome indicators to measure
progress under the program. It thus avoids the inclusion o f a host o f actions as benchmarks that was perceived as
burdensome by government and donors and made the previous PAF heavily focused o n inputs. The Bank relies on
the PAF to identify a few actions and sector reviews as critical for measuring progress. The inclusion o f outcome
indicators inthe PAF and their link with the MKUKUTA outcome indicators and monitoring framework ensures a
close monitoring o f results under the revised PAF.

Principle 5: Conduct transparentprogress reviews conducive topredictable and performance-based financial
support

The PAF review cycle is closely aligned with the budget cycle. The annual review in October allows donors to
make fmfinancing decisions for the following fiscal year (beginning inJuly) before budget allocations are set. It
thus allows for a high level o f short-term predictability. Harmonizedreviews also reduce the transaction costs for
the government (i.e., by reducing the number o f separate missions). The Bank is filly respecting the review
calendar. Based on the Bank's evaluation o f progress under the PAF, financing decisions are made based on
performance and announced sufficiently early to avoid disruptions inbudget implementation. The Annual Review
was carried out inOctober 2006 and, inline withthe partnership framework, the Bank informed government o f the
performance-related reduction in the amount o f PRSC-5 in January 2007, well in time for government to reflect
the revised amount o f PRSC-5 inthe budget preparation process for the following fiscal year.



70.      The PAF (Annex 3) covers six focus areas. These are the three clusters o f the MKUKUTA
(i.e., growth and the reduction o f income poverty, improvements o f quality o f life and social well
being, and governance and accountability), macroeconomic stability, resource allocation and
budget consistency, and public financial management. It supports the outcome orientation o f the
MKUKUTA and tries to achieve greater domestic accountability and coherence o fpolicy dialogue
through the reliance on domestic processes. It focuses primarily on a small number o f indicators
that allow an assessment o f overall progress and the definition o f local processes, that provide the
forum for in depth policy dialogue on which the GBS facility can draw.                   In addition to the
reference to sectoral and thematic review processes and results indicators, the PAF also includes a
small number o f specific actions to monitor progress in areas where no sufficiently mature
processes for regular dialogue exist as yet.

71.      The GBS review process consists o f one joint GBS Annual Review typically held in
October, in alignment with Tanzania's budget process. Figure 7 shows the integration o f the GBS
Annual Review with other review processes and the budget preparation process. Sectoral and
thematic reviews precede the GBS Annual Review. Once a credible MKUKUTA Cluster review
process has been established, it i s expected that the individual reviews will feed into it, which in
turnwill feed into the GBS Annual Review. However, for the time being, the sector and thematic
reviews feed directly into the GBS Annual Review, which provides two inputs to the budget
guidelines process.        First, it provides feedback from GBS donors on strategic issues for
consideration in the budget guidelines process. Second, it provides a firm commitment o f the
amount o f GBS for the next fiscal year to allow government to prepare its budget within a
relatively stable and firm external financing framework.




                                                        19

72.     The proposed operation would support the government to pursue its policy objectives laid
out in the MKUKUTA by providing financing for the government's FY08 budget. It would also
provide the incentives to implementkey economic, social and institutional reformto strengthenthe
overall performance o f the economy and contribute to poverty alleviation. Over the period 2006-
2010, it is expected that the reform program under the series of five annual PRSCs (PRSC-4 to
PRSC-8) would help the government sustain high rates of economic growth in the range of 6 to 8
percentannually, which are projected to translate into a significant reduction o f poverty incidence.
It would also help the government in making progress toward reaching the MDGs, namely,
increase school enrollment and literacy rates, reduce child and maternal mortality, increase access
to safe water, and strengthen the sustainability of development efforts.

which are recognized inthe MKUKUTA as critical areas for its successful implementation. Table
