data and to set up appropriate data collection processes in those areas where data are not yet
available.
DESCRIPTIONOFPOLICY AREAS
76.       This section provides an assessment o f recent developments inthe policy areas supported
by the PRSC, focusing particularly on the discussion o f prior actions for PRSC-5 and triggers for
PRSC-6, which are critical to the achievement o f Tanzania's MKUKUTA objectives. (The
program document for PRSC-4 provided a summary o f the government strategy in each o f these
areas).




                                                           24

MKUKUTAImplementation:Custer 1-GrowthandReductionofPoverty

77.      Agriculture Sector. Growth o f agriculture GDP dropped from 5.8 percent in2004 to 5.2
percent in 2005 and further to a projected 3.7 percent in 2007 on account o f a severe drought that
began in October 2005. The government has completed the formulation o f an Agricultural Sector
Development Programme, which was officially launched in July 2006. Key areas o f reform to
enhance productivity and incomes in the agricultural sector include (a) improved market access,
especially through strengthened rural infrastructure; (b) reform o f sector governance and
marketing; (c) scaling up o f irrigation; (d) scaled up research and development; and (e) improved
public service delivery, including reforms o f the extension system, systems for input subsidies, and
the strategic grain reserve.
78.      The Bank i s providing complementary support to agriculture through an Agriculture
Sector Development Project and the PRSC. PRSC support has focussed on the reform o f the crop
boards, to improve producer prices and incentives. Following the adoption o f a strategy for the
reform o f crop boards based on the recommendations o f a poverty and social impact assessment,
implementation o f the reforms commenced after the government issued the Ministerial Circular on
the Reformo f Crop Boards on 10thMarch 2006. The Circular abolished the crop levyhess levied
by the respective Crop Boards on crops for exports, limited the role o f Crop Boards primarily to
regulatory functions, initiated the reforms o f the structures, roles and functions o f crop boards, and
made the establishment o f Crop Development Funds subject to stakeholder agreement.
Government decided to amend legislation for all crop boards simultaneously, instead o f submitting
amendments for individual crop boards sequentially. Inaddition, government decided to carry out
a more consultative process which requires significantly more time than the originally envisaged
focus on only two crop boards.                Subsequently, government initiated broad stakeholder
consultations on proposed measures for financing functions shared with the private sector
(including extension, research, training, and procurement o f inputs) and measures to increase
farmers' incomes. For four crop boards (coffee, cotton, sugar, and tobacco) these consultation
have been concluded and resulted in the signing o f Memoranda o f Understanding on transitional
and long term measures for financing shared functions. Stakeholder consultations for cashews,
tea, and pyrethrum have taken place but have not yet resulted in an agreement. In the coffee
sector, following these consultations the single licence requirement and blind auction requirement
inthe marketingofcoffeewere relaxedandlicensingfees abolished.
79.       The envisaged timetable for preparing and submitting amendments to crop board
legislation to Parliament includes the completion o f stakeholder consultations on the scope and
substance o f the legal amendments with all crop boards, the preparation o f draft legislation by
legal consultants, and the submission o f the draft legislation for another round o f stakeholder
consultations. It i s now expected that these amendments will be submitted to government at the
earliest by October/November 2007 but not later than February 2008.

PRSC-5 Prior Action: Consultations on implementation o f reforms with four crop Boards completed and
MOUs signed.

PRSC6 Trigger: Draft amendments to crop board legislation submitted for stakeholder consultations and
strategic plans for all crop boards finalized.

80.      Business Environment. Business in Tanzania is handicapped by severe weaknesses in
the regulatory environment. The business licensing regime, employment regulations, and
registration o f property are particularly problematic areas. The government aims to improve the
business environment through several reform programs, including the Business Environment



                                                    25

Strengthening Program (BEST), a Tax Modernization Program (TMP), and the Second Generation
Financial Sector Reform Program. The impact o f these reforms i s reflected in an improved
ranhng o f the ease o f doing business in Tanzania from 150 to142 among 175 countries. "Doing
Business in 2007" assesses Tanzania as being among the top ten reformers. Recent reforms
include simplification o f the business licensing regime; reduction in the cost o f registering
property; revision o f the Companies Act to give investors greater protection; and reform o f
customs administration through the introduction o f risk management techniques, electronic data
interchange systems, and border cooperation agreements.
81.      A particular focus o f the Bank's support have been reforms o fthe businessregistrationand
licensing regime, since the number o f procedures (23), the number o f days required (3 13) and the
cost involved (3796.6 percent o f income per capita) are among the highest worldwide and
Tanzania ranks 172ndamong 175 countries on this indicator. Initial reforms included the abolition
o f business license fees for small businesses and the removal o f annual licensing requirements. A
report on regulatory licensing outlines options for the reform o f the licensingregime.
82.       Government also introduced new legislation in the form o f a Business Activities
Registration Bill, the first reading o f which took place in July, 2005. Subsequent stakeholder
discussions on reforms o f the business licensing system and the functions currently under the
Business Registration and Licensing Authority (BRELA), including the company's and business
names registries, have led to strong consensus for the establishment o f a One-stop shop business
registration centre under the mandate o f BRELA. The foregoing measures will become part o f a
one-stop business registration concept to be developed as a package comprising o f the following:

          Enact legislation on business registration and implement the same through a
          comprehensive holistic and programmatic approach under the management o f BRELA
          with interface to other nationalregistries includingTRA, Regulatory licensing etc.

     0    Development and adoption o f a national policy on business licensing; and

     0    Drafting legislation on a coherent and comprehensive Regulatory LicensingRegime.
83.       The Business Activities Registration Bill was revised and passed by Parliament in
February 2007, reflecting the outcomes o f the stakeholder consultations and private sector views.

PRSC-5 Prior Action: Progress in the reform of the business activities registration and the business
regulatory licensing regime, including reflecting private sector views in the redrafting o f the business
activities registration bill.
                                                                                            ~~    ~




84.       Energy Sector. Tanzania's energy sector has previously relied heavily on hydro power,
demonstrated by the fact that 60% o f installed generation capacity i s still from hydro sources,
while 31% i s from independent thermal power producers (Songas and IPTL). A prolonged
drought, conflicting uses o f water, poor management o f catchment areas, and overuse o f the
hydropower system resulted in a severe energy crisis which has forced Tanzania's government
owned power company TANESCO to rationpower for most o f 2006. Drought and water shortages
reduced the available hydropower generation capacity from 561 MW to about 300 MW duringthis
time. In addition, the quality o f power supply has deteriorated and system losses increased from
23% in2003 to 33% in2005 due to lack o f investments duringthe past decade.
85.       The sector has been impactedby three severe emergencies over the last year regarding: a)
generation shortages b) management issues and c) cash flow shortfalls.               These introduced
tremendous uncertainty regarding the future prospects o f the sector. With generation capacity
restored and increased, overall sector performance is improving; however, some critical actions



                                                   26

need to be taken by the government and TANESCO, such as filing for continued tariff increases,
over the next few months to completely resolve these issues, notably regarding TANESCO's
financial health.
86.     Generation. TANESCO has entered into 2-yr lease contracts (financed by the MoF from
MDRI funds) to acquire about 180MW of emergency thermal power generation capacity. Even
though the cost o f such generation capacity has been reasonable, the procurement process could
have been better for the selection o f the service providers. The government has yet to set out a
clear and effective strategy for confronting such situations in the future. At present, due to short-
rains, hydro capacity was fully restored in late 2006, and generation shortfalls have been avoided.
With the addition o f the leased capacity and permanent plants coming online over the next several
months, it i s expected that TANESCO will have adequate reserve margins for at least the next 2-3
years and no load shedding i s expected over the mediumterm.
87.     Management. TANESCO's Board and the Ministry o f Energy and Minerals decisided in
2006 not to renew the management contract for TANESCO because o f concerns with operational
effectiveness. A new Managing DirectorKEO was appointed in December 2006. However, the
CEO's effectiveness has been limited by the fact that a full management team i s not in place
following the departure o f the management contractors. The Bank i s also concerned about the lack
o f autonomy and commercial focus in TANESCO operations. The Bank through a SIDA trust
fund is assisting TANESCO with financing o f technical and financial consultants, as an interim
measure until the right skills are recruited. It i s expected that such skills will be recruited as soon
as possible but not later than September 2007.          Government intends to allow TANESCO to
function as an autonomous and commercial entity, and therefore be able to engage in forward
planning in order to avoid a repetition o f the crises that have been experienced at various points
over the last decade. The appointment o f a professional management team (financial, investment,
O&M) and ensuring increased autonomy o f TANESCO are critical in turning TANESCO into a
commercially viable entity and implementing a large capital investment program.
88.     Financial. TANESCO has been facing operational cash flow shortfalls for some time
now. In early 2007, a tariff increase o f 6% was approved by the independent sector regulator.
TANESCO continues to face cash flow shortfalls as current tariffs are significantly below
operating costs. Operating costs are high due to inefficiencies in the sector (technical losses are
high), the proportional decline inlow-cost hydro generation, and relatively highcapacity charges
o f IPPs. With improved hydro availability since late 2006, TANESCO's heavy fuel oil expense
has substantially reduced therefore improving its financial condition. TANESCO has prepared a
five year financial recovery plan (FRP) o f which key elements include (i)     increasing tariffs to long
run marginal cost of supply levels; (ii)   securing a commercial loan o f up to TSh 300 billion to
reduce the need for subsidies from the budget for operational cash shortfalls; (iii)       reducing the
capacity charges being incurred by buying back the IPTL plant and refinancing private equity in
the Songas 5 & 6 Units; and (iv) optimizing the mix o f generation between hydro and thermal
plants.  These underlying principles o f the FRP were approved by the Cabinet in February 2007.
While many o f the challenges are clear, leadership in addressing TANESCO's immediate and long
term financial challenges i s required.
89.     Longer Term Sector Vision. The government has prepared a new Power Sector Reform
Strategy, a revised Electricity Act, and has commissioned a new long term least cost Power Sector
Development Plan. Inorder to ensure an energy supply that i s consistent with the energy demands
o f a growing economy, TANESCO needs capital investment o f TSh 1.5 trillion duringthe next 5
years largely for rehabilitation and expansion o f the transmission and distribution systems,
acquiring new business and IT systems, increasing access to electricity, and capacity building.
Generation investments are likely to be financed by the private sector. Interconnections o f the


                                                   27

 Tanzanian grid with the grid o f EAC and SADC countries i s also important in order to be able to
 smooth fluctuations in supply and demand through exports and imports o f energy. However, these
 investments require the above mentioned issues to be successfully addressed in order to provide
 the necessary sound foundation for the development o f the sector.
 90.      The Electricity and Water Utilities Regulatory Authority (EWURA) commenced
 operations inJune 2006 and i s now fully functional. It has already dealt with a water tariff request
 by DAWASA and a request by TANESCO for a tariff increase o f 6% effective February 2007.
 EWURAplans to establishregulatory rules that would encourage competitive procurement o fnew
 generation projects. Inthis context, EWURAi s working with the Bank on a two-phased approach,
 first focused on the creation o f standardized application and tables that would allow the
 benchmarking o f proposed Power Purchase Agreements (PPAs) and, secondly, the creation o f
 guidelines for competitive procurement and model PPAs. EWURA i s also planning to develop a
 monitoring system for service delivery and financial performance o f TANESCO. For the water
 sector, such a monitoring system i s already inplace.

 PRSC-6 trigger: Progress inthe implementation ofthe TANESCO financial recoveryplan and strengthening
 governance o f the sector by improved regulation

 91.      Transport Sector.       Improving Tanzania's transport network requires a strengthened
 institutional and policy framework for the transport sector and a clear prioritization o f investment
 and maintenance spending. The sector continues to suffer from insufficient funding for road
 maintenance and rehabilitation. While the MKUKUTA target was to rehabilitate 3000 kmo f rural
 roads in2005/06, only 959 lanwere rehabilitated,
 92.      Inlate 2005, the Ministry of Works and the Ministryo f Communications and Transport
 were merged into a Ministry o f Infrastructure Development (MID), laying the foundation for a
 more effective development o f Tanzania's transport sector. A new Roads Bill was expected to be
,submitted to Parliament by November 2006, designating clear responsibilities for the development
 and management o f Tanzania's road network. A draft Road Bill has been prepared. However, the
 road inventory (which will be one o f the schedules o f the Bill) was only completed in October
 2006 and the submission o f the Road Bill to Parliament i s now expected to occur by April 2007.
 Submission o fthe new Roads Bill to Parliament was approved by Cabinet inFebruary 2007.
 93.      A draft Transport Sector Investment Plan (TSIP) was prepared in2006. However, a better
 coverage o f maintenance expenditures and a better prioritized and more realistic and less
 ambitious scale o f planned investments, including a stronger framework for private sector
 participation in infrastructure, are required as a basis for more effective spending in the transport
 sector. A road sector stakeholder meeting held on February 20,2007 provided important inputsto
 the way forward in refining the TSIP. Inparticular, implementation o f the TSIP starting with the
 budget for 2007/08 will require a detailed three year rolling plan 2007/08 to 2009/10 as part o f the
 TSIP by April 2007. Related to road maintenance, the Minister for Infrastructure Development
 indicated that the 3-year rolling road maintenance program for the MTEF 2007/08 i s estimated at
 US$370 million, giving an average expenditure o f US$ 120 million per financial year in the
 medium term. An increase in the fuel levy from 7 to 10 US$ cents per litre and an increase in
 annual access fees would increase the Road Fund level from currently about US$80 million by
 about US$60 million.
 94.      In order to build on recent sector reforms and with a view to establishing a satisfactory
 review process, the government will need to collaborate with GBS partners to strengthen the
 consultative processes such as regular meetings o f the Transport Sector Joint Technical
 Committee, workshops, and high-level Consultative Forums.


                                                  28

PRSC-5 Prior Action: Preparationof a draft RoadBill, including a road inventory and Cabinet approval of
the submissionof a newRoadBillto Parliament
PRSC-6 triggers: (i)Roads Billsubmittedto Parliament(previously trigger for PRSC-5)
                 (ii)Transport SectorInvestmentPlanfinalized with adequate provision for maintenance
                     anda framework for PPP


MKUKUTAImplementation:Custer2 -QualityofLifeand SocialWellBeing

95*       The PRSC focus areas under Cluster 2, i.e., education, health, and water are covered by
sector development programs. Satisfactory sector reviews for all three sectors were held in 2006.
Below i s a summary o f the main developments inthese sectors.


PRSC-5 Prior Actions: SatisfactoryHealth, Education, andWater Sector ReviewsCarried Out (in2006)
PRSC-6 Triggers: Satisfactory Health, Education, andWater Sector ReviewsCarried Out (in2007)

96.     Education. The introduction o f the Primary Education Development Program (PEDP) in
July 2001 and the abolition o f primary education school fees have resulted in a continuous
increase in the gross enrolment ratio (GER) which reached 112.7 in 2006, with no significant
gender differences in enrolment. The primary completion rate i s 58 percent for the seven year
primary cycle, which compares favourably with those o f other countries with shorter primary
cycles. The average drop out rate i s 3.4 % for standard one to seven and 4.9% for standard six to
seven. Tanzania included early childhood development (pre-primary) in its implementation o f the
Education for All program. The net enrolment ratio (NER) in pre-school was 27.7% in 2005 and
28.5% in2006.

97.     The country has undertaken a very successful decentralization o f service delivery for
primary education, with local authorities now properly resourced to deliver services, and local
communities are engaged through appropriate and effective governance structures at the school
level. In addition, the country has recruited more teachers. Teaching staff for primary education
rose from 112,860 in2002 to 151,882 in2006. The teacher pupilratio inprimary school improved
from 1:56 in 2005 to 1:52 in 2006 and the pupil-teacher textbook ratio showed improvements as
well.  As a consequence, performance in learning measured through Primary School Leaving
Examinations has also improved from 28.6% in 2001 to 61.8 % in 2006. The country ranks fourth
among the 14 African countries that participated in the 2000 SACMEQ student assessment.
Nonetheless, concerns about the quality o f primary education remain, especially in remote areas
where it i s difficult to retain qualified teachers.     The government i s focusing on further
improvement o f the quality o f education through continued professional development o f teachers.
In order to improve teacher deployment, the government continues to construct houses within
primary and secondary school compounds for teacher accommodation.

98.     An ambitious program to expand and improve the quality o f secondary education is being
implemented since January 2005.       The program has helped to reduce the cost o f secondary
education to households by reducing school fees per pupil from TSh 40, 000 to TSh 20,000 in day
secondary schools. The GER for the four-year lower secondary cycle rose from about 6% in 1990
to about 15.9% in 2005, reflecting steady progress in broadening access to secondary education.
The GER for upper level secondary education was 2.4 percent. The GER in lower secondary
education (Form 1-4) rose from 12.9 % in 2004 to 20.2% in 2006. The NER rose from 8.4% to



                                                  29

13.4% inthe same period. A lot needs to be done to improve the quality o f secondary education,
especially at lower secondary education. For a number o f years, performance o f form four students
based on the form four examination results has been poor with about 50 percent o f students
achieving only Division IV, For example in 2005 students achievement was as follows: Division I
-5.2 %; DivisionII-6.5%; DivisionIII21.9%; DivisionIV 55.7%. Entryto higher education
requires students with division I-II.

99.     Other levels o f training such as vocational and technical education would also need to be
strengthened to meet the current demand for skills development for economic growth. Only about
9,946 students (4,123 females) completed training inpublic and private Vocational Education and
Training institutions in2006.

100.    Enrolment in higher education institutions has grown rapidly in recent years, from about
24,000 in 2001/02, to about 55,000 in 2005/06. The rapid growth o f enrolments has stressed the
higher education subsector.     According to the Ministry o f Higher Education, Science, and
Technology (MHEST), qualified teaching staff, laboratory and ICT equipment, and library
materials are scarce in relation to needs. A program i s under preparation to address the needs o f
the subsector. Disbursements o f zero-interest student loans from the Student Loans Board have
been increasing rapidly, with relatively little targeting. In view o f the heavy demand for these
zero-interest loans, the Board has supplemented the government budget funds it receives through
MHEST with government-guaranteed borrowings from financial institutions.                The task o f
reconciling enrolment expansion, improved quality and relevance, and the stable share o f the
government budget devoted to higher education foreseen in the MTEF remains to be
accomplished.

101.    The sector review held in February 2006 identified a number o f additional issues that
require attention. Strengthening sub-sector linkages and coordination continues to be a challenge.
There i s also a need to strengthen links with ongoing core reforms (PFMRP, LGRP, PSRP). An
adequate analytical assessment o f public expenditures in the sector i s laclung. Developments
partners and government have been discussing Terms o f Reference for a study on costs and
financing o fthe education sector for the next ten years.

102.    Health. The government's health sector development programme aims to improve the
accessibility, delivery and quality o f health care services available in the country, underscored by
far-reaching reforms at all levels. Over the last five-years there have been some remarkable gains
inhealth: infant and under-fivemortality has declinedand childhoodmalnutrition has also showed
improvement although it still remains an underlying factor o f almost 50 percent o f under-five
mortality. At the same time there has been significant progress inpreventing and treating malaria
with a large increase inthe proportion o fchildren under-five that sleep under an insecticide treated
net. Unfortunately, there has been much less progress in the area o f reproductive health with the
rate o f maternal mortality stagnating. However, an encouraging effort underway i s the
development o f a Maternal and Newborn Road Map which, with adequate support will help to
address the challenges. Finally, so-called "neglected tropical diseases" such as Trachoma,
Onchocerciasis and Lymphatic Filariasis require increased attention.

103.    The combination o f improved public expenditure, targeted exemptions (for pregnant
women, children under five and many illnesses), and mandatory insurance for the formally
employed population (under the National Health Insurance Fund and the National Social Security
Fund) provide some protection against the financial burden o f illness. The government is working
with partners to expand protection for catastrophic care for the unemployed poor. The share o f


                                                  30

public expenditure on health in FY05/06 was $lohapita, almost 12% o f public expenditure.
Combined with external financing and insurance, it allows an appropriate package o f preventive
and some curative services.

104.    The sector has experienced a substantial decline in availability o f human resources over
the last decade, to the point that Tanzania has one o f the lowest per capita medical practitioner
ratios in the developing world, which has impacted the quality o f health care services. Lack o f
skilled staff i s further complicated by inadequate distribution and lack o f flexibility in
decentralised human resource management. At the same time, the financing o f health care services
remains a significant challenge with competing priorities, increasing demands, new technologies
and growing drug resistance. Important cross-cutting issues, such as the inclusion o f a disability
component inhealthprogrammes, do not receive the attention they merit.

105.    The Health Sector Strategic Plan forms the framework for GOT investments in health and
development assistance to the sector. The health sector review process i s well established and has
improved continually since the first review in 1999. It reviews the implementation o f the Health
Sector Strategic Plan and makes commitments on issues for the coming year. Within the existing
health sector review, 22 performance indicators are assessedevery year, with a further 11reviewed
at longer intervals. The review held in April 2006 was satisfactory, but highlighted the following
issues and challenges:

     0  As the foundation o f the review process, development partners and the MOHSW need to
        work more closely on coordinating M&E activities, tracking the indicators, and analyzing
        and summarizinginformation so that it can better inform decision making;

     0  It is important to maintain and strengthen the SWAP fora for coordinating dialogue and
        communicationbetween government and development partners around sector issues;

     0  There i s scope for more effectively engaging civil society in the monitoring o f the sector
        and in the design o f strategies. A meeting for NGOs i s proposed as part o f the review
        process.
106.    HIV/AIDS (National Multisectoral Framework, NMSF). Progress inthe fight against
HIV/AIDS includes increased public awareness, high-level commitment, the establishment o f
community mulitsectoral AIDS committees in all districts, regional facilitating agents charged
with expanding the contributions o f civil society organizations and supporting local government
capacity, funding for about 1050 civil society organizations for HIV/AIDS related activities,
effective mobilization o f private sector, mainstreaming o f HIV in the national budget, successful
(external) fundraising, and the enrolment o f 103,036 patients in the national care and treatment
program, o f which 49,3 15 have started anti-retroviral treatment.          Nonetheless, important
challenges remain in order to further increase the effectiveness o f Tanzania's HIV/AIDS program.
These include enhanced mainstreaming o f H N / A I D S activities in the public and the education
sector; establishment o f a functioning M&E system, and scaled up enrolment in anti-retroviral
therapy.

107.    A Joint HIV/AIDS Programme Review is held biannually to take stock o f progress made
in implementing the NMSF by all sectors involved including TACAIDS,                     and make
recommendations for future actions. The second biannual review took place from March 15 to 16,
2006.




                                                 31

108.    Water sector. Access to clean and safe water continued to expand, reaching 54.7 percent
inrural and 74 percent inurban areas inJune 2006. Access to sewerage services was maintained
at 17 percent, but services are only in parts o f urban areas. There has also been progress in
developing the sector wide approach (SWAP) and the establishment o f a Joint Sector Oversight
Arrangement.

109.    The first joint water sector review took place in Dar es Salaam on September 19-20,2006,
with the participation o f about 180 stakeholders. The sector review concluded that reforms inthe
sector are on track and that performance i s improving.        It highlighted the need to improve
coordination to include allied ministries such as PMO-RALG and the Ministry o f Health, as well
as civil society and the private sector. Steps are needed to accelerate capacity building in local
authorities and for private sector suppliers.

MKUKUTA Implementation:Custer 3 GovernanceandAccountability
                                              -
110.    As discussed above, Tanzania continues to make good progress in strengthening
governance and accountability. Tanzania's National Framework on Good Governance provides
the overall framework for reforms in this area.        The implementation o f public expenditure,
financial management, and procurement reforms as well as the Public Sector Reform Programme
and a Local Government Reform Program have made important contributions towards improving
the governance environment. The launch o f the legal and judicial reforms in 2005 has further
broadened the reforms underway.

111.     The Second National Anti-Corruption Strategy and Action Plan (NACSAP 11) was
launched in December 2006. The NACSAP 11proposes to establish a permanent forum with
representation from government, civil society, private sector and DPs to coordinate and review
implementation o f the program.       Government has also established integrity committees in
Ministries, Departments and Agencies and Local Government Authorities to monitor
implementation o f individual anti-corruptionaction plans.

112.     Governmentenvisagesto undertake a governance and corruption survey to provide a
baseline for assessingprogress. To date, Tanzania's anti-corruption undertakings have not been
base-lined or formulated on the basis o f empirical diagnostic surveys which generate data that can
provide the basis for analysis, help determine policy and action priorities and inform the design of
reform processes and more generally the discussion about corruption issues. By undertaking the
governance and corruption survey and updating it in subsequent years to provide inputs to the
annual consultative process on the implementation o f the NACSAP 11, a robust mechanism for
review o fprogress under the this sub-component will be established.

113.     The current institutional (legal) framework constrains the legal-judicial-penal
organizations dealing with corruption effectively to investigate, prosecute, sentence and
punish corrupt practice. The same organizations themselves lack the technical and financial
capacity to carry out their mandates effectively. The system currently inplace for public officials
to declare their assets i s weakened by the fact that it does not provide for verification via public
scrutiny. Revised anti-corruption legislation has been submitted to Parliament in February 2007
and will remove some o f these constraints.




                                                 32

                 ~~




PRSC-6 Trigger: Submission ofAnti-corruption Act to Parliament

114.      Implementation of the Legal Sector Reform Program (LSRP) has been slow, largely
due to delayed recruitment of the relevant staff to coordinate the program. The capacity o f
the Ministry o f Justice and Constitutional Affairs to implement the program i s recognizably weak
and needs substantial technical assistance to achieve progress. The LSRP could benefit from being
fully integrated into the government's comprehensive reform agenda through the Inter-Ministerial
Technical Committee and close coordination with other core reform programs. The
implementation o f LSRP could benefit from enhanced collaboration and support from senior
officials with longer experience o f implementing core reforms.                 To enhance domestic
accountability, the involvement o f a wide range o f domestic stakeholders (Parliament, NGOs,
CSOs, professional associations, etc.) in early stages o f implementation o f the LSRP would be
beneficial.

115.      The implementation of Decentralization by Devolution (D-by-D) is still at an early
stage. Decentralizationby Devolution      - based on the "Policy Paper on Local Government Reform"
from 1998 - i s the government's policy to give more powers and responsibilities to Local
Government Authorities (LGAs). Only in June 2006 was a road map developed which addresses
mainly attitudinal, institutional and legal aspects. The push for the actual implementation o f the
decentralization process by central government i s still insufficient to assure the application o f the
principles o f D-by-D across government.             Systems are relatively well developed, but
implementation lags behind. Some examples are:

    -     Sectors are not using the fiscal transfer systems developed for decentralization.
    -
    --    The budget allocations do not reflect the decentralization policy.
          Legal reforms in different sectors to allow D-by-Dimplementation are pending.
          Amendment o f the Public Service Regulations to enhance HR autonomy i s pending.

116.      The right to plan and manage their own human resources i s a key factor to increase LGAs
accountability to residents and improve the sense o f common responsibility o f councillors and
staff. The Public Service Act was amended in 2004, but the respective regulations are yet to be
amended.      Linked to human resource autonomy i s the issue o f how peripheral or otherwise
disadvantaged councils can attract and retain qualified staff. Measures to stimulate people to
accept positions in the disadvantaged areas would relate to salaries, allowances, working
conditions and non-wage inputs,and other incentives.

117.      There is a trend towards more frequent consultations on governance issues between
government and its various stakeholders. However, frequency and quality o f dialogue between
government, development partners, Parliament, NGOs, CSOs and media does not meet the
expectation o f stakeholders. Consultations are still too few and poorly organized (last minute
invitations and difficult access to relevant preparatory documents by stakeholders). Inaddition,
there i s a lack o f transparency regardingthe selection o f stakeholders invited to for consultations.

PublicFinancialManagement

118.      During the past decade, Tanzania has strengthenedits public financial management
system, which is now generally assessed as being among the best in Africa. Nonetheless,
government i s keenly aware o f the need for continued improvements to prevent leakages o f funds
and avoid a loss o f confidence by tax payers and development partners. A Public Financial



                                                    33

Management Reform Programme (PFMRP) i s in place as a funding and coordination mechanism
o f reforms. A recent external evaluation suggested that the effectiveness o f the PFMRP could be
improved through better horizontal coordination and management o f the program, a broadeningof
the program beyond the Ministry o f Finance (MoF), and better linkages with other reform
programs. M o F has agreed to review the implementation o f the PFMRP with a view to improve its
impact. This section highlightskey developments in the areas o f procurement, external audit, and
financial management inlocal authorities.

119.     Government continuedthe roll-outof IFMS,andthe accompanyingtrainingto Local
Government Authorities (LGAs), covering already 86 of Tanzania's 114 LGAs. More and
more LGAs are qualifying to benefit from capital development grants. This improvement i s also
reflected in the number o f LGAs receiving clean audits from the Auditor General. The recent
posting o f 489 accountants and auditors to LGAs will further enhance these developments. For a
real deepening o f the decentralization process, a number o f key challenges have to be addressed.
These include reduction o f the multitude o f reporting and audit requirements - in particular by
donors, the lack o f qualified staff, and the compartmentalising o f funds for the different sectors,
resulting in large unspent balances at the district level. In addition, budget formulation and its
approval process in the LGAs continue to be faced with the challenge that central government
allocations do not reflect these budgetdplans, and thus undermine LGA authority/legislature. The
PEFAR 2006 assessment showed that particular areas o f weakness are large deviations in MDA
budget outturns, the lack o f a functional classification o f the budget, weak transparency in inter-
government fiscal relations, large proportion o f uncollected taxes, continuing leakages through the
procurement system, little follow up on audit recommendations, and insufficient parliamentary
oversight. To address these challenges strong leadership from the centre will have to be provided.

120.     For the first time, the NationalAudit Office (NAO) was able to produce the annual
audit report in a timely manner, with the 2004/05 report having been submittedby March
2006 and tabled for discussionin Parliamentbefore the June 2006 budget session. However,
due to a continued serious lack o f capacity (in particular in terns o f skills rather than number o f
staff), improving the quality o f these reports remains a challenge. Whilst the NAO capacity i s
already stretched for its responsibilities at the federal level, it also faces a serious challenge with
regardto the lower tier governments.

121.     The work o f the NAO covers the majority o f central government entities, but audits
comprise primarily o f transaction level testing. Whilst six value for money audit projects are
ongoing, the NAO still does not have the capacity to carry value for money audits on a regular
basis. Its capacity interms o f procurement audits i s also hampered by delays inthe implementation
o f the PPRA (details below).

122.     The NAO is improvingaudit quality with the support from SIDA and the PFMRP.
Looking forward, the following i s planned in order to ensure that NAO attains its target o f having
a wholesale adherence to international standards and highquality audit reports by 2010:
         ---- Have new financial audit manual inplace by 2007
              Adopt computer Audit packages by 2009
              Train Auditors on tracking expenditure through IFMS by 2009
         --   Incorporate matters o f Quality o fAudit report inthe new Public Audit Act by 2008
              Continue with the current spirit o f timely issuingthe audit report
              To continue translating C A G audit reports into Swahili.




                                                     34

123.      These actions are reflected in a new Corporate Plan being currently finalized by the NAO.
A New Public Audit Bill is expected to be submitted to Parliament by November 2007,
strengthening the independence o f the NAO.


PRSC-5PriorAction:NationalAudit OfficeGeneralReportfor FY05 issuedbyApril2006.
PRSC-6Trigger:Audit BillSubmittedto Parliament


124.      A new Public Procurement Act was enacted in 2005, which led to a decentralization
of procurement under the oversight of a newly established Public Procurement Regulatory
Authority (PPRA). The key challenge is to make the new law and the regulatory body work.
Staffing o f the PPRA has taken longer than expected and PPRA has only been fully staffed since
the end o f 2006. This i s an important step towards achieving progressively sufficient oversight o f
central government's as well as lower tier governments' procurement.

125.      PPRA is implementing a system for checking and monitoring procurement activities
in the procuring entities, including the computation of performance indicators based on
OECD/DAC/WorldBank Round Table process.' Implementation o f the system started inearly
2007 on a pilot basis inthe Ministry o f Water, Ministryo f Agriculture and Food Security, Ministry
o f Health and Social Welfare, TANROADS and TANESCO, but i s expected to be rolled out to all
MDAsandlocal authorities in2007/08.

126.      The implementationof procurement audits which would allow assessing compliance
of MDAs was contingent upon PPRA recruitingstaff to fill various positionsin its structure
including those of the investigation and procurement audits section. On a pilot basis, the
PPRA will carry out 20 procurement audits in the Ministry o f Health and Social Welfare,
TANROADS, and Ilala Municipal council in 2006/07. An audit o f TANESCO procurement i s
also ongoing.


Macroeconomic Stability, ResourceAllocation, and Budget Consistency

127.      Macro-economic stability and a public expenditure program that i s consistent with
Tanzania's MKUKUTA are at the core o f general budget support to Tanzania. Macro-economic
management and performance are discussed in the section on recent economic developments o f
this document. This section focuses thus primarily on public expenditure management issues.
Summarized below are the main issues pertaining to budget execution for 2005/06, the approved
budget for 2006/07 and the strengthening o fthe linkbetween the budget and policy.

128.      Budget Execution2005/06. The implementation of the budget for 2005/06 was on track,
despite the adverse effects o f a drought and rising oil prices. The drought-induced food shortages
forced the government to make internal budget reallocations to cater for the procurement and
distribution o f emergency food to severely affected areas. Additional financial support was also
provided to TANESCO to pay for capacity and energy charges to IPTL and SONGAS, and to
procure transformers.




'Section 17 of the ProcurementAct provides remediesto be taken in case of non-compliance. Further Section 26 of the Act requires the
Annual Performance Evaluation Report of PPRA which includes an evaluation of the operations of PPRA and the ProcuringEntities to
be submitted to Controller and Auditor Generaland to the Ministerof Finance who shall table it to the Parliament.



                                                               35

129.     Revenue collection for 2005/06 registered at TSh 2,124,844 million implyinga 3 percent
over performance and 20 percent higher than that o f 2004/05. The key drivers o f this performance
are import duty, corporate tax, VAT on imports and taxes on domestic sales. Other significant
contributors to the performance are rental taxes as well as income tax (Pay As You Earn).

130.     Expenditure performance for 2005/06 was broadly in-line with budget estimates, with
recurrent (excluding CFS) and development expenditure reaching 98 percent and 87 percent
respectively. The underperformance in development expenditure was partly a result o f non-
implementation o f infrastructure projects through the infrastructure bond financing.              The
percentage deviation o f actual recurrent expenditure from budgeted expenditure for MDAs
amounted to 17 percent as compared to 18 percent inthe previous year.

131.     Total grants received during the financial year 2005/06 amounted to TSh 1,043.4 billion
which i s 2 percent higher than the budget. The analysis by components indicates that program
grants, basket grants and HIPC relief each performed at 92, 94 and 82 percent respectively against
estimates, while project grants reached 23 percent above the estimate. Overall foreign financing
performed at 96 percent and net domestic financing (NDF) registered a draw down o f deposits o f
TSh 348.9 billion duringthe financial year 2005/06, which i s 97 percent o f the budget estimate.


PRSC-5 Prior Action: Expenditureoutturn for FY06consistentwith approvedbudget
PRSC-6 Trigger:Expenditureoutturn for FY07consistentwith approvedbudget




132.     Approved budget for 2006/07. Budget revenues in 2006/07 are expected to increase to
TSh 2,461 billion (14.4percent o f GDP), a 16 percent annual increase over 2005/06 revenues
primarily on account o f policy measures announced in the budget for 2006/07 and continued tax
and customs administrationreforms.

133.     Salaries and wages have increased by 47.2% from TSh 681 billion in 2005/06 to TSh
1,003 in 2006/07. The TSh 322 billion increase in salaries and wages takes up most o f the overall
recurrent budget increase o f TSh 325 billion. The 2006/07 increases primarily fund general salary
increase (TSh 257 billion). About TSh 20 billion will finance promotions o f existing staff.

134.     About TSh 45 billion are allocated for the recruitment o f 24,000 new government
employees in the health, primary and secondary education, and public safety services, accountants
and lawyers, where priority i s given to the middle professional cadres. The planned recruitment
during 2006/07 caters for staff in areas critical to the achievement o f MKUKUTA objective and
the filling o f vacancies that resulted from a slow pace in recruitment over the past three years.
About half o f all planned recruitments will be carried out by local governments, to increase
capacity at that level. The increase in salaries i s partly financed through a significant reductionin
allowances inthe FY07 budget.

135.     The increase in the wage bill reduces the flexibility o f government to react to fluctuations
in the overall resource envelope.     Given the demands for a more professional public service
coupled with front line service delivery, government needs to focus on ensuring the `right-size' o f
the public sector across MDAs and local governments.

136.     Withregardto the decompression o f salary structure, the Presidential Salary Enhancement
Commission i s undertalung a study which, among others, will propose ways to rationalize the


                                                   36

salary structure in the government sector as well as a medium term path to efficiently implement
the pay reform.     PO-PSM has also commissioned a study to compare salary levels in the
government sector and that in the private sector. The output o f this study will feed into other
policy formulatiodreview processes.

137.     Other recurrent expenditures are projected to increase only modestly because of the
unwinding o f one-off spending for last year's drought, and for payments to the former EAC
workers.     The savings from which were directed mostly to development expenditure.
Development expenditure i s projected to increase by 25 percent in 2006/07, mainly reflecting the
use o f IMF MDRI resources for investment in the energy sector to diversify TANESCO's
generation sources.

138.     Net domestic financing during2006/07 i s projected at TSh 163.5 billion, or 1 percent o f
the GDP compared with 2.3 percent for 2005/06.

139.     MKUKUTA - Budget Links. The government has further strengthenedthe linkbetween
budgetary resource allocation and MKUKUTA priorities through cluster-based expenditure
programming, and rolling out the Strategic Budget Allocation System (SBAS) to the Regional
Secretariats in addition to the Ministries, Departments and Agencies (MDAs), that were already
usingthe system. Direct MKUKUTA interventions account for 48 percent o f planned expenditure
in2006/07, compared to 47 percent in2005/06. The relevant shares o fMKUKUTACluster are: 46
percent for Growth and Reduction o f Income Poverty; 36 percent for Improvement o f Quality o f
Life and Social Well-being; and 18 percent for Governance and Accountability.

140.     The completion o f the ongoing MKUKUTA costing exercise will further strengthen the
government's effort to prioritize and link the resource allocation with MKUKUTA.The costing o f
MKUKUTA interventions for the roads, water, health and agriculture is already in an advanced
stage and will provide inputto the next budget cycle. A preliminary investment plan for the energy
sector has been developed as part o f TANESCO's MediumTerm Financial Recovery Plan (FRP).
The government will use the plans to solicit funding from development partners as part o f
MKUKUTAfinancing.

141.     A large operational deficit o f TANESCO, reflecting tariffs that are significantly below
operating costs as well as the procurement o f emergency generating capacity and higher cost o f
thermal generation, was i s expected to be covered by a commercial loan in the amount o f TSh 300
billion in 2006/07, which i s currently under negotiation. The implementation o f the FRP entails
measures to increase revenue and reduce cost which would significantly reduce TANESCO's
operational deficit in2007/08 and thus significantly reduce the need for government to subsidies.


PRSC-5 Prior Action: Approved budget for FY07 in line with policy objectives (MKUKUTA, sector
policies)
PRSC-6Trigger:Approvedbudgetfor FY08inline withpolicyobjectives(MKUKUTA, sectorpolicies)


142.     Government has also taken measuresto strengthen the link between budgets of the
local authorities and the MKUKUTA and to advance the decentralizationprocess. These
include:

     J The Prime Minister's Office     -RegionalAdministration andLocalGovernments (PMO-
         RALG) consolidates and submits budget requests from the local authorities to the budget


                                                 37

        guidelines committee.         T o strengthen further LGAs' representation in the budget
        formulation process, the number o f PMO-RALG members o f the budget guidelines
        committee will be increased to three starting inthe 2007/08 budget cycle.

     J  The central government i s organising a training session to further build capacity o f LGA
        officials inplanning, budgeting, and budget execution.

     J  Currently LGAs use PlanRep to prepare their budgets based on the ceilings set using
        formula based allocation system. The software enables them to linkthe MTEFtargets with
        MKUKUTA,and is compatiblewith SBAS.

     J  The government continues with the efforts to increase autonomy at the LGAs level by
        ensuring that resources are channelled to the lowest possible level of service delivery.
        Thus all resources which are budgeted at the sector ministrylevel will be gradually shifted
        to the LGAsbudgets.

143.    Although good progress can be observed in the elaboration o f the fiscal decentralisation
framework, the use o f the framework i s still limited, as most central ministries are not abiding by
it. D-by-Dimplies that the fundingo f services like education, health, etc. is done under the LGA
budget line, via a formula based allocation mechanism, and not under those o f the respective
ministries concerned. Though this i s generally accepted, the budget clearly shows that the
implementation o f the Tanzanian decentralisation policy i s not yet reflected in the national budget
for the coming years. Implementation o f D-by-Dwould imply a reduction in the allocations to
sector ministries and an increase inthose for the LGAs.Figures inthe budget indicate that increase
o f funding to the local level as a percentage o f total expenditures i s only increasing marginally
(82% o f the budget i s still allocated to the central level). However, some sectors, such as health,
have undertaken an exercise with PMORALG to review what expenditures could be further
decentralized inpreparation for the FY07/08 budget.

SUMMARYASSESSMENTOF OVERALLPROGRESS

144.    The GBS Annual Review 2006 was held froml6th to 20th October 2006 inDar es Salaam.
It was the first to be conducted under the new structure of the Partnership Framework
Memorandum (PFM) governing GBS for the implementation o f the MKUKUTA. The P F M was
signed by the government and the GBS partners in January 2006. The main objective o f the
Annual Review 2006 was to jointly assess performance against the commitments made inthe PFM
and inparticular in the Performance Assessment Framework (PAF), which draws on MKUKUTA
and other national processes. Six Thematic Workmg Groups (TWGS)'reviewed all areas o f the
PAF and discussed their findings in TWG pre-review plenary sessions from the 10th to 12th
October 2006. For the first time, non-state actors and UN agencies were invited to participate in
the review process. The GBS Annual Review focused then on a number o f jointly agreed key
issues arising from the work o f the TWGs. The Annual Review also acknowledged major
developments that took place during the review period and which influenced the pace o f
implementation o f reforms. These include the general elections that took place in late 2005 and
which saw the election o f a new President, followed by the formation o f a new structure o f

'TWG    1 - MKUKUTA Cluster 1 (Growth and Reduction of Income Poverty), TWG 2 - MKUKUTA Cluster 2
(Improvement of Quality of Life and Social Well-being), TWG 3 - MKUKUTA Cluster 3 (Governance and
Accountability), TWG 4 - ResourceAllocation and Budget Consistency, TWG 5 - Public FinancialManagement, TWG
6 - Macroeconomic Stability


                                                    38

government. During the period under review, Tanzania also suffered from a severe drought which
claimed much o f government's attention in an effort to mitigate its impact on agriculture, food
security, and the power supply. Within this context, the assessment o f the six TWGs and the
Annual Review 2006 concluded that overall progress against the PAF indicators has been
generally satisfactory. At the same time, a number o f critical issues were highlighted that require
attention by the authorities ifthe implementation o f the MUKUKUTA is to remain on track. Areas
o f particular concern were the energy crisis and slower progress than anticipated in legal sector
reform and anti-corruption legislation.

145.     The following main findings emerged from the TWG reports and the Annual Review:

           The government has produced three extensive analytical national reports, which provide
           information on changes in the three MKUKUTA clusters o f Growth and Reduction o f
           Income Poverty; Improvement o f Quality o f Life and Social Well-being; Governance
           and Accountability. These are the Poverty and Human Development Report 2005, the
           Status Report 2006 on 86 MKUKUTA indicators, and the zero draft MKUKUTA
           Annual ImplementationReport 2005/06. For future reviews, the commitment inthe PFM
           to provide the final MKUKUTA Annual Implementation Report in sufficient time as a
           main input into the Annual Review was stressed.

           Ina rather difficult external environment which included a severe drought and high oil
           prices, overall macroeconomic management in Tanzania remains consistent with
           sustaining macroeconomic stability and a supportive environment for Tanzania's poverty
           reduction efforts. In addition to the effects o f drought on agriculture during 2005,
           economic growth this year i s threatened by a substantial loss o f generation capacity in
           the energy sector, which requires urgent attention by the government. A regular forum
           for dialogue on the implementation o f the government's growth strategy has not yet been
           established.   There i s also a need for continued efforts on reforms that remove
           bottlenecks to growth in the economy, particularly in agriculture, energy, infrastructure
           and private sector development.

          Notable progress has been made inthe social sectors under cluster 2 o f the MKUKUTA,
           with considerable improvements in outcomes on education, water and health. However,
           financing and human resource constraints are great impediments to effective service
           delivery, in particular at local government level. A necessary expansion in frontline
           service delivery needs to be balanced with considerations o f ensuring a sustainable wage
          bill.

           The Public Service Reform Programme (PSRP) and the Local Government Reform
          Programme (LGRP) have been progressing, though continued efforts o f the government
           are needed to fully implement its public sector pay reform and its policy o f
          Decentralisation by Devolution (D-by-D) across government. The Legal Sector Reform
          Programme (LSRP) has still to become fully operational and requires broadened
           ownership and strengthened dialogue around it. Stakeholder dialogue on governance and
           anti-corruption and a review mechanism on the soon to be launched second National
          Anti-Corruption Strategy and Action Plan need to be enhanced. The revised anti-
          corruption bill, initially expected to be submitted to Parliament in November 2006, was
          only submitted in February 2007. This legislation i s crucial for further progress in the
          fight against corruption. Overall, better coordination o f the different existing core
          reforms i s needed.


                                                 39

    (v)    The government has continued with its effort to maintain macroeconomic stability,
           improving the quality o f budget formulation, budget implementation as well as the
           overall expenditure discipline. Budget execution inFY 2005/06 and the budget frame for
           FY 2006/07 are consistent with the MKUKUTA, with more in-depth assessment to be
           carried out as part o f the regular PEFAR exercise. The government continued to
           strengthen the planning and budgeting process and its link to MKUKUTA with the
           rolling out o f the Strategic Budget Allocation System (SBAS) to Regional Secretariats in
           addition to MDAs, the use o f the PlanRep (Planning and Reporting) software by Local

           Utekelezaji wa MKUKUTA) - a system to monitor and report on MKUKUTA
           Government Authorities (LGAs), and the recent development o f RIMKU (Ripoti ya

           implementation. The new software enables the linking o f planning and budgeting with
           performance monitoring on MKUKUTA. Further efforts to improve the planning and
           budgeting process are however needed. First, in line with the introduction o f the SBAS
           system, priority needs to be given to monitoring and reporting o f budget outturns in the
           SBAS format. Other areas o f reform include the formulation o f an overall strategy for
           planning and budgeting reforms, strengthening o f the PER process,, and increasing the
           predictability o f external resource projections, in particular for the outer years o f the
           MTEFperiod.

    (vi) Progress in public financial management has taken place on several issues. Most
           notably, the 2004/05 National Audit Report was presented within statutory time limits
           and operationalisation o f the Public Procurement Regulatory Authority (PPRA) has
           made good progress. A solid underlying process for public financial management review
           i s in place in the form o f the Public Expenditure and Financial Accountability Review
           (PEFAR). The Public Financial Management Reform Programme (PFMRP), however,
           continues to suffer from weak coordination mechanisms and a lack o f strategic
           framework.

Review of progressagainst PRSC-5 triggers and conversionto prior actions
146.     Seven of the nine PRSC-5 triggers were substantially completed at the time of the
Annual Review in October 2006 and were retained as prior actions without substantive
changes. A new Roads Billhad not been submitted to Parliament by October/November 2006, as
envisaged under the PRSC-5 trigger, following delays inthe completion o f a road inventory. The
adjusted prior action reflects these delays, but achowledges the progress that has been made to
date, including the preparation o f a draft bill and completion o f the road inventory as well as
Cabinet approval o f the submission o f a new Road Bill to Parliament. It i s now expected that the
Road Bill will be submitted to Parliament by April 2007, which i s also a trigger for PRSC-6. The
trigger related to crop board reform i s adjusted to reflect the more comprehensive and consultative
approach adopted by government as discussed in paragraphs 78 and 79. The related prior action
for PRSC-5 i s "Completion o f consultations and signing o f MOUs for at least four crop boards".
Further progress in the preparation o f the legal amendments for all crop boards i s proposed as a
trigger for PRSC-6. The submission o f the revised legislation for all crop boards i s expected to
occur byNovember 2007, but not later than February 2008.

147.      Implications of performance assessment for PRSC-5 financing.                      The 2006 GBS
Annual Review concluded that there was generally satisfactory performance as assessed against
the performance assessment framework. However, the review highlighted serious concerns about
weaknesses and delays in the government response to the energy crisis and the implementation o f
the Legal Sector Reform Program, as well as the delays in submitting new anti-corruption
legislation to Parliament. The more narrowly focused review o f performance against PRSC-5
triggers broadly reflects the overall performance assessment, with satisfactory progress in most
areas covered except infi-astructure,where there has been a delay inthe submission o f a new Roads
Bill to Parliament. Inaddition, performance inthe roads sector showed additional shortcomings,




                                                   41

including the significant underperformance on rehabilitation o f rural roads, which i s a specific
PAF indicator.

148.     Several options were considered regarding the appropriate response to the delay in
the completion of the Roads Bill trigger. The first option considered was to delay the credit
until the trigger had been completed. However, this would not have been consistent with the
Partnership Framework Memorandum, which requires that development partners make firm
commitments o f the amount o f budget support for the next fiscal year not later than six weeks after
the issuing o f the final annual review report.             The option o f proceeding with the originally
envisaged amount o f PRSC-5 o f US$200 million was considered to be inconsistent with the
explicit performance link between progress in the implementation o f key reforms and the amount
o f PRSC support. Thus, the option adopted was to reduce the amount o f PRSC-5 to US$190
million in line with the adjustment made in the conversion o f the respective trigger into a prior
action. This i s intended to signal clearly the importance o f advancing reforms inthe infrastructure
at an appropriate pace and also to maintain the credibility o f the performance based nature o f the
instrument.      Significant progress in infrastructure reforms will also be critical to justify the
proposed increase to US$250 million for PRSC-6.


PRSC-6 Triggers
149.     Eleven triggers for PRSC-6 have been carefully selectedjointly with government to reflect
critical measures towards the achievement o f overall MKUKUTA objectives.                           In particular,
government has identified reforms in the infrastructure sector as the key priority for the current
year which i s reflected in three triggers. A trigger related to the reform o f crop board reforms
continues previous PRSC support and monitoring in this area. Not only are these agriculture
sector reforms critical to enhancing the incomes o f the poor, but the recent change in government
strategy and previous delays inthis area suggest a continued focus o f the PRSC on these reforms.
For the MKUKUTA Cluster 2, the three triggers targeting satisfactory sector reviews reflect thsit
these sectors (a) are critical for service delivery to the poor, (b) claim a large part o f MKUKUTA
related public expenditures, and (c) are being mainstreamed into the PRSC through the ongoing
transition from project and sector adjustment support to general budget support financing. Inthe
Cluster 3 area o f good governance and accountability, government's efforts to strengthen anti-
corruption and audit legislation are included as two separate triggers. Finally, two triggers related
to consistency o f the budget and budget execution with the MKUKUTA are central to monitor
whether PRSC resources (as well as GBS resources and government's own revenue) are used in
line with MKUKUTA objectives.
