IMPLEMENTATION, MONITORING AND EVALUATION
150.    Monitoring arrangements. In the context o f the PRSC and the MKUKUTA, outcome,
output, and input indicators provide direct information on the status o f poverty in all its
dimensions. Monitoringo f these indicators i s based on a broad range o f surveys and administrative
data systems identified in the MKUKUTA monitoring master plan. The government has produced
three national analytical reports, which provide information on changes in the three MKUKUTA
clusters o f Growth and Reduction o f Income Poverty; Improvement o f Quality o f Life and Social
Well-being; and Governance and Accountability. These are the Poverty and Human Development
Report 2005, the Status Report 2006 on 86 MKUKUTA indicators, and the draft MKUKUTA
Annual Implementation Report 2005/06. The Poverty and Human Development Report (PHDR),
which i s published bi-annually, consolidates poverty measures from various sources, and provide
analysis o f poverty trends and underlying factors. Sector reviews are key monitoring mechanisms
for developments at the sectoral level, and the PRGFFSI reviews, quarterly macro-financial
developments report, as well as the monthly Bank o f Tanzania economic reports are the key
sources for the assessment o f the macroeconomic situation. Progress in public expenditure
management covering improvements in systems, enhanced allocative and operational efficiency in
the use o f inputs, and the consistency o f the budget and budget executionwith the MKUKUTA are
tracked by the annual PEFARs.

151.    An additional dimension o f the monitoring framework covers the government-donor
relationship in monitoring progress on PRSC objectives, such as enhanced local ownership o f the
development process, participation by all stakeholders, and efficiency o f aid delivery mechanisms.
This dimension will be reviewed in the context o f the joint annual reviews o f the JAST and
through a mid-term and final assessment o f the performance o f government and development
partners in implementing the JAST in line with JAST indicators, targets, and actions. These
assessments will be carried out by an Independent Monitoring Group.

152.    Supervision and subsequent PRSCs. Supervision and preparation o f subsequent credits
will take place in close collaboration with other donors and be consistent with the MKUKUTA
review mechanism. For most o f the program areas, supervision and dialogue will be carried out on
a continuous basis in the context o f sectoral or thematic programs. A joint GBS review will be
conducted annually for an overall assessment of progress made in each o f the program areas. In
addition, the achievement o f PRSC targets will be assessed annually in the context o f the
MKUKUTAreview that will document the progress made inthe MKUKUTA implementation and
analyze the outcomes o f reforms. The reform program sets out clear quantitative and qualitative
benchmarks for the implementation period o f the MKUKUTA. The PRSC targets are fully
consistent with the MKUKUTA. Given satisfactory progress, Tanzania's reform program will be



                                                  43

supported through subsequent PRSCs under the programmatic lending framework, subject to the
approval by the Board o f Executive Directors.

POVERTYAND SOCIAL IMPACT
153.     Preparation and implementation o f the MKUKUTA i s underpinned by a wide range o f
analytic work on the poverty and social impact o f reforms. Here we highlight some o f the main
findingsthat relate to the triggers and prior actions monitoredunder PRSC-5 and PRSC-6.

154.     The stakeholder and institutionalmapping carried out as part o f PSIA work on crop boards
by and large confirmed the positive impact o f such reforms on smallholders. A PSIA on local
government revenue showed that the reforms o f local taxation and business licensing have made
the tax system more progressive and thus pro-poor.

155.     Improvements in the road network are a critical for improved market access inrural areas
and are thus a key element o f Tanzania's poverty reduction strategy. Specific road projects are
typically subject to environmental and social analysis with appropriate measures taken to mitigate
any negative impacts. The reforms in the road sector supported by the PRSC are expected to
strengthen the assessment o f impact and the design o f appropriate mitigating measures.

156.     Reforms in the energy sector, including potential increases in tariffs, are expected to have
an overall positive impact on the poor. This i s due to the fact that at present, the poor have
extremely limited access to energy which implies, that energy sector subsidies benefit primarily
the better o f f and potentially drain resources from pro-poor spending. Analytic work (e.g., the
investment climate assessment) shows clearly that lack o f access and interruptions to the power
supply are the primary constraints to economic growth andresult inhighcost o f doing business, if
generators have to substitute for electricity from the grid. The private sector also recognizes the
need for power sector reforms, including increased tariffs, as the basis for improvements in the
reliability and availability o f the power supply.

157.     Poverty incidence analyses o f Tanzania's efforts to strengthen primary education and to
expand access to health services and water show mixed results.            The expansion o f primary
education has clearly benefited the poor, while improvements in health indicators are more evenly
distributed across income quintiles.      Improvements in access to water have to date occurred
primarily in urban areas and benefited households in the upper income quintiles. However, with
the establishment o f a water sector development program and the scalingup o f support to the water
sector, access to water by the poor i s expected to improve.

FIDUCIARYASPECTS
158.     Foreign exchange. The safeguards assessment o f the Bank o f Tanzania (BOT),which was
completed by the IMF on December 5, 2003, found that, while the bank had a relatively strong
internal control environment, some vulnerabilities existed, notably in the external audit and
financial reporting areas. In order to mitigate these vulnerabilities, staff made a number o f
recommendations. The BOThas since completed most o f these recommendations, including: (i)
annual external audits that are conducted in accordance with International Standards on Auditing
(ISA); (ii)adoption o f International Accounting Standards (IAS) as the financial reporting
framework; (iii)  establishment o f a formal process o f reconciling accounting data to the program
data reportedto the Fund; (iv) publication o f the full audited financial statements on a timely basis,
within five months o f the financial year's end; and (v) adoption o f an audit charter for the internal
audit function to strengthen audit methodologies and procedures. There i s the need for greater


                                                  44

clarity on the mechanism by which the BOT'Sforeign exchange losses are covered by the
government. Recent allegations o f improprieties in the management o f the external commercial
debt account are currently under investigation.

159.    Budget resources. In depth assessments o f Tanzania's public financial management and
procurement systems are carried out in the context o f the annual Public Expenditure and Financial
Accountability Review (PEFAR). Overall, Tanzania's public financial management system
continues to be among the strongest in Sub-Saharan Africa. The 2006 PEFAR and the PEFA
indicators suggests particular vulnerabilities in the areas o f the composition o f expenditure out-
turn compared to the original approved budget, the tax collection system, competition, value for
money, and controls in procurement and, the quality o f and follow up to external audits. The
PRSC supports government's efforts to address residualweaknesses inthese areas. A Public
Finance Management Reform Program i s in place to support sustained improvements in
Tanzania's public expenditure management system.

DISBURSEMENTAND AUDITING
160.    Borrower and credit amount. The borrower i s the United Republic o f Tanzania. A single-
tranche credit o f SDR 127.6 million (US$190 million equivalent) would be made available upon
credit effectiveness, anticipated for July 2007. The closing date o f the operation would be June
30, 2008. It i s envisaged that there will be three further single-tranche, annual PRSCs to support
the implementation o f the MKUKUTA duringthe period FY09 -FY11.

161.    Disbursement, reporting, and auditing arrangements. The proposed credit will follow
the Bank's disbursement procedures for development policy credits. Credit proceeds will be
disbursed against satisfactory implementation o f the development policy program and not tied to
any specific purchases or be subject to procurement requirements. Once the credit i s approved by
the Board and becomes effective, the proceeds o f the credit will be deposited at the request o f the
Borrower by IDA in an account designated by the Borrower at the Bank o f Tanzania and which
forms part o f the country's official foreign exchange reserves. The Borrower shall ensure that upon
the deposit o f the Credit into said account, an equivalent amount i s credited in local currency to an
account that i s available to finance budgeted expenditures. The Borrower will provide a written
confirmation to the Bank when the amount has been credited to an account that i s available to
finance budgeted expenditures. If the proceeds o f the credit are used for ineligible purposes as
defined in the Development Credit Agreement, IDA will require the Borrower to promptly upon
notice from IDA, refund an amount equal to the amount o f said payment to IDA. Amounts
refunded to the Bank upon such request shall be cancelled. The administration o f this credit will be
the responsibility o fthe Ministryo fFinance.

162.    The following arrangements support the requirements related to fiduciary assurance:

        Foreign reserve account. The government will acknowledge to IDA receipt o f the money
        into the foreign reserve account and the crediting o f this amount in local currency to the
        Tanzania government Consolidated Fund Account.             While no external audit will be
        required, it i s expected that the confirmation o f receipt will be countersigned by the
        Accountant General and the Controller and Auditor General.

    0   Public (government) accounts. The Controller and Auditor General i s required by law to
        produce his annual report to Parliament on the public accounts within nine months o f the
        financial year-end. IDA will have access to those reports.




                                                  45

     0  Bank of Tanzania. The annual entity financial statements o fthe Bank o f Tanzania, audited
        in accordance with international auditing standards as promulgated by the International
        Federation o f Accountants, are publicly available.
ENVIRONMENTALASPECTS
163.    The PRSC contributes to the financing o f the implementation o f Tanzania's MKUKUTA
across all three MKUKUTA clusters, but the policy dialogue focuses primarily on institutional
reforms in selected areas. This section thus discusses (a) the likely environmental effects of the
actions monitored under the PRSC performance assessment framework and (b) the overall capacity
o f government to monitor and mitigate environmentaleffects o fbudgetary expenditure.

164.    With respect to the policy reforms contained in the Performance Assessment Framework
and supported by the operation, and given the potential negative environmental implications o f
policy reforms and their implementation, several measures are taken to ensure that any negative
environmental effects o f the various activities will be properly addressed by the government of
Tanzania in collaboration with its development partners. Among the policies supported by the
operation that may raise concerns from an environmental perspective i s the rehabilitation o f
15,000 kmo f roads by 2010. The implementing agency for road works on main roads in Tanzania,
TANROADS, has established procedures for the environmental impact assessment o f its road
works. The preparation o f EMand EMP i s mandatory for all upgrading works. An environmental
unit at TANROADS reviews such EIA (normally done by external consultants) and monitors the
implementation o f the EMP. Only very low impact road works are done on rural roads by the
local governments. The supervising agency at PMO-RALGneeds to buildup some EA capacity to
advise the LG accordingly. In the rural sector, another area o f potential environmental and natural
resource concerns, supported country policies will promote a more sustainable use o f natural
resources and minimize waste. The implementation o f the crop board r e f o m actions i s unlikely to
have significant adverse environmental effect. The crop board reform action plan o f October 2005
focuses on improving the legal and regulatory framework for coffee, cotton and other traditional
export crops. The actions include abolishing levies and fees, increased accountability to
stakeholders, greater transparency and elimination o f rent seeking behavior, rationalization o f
licensing fees, and discontinuance o f some input subsidy programs (e.g. the input voucher
program). Crop board reforms are purely institutional reforms to improve marketing and access to
markets and any increased levels o f activity resulting from crop board reforms are being monitored
under the country's framework for managing environmental risks, as outlined below.

165.    In analyzing potential effects of supported country policies on the environment and the
government's environmental management capacity inthe roads and rural sector, the Bank relies on
a variety o f analytic work undertaken over the past years. These include a Strategic Environmental
Assessment under previous PRSCs and analytic work underpinning environmental aspects o f
investment projects, including Bank projects in the roads, water, and energy sector. In addition,
the Bank supported studies on the interaction between poverty and environment as well as growth
and environment.

166.    As sectoral support for health, water, and education is being shifted from specific sectoral
operations to the PRSC, the PRSC draws on the environmental management systems that
government has established with the support o f these projects.

167.    In addition to the assessment of potential effects of policy actions directly supported by
the PRSC, the PRSC also supports government in its efforts to monitor and mitigate environmental
effects o f public expenditures, financed from domestic resources and general budget support


                                                 46

provided under the GBS framework.            The implementation o f the National Environmental
Management Act will strengthen in-country capacity for screening, monitoring investments for
positive and negative environmental effects and the ability at national and district level for
identifying mitigation measures for reducing effects. The Act i s very comprehensive and has
specific provisions for EA, SEA as well as conflict resolution and administrative processes for
pollution charges according to the `polluter pays' principle. The Vice President's Office and the
National Environmental Management Council have been supporting the development and
implementation o f various sector-specific guidelines for EA (initially inroads and agriculture), and
have more recently pursued procedures for strategic and sectoral environmental assessment to
permit mitigation o f any adverse environmental effects while tahng advantage o f potential
synergies between poverty reduction and environmental protection efforts.

PARTICIPATORY ASPECTS
168.    The reform agenda supported by the PRSC derives from Tanzania's MKUKUTA, which
has been developed through a consultative process in which government sought inputs and
feedback from a wide array o f stakeholders representing all levels o f society and all regions o f
Tanzania. Similarly, Tanzania's MKUKUTA monitoring system also i s open to inputs from inside
and outside government.

169.    Aside from the MKUKUTA, the government o f Tanzania carries out wide ranging
consultations on most policy reforms and many o f the policy areas covered by the PRSC are
underpinned by sectoral or thematic processes that are open to stakeholders from civil society,
NGOs, academic and research institutes, the private sector, and the donor community. This
includes the PER process as a regular forum for dialogue on public expenditure, financial, and
macro-economic management, the Investor Round Table and the Tanzania National Business
Council for government -private sector dialogue, and the environment working group. Inaddition,
Tanzania has also put inplace a process for an independent monitoring o f the government - donor
relationship, which also covers the PRSC.

170.    The design o f the GBS framework seeks to encourage the further development o f
participatory review processes in all areas, which would then provide the main inputsto the overall
GBS assessment.

RISKS AND R I S K MITIGATION
171.    PRSC-5 supports the implementation o f Tanzania's MKUKUTA and there are two closely
interrelated and overlapping sets o f risks. The first set includes general risks that may interfere
with the successful implementation o f the MKUKUTA, including the vulnerability o f Tanzania's
economy to external shocks, political risks related to the situation in Zanzibar, aid dependence,
decentralization, and capacity constraints. Second are risks that are related to the design o f the
GBS process in Tanzania and the effective utilization o f PRSC resources towards the achievement
o f the MKUKUTA objectives, including the reliance on the domestic budget process, the
transition to sector review processes, weak domestic accountability, and fiduciary risks. Second

        Vulnerability of Tanzania's growth.            Tanzania's growth performance remains
        vulnerable to external shocks which can be exacerbated by domestic structural
        weaknesses. Recurrent droughts have significant impacts on agriculture and the power
        sector, which i s heavily dependent on hydro generation.         The planned expansion o f
        Tanzania's power generation capacity, which also includes diversification into thermal
        (gas, coal, and fuel) power sources, will be important to avoid recurrent energy crises.


                                                 47

  Other areas o f vulnerability to external shocks include tourism, which has become a
  leading source o f foreign exchange and which could be affected by developments in the
  international security situation, agriculture which remains the main source o f income and
  employment for Tanzania and exposed to climatic shocks, and terms o f trade, especially
  with regardto the prices o f the leadingexport commodities including gold, coffee, tea, and
  fish, and import commodities, especially oil.

0 Politicalrisk. Since independence in 1961, Tanzania has developed a strong track record
  o f political stability which underpins the steadfast implementation o f reforms, once a
  consensus has been reached. The main political risk relates to Zanzibar. Zanzibar's semi-
  autonomous status and popular opposition have led to three contentious elections since
  1995, which the ruling party won despite international observers' claims o f voting
  irregularities.  In October 2001, the ruling and the main opposition party reached a
  bilateral accord, which was hailed as a landmark achievement by an African country to
  solve its thorny political issues through internal dialogue. The main opposition party has
  as a result been represented in the Zanzibar Electoral Commission as part o f the
  agreement.     In 2006, government and the main opposition party reaffirmed their
  commitment to revive the process for mutual political understanding in Zanzibar,
  popularly known as Muafaka. The Zanzibari government i s preparing its own poverty
  reduction strategy (MKUZA) inconsultation with stakeholders and development partners

0 Aid-dependence. Tanzania's dependence on external aid, which is about 12 percent o f
  GDP and finances almost half o f its budget, makes the country extremely vulnerable to
  changes in donor sentiment. The potential impact o f cutbacks ingeneral budget support i s
  likely to be larger than that related to traditional project support for two reasons. First,
  general budget support financed about one third o f Tanzania's recurrent government
  expenditures in FY06 and cutbacks on recurrent expenditures would have an immediate
  impact on service delivery. Second, a cutback in project support typically only affects new
  commitments, while disbursement o f existing projects typically continue. With respect to
  general budget support, disbursements are directly subject to change at donor's discretion.
  The PFMunderlying the PRSC recognizes these risks and contains a general commitment
  by all participating donors that, in case budget support is to be reduced, this should be
  done in a gradual manner. The Tanzania Assistance Strategy also recognizes the need to
  design aid modalities with a view towards minimizing the scope for abrupt changes.
  However, there i s also a clear recognition that the authorities need to maintain an
  appropriate environment for sustaining relatively highlevels o f aid.

  Decentralization.Another risk for the achievement o f MKUKUTA objectives arises fiom
  the ongoing decentralization process, which shifts responsibility for key poverty reducing
  programs in education, health, the water sector, and agriculture to the districts. The local
  government reformprogram focuses on strengthening the capacity o f local authorities. As
  a result, there have been significant improvement in accountability processes at the local
  level and about 50 percent o f local authorities now meet basic accountability standards,
  compared to an estimated 10 percent only a few years ago. The PRSC also supports
  actions to strengthen the watchdog role o f beneficiaries at the community level through
  disclosure o f the flow o f resources to various activities at the grassroots level and
  expenditure tracking o f financial flows from the central government through the annual
  PERs.




                                            48

   0    Capacity constraints. Limited human resources capacity to implement and manage the
       PRSC reform agenda represents another risk.A large number o f capacity-building efforts
        are in place. Most reform programs have capacity-building components embedded,
        including the PFMRP, the LGRP, and the PSRP. Similarly, sector programs and projects
        typically also contain capacity building components. The challenge for Tanzania i s to
        enhance the effectiveness o f capacity-building efforts by shifting from supply-driven to
        demand-driven efforts and address capacity building in a holistic way, in which the
        incentive structure, education system, and specific capacity-building efforts are organized
        ina mutually reinforcing manner. Analytic work to address these issues is planned. Inthe
        medium term, it i s expected that the shift from project to budget support by the Bank and
        other donors will release staff who are currently tied up in project management to play a
        greater role inoverall public sector management.

   0    Reliance on domestic budget process. The utilization o f PRSC resources i s determined
        by the domestic budget process with the possibility that resource allocation is influenced
        by other factors than the pursuit o f the MKUKUTA objectives. The principal mitigating
        measure derives from strengthening the role o f domestic stakeholders in budget
        formulation and budget review. Government's continued commitment to an open and
        participatory PER and budget process i s an important and well established tool to support
        and monitor consistency o f budget formulation and execution with the MKUKUTA. It i s
        worth noting that due to fungibility o fresources, the allocativerisk also exists for other aid
        modalities and the PRSC i s explicitly designed to address this risk.

   0    Transition to sector review processes. An important innovation o f the PRSC program i s
        the shift from a detailed policy matrix with annual monitorable actions to greater reliance
        on sector reviews as the primary fora for dialogue and assessment. Experience from
        Tanzania's health sector review and from other countries suggests that it typically takes
        several years for these reviews to mature and to provide a fully satisfactory forum for
        dialogue and assessment. A central objective o f the GBS i s thus to ensure that these
       reviews do take place and improve over time as government develops adequate capacity
        for these sector reviews.

   0    Weak domestic accountability. One o f the objectives o f the redesigned GBS framework
        i s to use domestic processes for the assessment o f progress and dialogue. This i s intended
        to foster domestic accountability. However, as some of the analytic work has shown, these
       processes are still weak, partly because o f weak capacity o f civil society organizations,
       NGOs, and the private sector to participateinthese processes.

        Fiduciary risks. The 2006 PEFAR assessment suggests that Tanzania's financial
       management system i s among the best in Africa. Nonetheless, significant fiduciary risks
        in the areas of financial management and procurement remain.                In recent years,
        government has implemented measures to reduce fiduciary risks, including the approval
        and application o f new public finance and procurement acts, the full operationalization o f
       the Integrated Financial Management System, strengthening o f the National Audit Office,
       and reform o f the public procurement system. The PFMRP and actions monitored by the
       PRSC are designed to address weaknesses in government's financial management and
       procurement systems.

172.   To ensure that such risks do not derail the implementation o f the MKUKUTA and PRSC,
appropriate monitoring instruments have been put inplace. The government's annual MKUKUTA


                                                 49

implementationreports, which build on the MKUKUTAmonitoring system, and a well-established
annual PER mechanism, provide open and transparent arrangements for monitoring outcomes and
fiduciary risks. Moreover, the annual nature of the PRSC provides a framework for appraising
risksidentified inthe PEFARand agreeing onnecessarycorrective actions.

                                     RE: Letter of DeveloDment Policv

1.          On behalf of the Government of the United Republic of Tanzania, Ihereby write to
request for approval of the Fifth Poverty Reduction Support Credit (PRSC-5) in the
amount of US$190 million (SDR equivalent). This credit will assist in the implementation
of the National Strategy for Growth and Reduction of Poverty (MKUKUTA) and facilitate
the continued implementation of Government reforms including those in support of
macroeconomic stability, the private sector and rural development, infrastructure and
energy, health, education, and water sector reforms, public financial management
reforms, public service reform, governance, and environmental sustainability.

I. BACKGROUNDANDRECENTDEVELOPMENTS

      A.         MACROECONOMIC PERFORMANCE

2.          The economy performed well in 2005, with a GDP growth of 6.8 percent, despite
the experienced drought and the continued increase in fuel prices in the world markets,
compared with 6.7 percent recorded in 2004. The sectors that have shown strong growth
during the year include trade and tourism, manufacturing, mining, construction and
communication, which together contributed about 40 percent of the GDP. The drought
experienced during 2005 and 2006 adversely affected food production and hydropower
generation. These developments coupled with the persistent global increases in oil prices
in that period have exerted upward pressure on inflation. After having successfully
contained inflation at around 4 percent for the past three years, it began inching upward
to an average rate of 6.2 percent in 2006. Starting at 5.4 percent in January 2006,


                                                    52

inflation reached 7.7 percent in May 2006, then easing to 4.8 percent in August 2006
before swinging up again to 6.7''          percent by December, 2006. The inflation rate is
expected to come down again in 2007, owing in part to policy measures and good food
harvest during 2006 as well as a slight decrease in petroleum prices.

3.      As at the end of December 2006, gross international reserves stood at USD
2,260.1 million, equivalent to 5.3 months of imports of goods and services. This level
represents an increase of 14.8 percent from their level of USD 1,968.6 million at the end
of June 2005. With the high dependence on imported inputs by most productive sectors,
the impact of the continued depreciation of the local currency on the country's export
capacity is being closely monitored. The shilling depreciated by about 9.6 percent, from
an average of TShs 1126.3 per USD at the end of June 2005 to TShs 1234.2 per USD by
the end of December 2006. Credit to private sector remained high, rising from an
annual rate of 26.2 percent in June 2005 to 34.0 percent in December 2005 and reached
42.3 percent at the end of December 2006. The volume of outstanding credit to private
sector at end-December 2006 was TShs 2027.2 billion at end of December 2006. A new
Policy Support Instrument was negotiated with the IMF and, together with the sixth PRGF
review, was approved by the Fund's Executive Board in February, 2007. The Government
remains committed to pursuing sound macroeconomic policies, and ensuring sustainable
growth and reduction of poverty.



    8.      REVIEW OF TANZANIA'S POVERTY REDUCTION STRATEGY AND
            ADOPTION OF THE NATIONAL STRATEGY FOR GROWTH AND
            REDUCTIONOF POVERTY(MKUKUTA)

4.      Since the adoption of MKUKUTA in February 2005, substantial progress has been
made in developing the processes and policies to ensure the effective implementation of
the strategy in the period 2005-10. The Government has produced three extensive
national analytical reports, which provide information on changes in the three MKUKUTA
clusters of Growth and Reduction of Income Poverty; Improvement of Quality of Life and
Social Well-being; and Governance and Accountability. These are, the Poverty and Human
Development Report 2005, the Status Report 2006 on 86 MKUKUTA indicators, and the
draft MKUKUTA Annual Implementation Report 2005/06. The link between the budget
and MKUKUTA has been strengthened through the Strategic Budget Allocation System
(SBAS) being used by the Central Government to bid for, and obtain resource allocations.
The Government has continued with its effort to maintain macroeconomic stability,
improving the quality of budget formulation, budget implementation as well as the overall
expenditure discipline. Budget execution results in FY 2005/06 and the budget frame for
FY 2006/07 are consistent with the MKUKUTA. In particular, the Government has
maintained its focus on enhancing domestic resource mobilisation so as to gradually
reduce Tanzania's aid dependence. Development Partners are aligning their programmes
around the MKUKUTA through the Public Expenditure Review and General Budget
Support structures. The costing exercise for the MDGs and elements under the cluster


loThere was a revision of the methodology used to measure inflation in September 2006, leading
to a generally higher level of reported inflation by about 1.5 percentage points comparedto the old
series.


                                                  53

strategies is progressing well. A new Partnership Framework Memorandum signed in
January, 2006 between Development Partners and the Government includes a
PerformanceAssessment Framework that outlines the commitments under the MKUKUTA
and complementary national processes. The PRSC actions are all contained within this
framework.

5.      The Government is committed to further reforms under Cluster 1that will remove
bottlenecks to growth in the economy, particularly in agriculture, energy, infrastructure
and private sector development. Notable progress has been made in the social sectors
under Cluster 2 of the MKUKUTA, with considerable improvements in education, water,
and health. For example, the 2006 MKUKUTA status report notes remarkably good
performance of overall enrolment and retention of both girls and boys at primary
education level, and gains in addressing infant mortality, under-five mortality, and
malaria. Under Cluster 3, implementationof the Public Service Reform Programme (PSRP)
and the Local Government Reform Programme (LGRP) is progressing well. However,
there are challenges in fully implementing the Public Sector Pay Reform in view of the
significant fiscal implications associated with it. Also,          implementation of the
Decentralisation by Devolution (D-by-D) across Government is receiving renewed
impetus, with reallocation of budgetary resources from MDAs to LGAs in line with the
division of labour between the two levels of Government. In order to accelerate legal
sector and judicial reforms, the ownership of and participation in the implementation of
the Legal Sector Reform Programme (LSRP) is being broadened and the stakeholder
dialogue framework strengthened. Stakeholder dialogue on governance and the related
anti-corruption effort, including a review of the mechanisms for implementation of the
second National Anti-Corruption Strategy and Action Plan (NACSAP 11) are ongoing.

    C.      BUDGET SUPPORT AND ASSISTANCE FOR THE GOVERNMENT'S OWN
            REFORM PROCESS

6.      General Budget Support (GBS) is the Government's preferred aid modality because
of its advantages over other instruments in terms of Government ownership over
resource allocation, and its full integration in the national budget, public financial
management, and accountability systems. Project and basket funding modalities are
expected to be fully in line with national priorities, strategies and programmes, and rely
on Government structures for implementation. In December 2006, the Joint Assistance
Strategy (JAST) was signed.        The JAST aims to coordinate and consolidate the
Government's efforts and Development Partners' support under a single Government-led
framework to achieve the targeted results of the MKUKUTA and the MKUZA (the Zanzibar
Strategy for Growth and Reductionof Poverty). Development Partners' country assistance
strategies and bilateral agreements with the Government of Tanzania are expected to,
over time, be brought in line with the JAST. JAST includes all aspects of development co-
operation between the Government and Development Partners and incorporates the full
set of internationally agreed principles of aid effectiveness - national ownership and
Government leadership of the development process; alignment of Development Partner
support to national priorities and systems; harmonisation of Government and
Development Partner processes; managing resources for results in development and
poverty reduction; and domestic and mutual accountability. It thus translates into action,
Tanzania's commitment to international initiatives in aid effectiveness, most importantly


                                            54

the 2005 Paris Declaration on Aid Effectiveness.The PerformanceAssessment Framework
(PAF) introduced in 2006 aligns the budget support review within the existing processes
and fora as a means of minimising transaction costs and strengthening national public
policy processes.


11.     REFORM PROGRAMMEUNDERTHE PAF 2004/5                  -2005/6
7.     The JAST adopts all of the existing national MKUKUTA and GBS processes for
monitoring and evaluation. Under the Partnership Framework Memorandum governing
the PAF, an annual review is held to bring together sector and thematic reviews and
against the overall framework and a joint assessment of performance is produced. The
latest Annual Review was held in October 2006 and the rest of this section details the
relevant results and assessment.

    A.     INFRASTRUCTUREAND ENERGY

8.      Energy and transport are the crucial infrastructure that will facilitate accelerated
growth and remove bottlenecks in the economy. MKUKUTA acknowledgesthe importance
of physical infrastructure in stimulating economic growth and reducing income poverty.
The Government plans to marshal a resource mobilization drive to raise additional
resources, to address the identified impediments to sustainable and pro-poor growth
within the MKUKUTA framework, with primary focus on these sectors.

        PRSC- 6 Trigger:
        Transport Sector Investment Plan finalized with adequate provision for
        maintenanceand a framework for PPP.

9.      The Government has prepared the Medium Term Transportation Infrastructure
Investment Plan (TSIP) covering all sub-sectors and reforms in the sector, including Local
Government and cross-cutting issues. The preparation process was highly consultative
and the prioritised TSIP was discussed in February 2007 at a consultative forum with
Government and Development Partners. These consultations provided important inputs
to the process of further refinement of the TSIP, including prioritisation and alignment
with the Medium Term Expenditure Framework, including the preparation of a detailed
three year rolling plan 2007/08 to 2009/10 as part of the TSIP.            TANROADS has
continued to improve its performance, as reflected by its improved absorptive capacity,
maintenance performance, and road conditions. To consolidate the improvements, the
Government will organise a Stakeholders' Consultations Forum in March, 2007 and take
measures to strengthen TANROADS autonomy. The Government is committed to the
maintenanceof the existing road network and will, through the Roads Fund and increases
in the fuel levy to generate greater yield, explore financing options for increasing the
resource envelopefor this purpose.

        PRSC-5PriorAction:
        Preparation of a drae Road Bilb includng a road inventoy and Government
        approvalof the submissionof a new Road Bill to Parliament



                                            55

         PRSC- 6 Trigger:
         Roads Bill Submittedto Parliament

 10.     The submission of the Roads Bill to Parliament was delayed partly as a
.consequence of the restructuring of ministries and delays in the preparation of the road
 inventory. There is now a roadmap for the completion of the Roads Act with the intention
 of submitting the bill to Parliament in the March/April 2007 session for first reading. This
 Draft Bill includes an inventory of all roads. The second and final reading of the Bill is
 expected in the October/November 2007 session of the Parliament.

 11.     The combined occurrence of drought and high petrol prices throughout 2005 and
 2006 highlighted the vulnerabilities in the energy sector. The Government is committed to
 realising reform of the Energy Sector towards the accelerated implementation of
 sustainable strategies for reform, investment and good governance. Completion of the
 reform strategy is expected by April 2007, followed by the submission of the Electricity
 Bill to Parliament.

 12.     The Government's policy with regards to the power sector in the medium-term is
 based on the following pillars:

                 Ensure the sector makes an appropriate contribution to the country's
                 growth agenda through, among others, better quantity, quality, and
                 pricing of energy;

                 Support TANESCO in achieving financial sustainability in the medium term,
                 and operational financial viability as soon as possible;

                 Finance capital expenditures, with support from development partners, as
                 required by TANESCO to improve its service delivery to consumers;

                 Support interventions to expand rural access to electricity; and

                 Support enabling conditions to attract more private investment in the
                 sector, notably for power generation projects, to promote competition and
                 lower costs.

         PRSC- 6 Trigger:
         Progress in the implementation of the TANESCO Financial Recovery Plan
         (FRP) andstrengtheninggovernanceof thesector by improvedregulation.

 13.     To achieve these goals, the government will implement a comprehensive package
 of reforms, aimed both at expanding investments, increasing efficiency and quality of
 service, and ensuring operational viability of TANESCO.

 14.     The Government, with support from DPs will implement investments to improve
 the reliability of TANESCO's transmission and distribution network, and support grid
 extensions. To this extent, TANESCO has identified a US$ 1.3 billion investment program
 and donor support will be solicited for its financing.          Financing of investments in


                                              56

generation is expected to include the private sector as IPPs, And the Government will
ensure that an adequate and transparent framework compliant with the Public
Procurement Act, 2004 is in place for such projects. In addition, the Government will
provide policy guidance to EWURA to establish regulatory rules that would encourage
competitive procurement of new generation projects in line with the stated policy
objectives above. In this context, EWURA is working with the Bank on a two-phased
approach, first focused on the creation of standardised application and tables that would
allow the benchmarking of proposed Power Purchase Agreements (PPAs) and, secondly,
the creation of guidelines for competitive procurement and model PPAs.

15.    To improve the operational efficiency at TANESCO, the Government will also
support corporate independence of TANESCO by clarifying the respective roles of
TANESCO`s management, its Board of Directors, and the Ministry of Energy and Minerals.

16.    The recovery of TANESCO`s hydrogenation capacity and installation of leased
lower-cost gas plants should go a long way in addressing the utility's cash flow deficits.
Guided by the FRP, the Government will continue supporting a quick financial turnaround
in TANESCO finances as a mean to ensure reliable provision of electricity, and adequate
funding for operational maintenance and improving operational efficiencies. Government
policy in this regard includes a cap on budget transfers for operational requirements,
supporting use of market based financing instruments, and supporting TANESCO's
application for the regulator's approval of such tariff increase by July 2007 as is necessary
to close the operational deficit in 2007/08 (except for the current level of Government
support for capacity charges). Furthermore, planned investment in the transmission and
distribution infrastructure to reduce technical losses, and the buy down of Songas
expansion cost will lead to sizeable additional cost reduction for TANESCO and therefore
contribute to a quick recovery of its financials. As a result of the investment effort, of the
improved cash flow for TANESCO, and of efficiency improvements, it is targeted that the
implementation of the FRP will be achieved.


    8.     IMPROVED INVESTMENTAND BUSINESS ENVIRONMENT

17.    The objective of the BEST Programme is to lower the costs of investing ,
establishing and operating businesses in the country through (i) elimination of policy,
legal, regulatory and institutional constraints that inhibit a growing and competitive
private sector; and (ii) improving the quality of services provided by the Government to
the private sector including expeditious resolution of commercial conflict.

18.    The BEST Programme received extensive review as part of re-design that took
place to enable the World Bank join the four Development Partners who provided initial
support. The first annual review of the programme was held in September 2006, and
found progress to be satisfactory, especially as regards participation and dialogue.
However, there was noted a need to set realistic targets and accelerate the pace of
reforms by addressing institutional bottlenecks in the many areas covered under the
programme. The review was satisfied that adequate steps were planned and were being
implemented to address these bottlenecks.



                                             57

19.     The three main areas of reforms already underway under the programme are
review of the Civil Procedure Code (CPC); Business Registration legislation; and land
reforms. In view of the institutional context of commercial justice reform, the review of
Civil Procedure Code will take longer to accomplish than was initially anticipated. A first
reading in Parliament of the Business Registration Act (BAR) took place in July, 2005,
after which the Bill was revised to accommodate private sector views and was
subsequently passed by Parliament in February 2007. Related to this, an assessment of
regulatory licensing is also being implemented by the Ministry of Planning, Economy and
Empowerment (MPEE). Also relating to the business and investment environment, the
Strategic Plan for the Implementation of Land Laws (SPILL) has been accomplished and
the computerised land registry has been introduced in one district on a pilot scheme
model. Also relating to the business and investment environment, the Strategic Plan for
the Implementation of Land Laws (SPILL) has been accomplished and the computerised
land registry has been introduced in one district on a pilot scheme model.

        PRSC-5PriorAction
Progress in the reform of the business activities registration and the business regulatory
licensing regime, including reflectingprivate sector viewsin the redraeing of the business
activitiesregistration bill.

20.     Stakeholder discussions led to strong consensus for the establishment of a one-
stop business registration centre under the mandate of BREIA. There will be a simplified
registration system leading to a reduction in the number of days that it takes to register a
business; lower costs ofregistration measured as a percentage of GDP and a higher
number of businesses registered. In the long term this will lead to rapid formalization of
the economy as many Small and Medium-Sized Enterprises (SMEs) emerge from the
unofficial economy. Further policy measures are being developed to facilitate lending by
commercial banks to SMEs. At the same time, a number of banks have established SME
lending units to help entrepreneurs prepare bankable business plans. Overall, during the
GBS Annual Review, it was noted that progress in relation to improved enabling
environment for private sector growth improved was satisfactory, as also reflected by
Tanzania's improved ranking in the World Bank's ease of doing business table,         from
150th in 2005 to 142nd in 2006.


    C.     AGRICULTURAL AND RURAL DEVELOPMENT

21.     The Government is committed to comprehensive reforms in the agriculture sector
to promote productivity and contribute to poverty reduction; the majority of Tanzanians
depend on agriculture. The overall Agriculture Sector Review is still in progress in 2007
and as such there was no final assessment at the GBS Annual Review. The inception
report was presented to stakeholders in the sector and a draft report presented to the
Agricultural Sector Reference Group. This has now been established as an annual review
process.




                                            58

        PRSC-5PriorAction:
        Consultationson implementation of reforms with four crop Boards completedand
        MOUssigned

        PRSC6 Trigger:
        DraR amendments to crop board legislation submitted for stakeholder
        consultationsandstrategicplans for all crop boards finalked.

22.     Progress has also been recorded in the restructuring of Crop Boards after the
issuance of the Ministerial Circular on the Reform of Crop Boards in March 2006. The
process adapted involves considerable consultation with the different stakeholders in each
sub-sector. The aim was to amend all Crop Board Legislations based on the crop board
reform findings. As a result, Memoranda of Understanding for each crop were prepared,
including how to operationalise the Government Circular after the abolition of the Crop
Development Levy in July 2006. Short and long term measures on how to finance shared
functions for the development of individual crops were identified and Actions Plans
prepared. In the coffee, cotton, sugar and tobacco sub-sectors, MOUs have been signed
and reports prepared, and consultations continue for tea, pyrethrum and cashews. The
crop levies have been abolished and one hundred percent funding mechanisms for crop
boards are underway for the new administrative, regulatory and research functions.
Annual license fees have also been abolished and compliance is being monitored. In the
coffee sector, reforms also include the removal of the blind auction and single license
requirements. A Legal Consultant has been hired to review the Acts establishing the
Boards. In January 2007 a budget was approved to facilitate consultative activities to
draw up strategic plans, strategies and specific amendments to legislation for each board.
There is a clear timetable and a commitment by Government to continue on this complex
path as quickly and as carefully as possible. The Government expects that the draft
legislations will be subjected to stakeholder consultations by the middle of 2007, and that
amendment of all Crop Boards legislations could be sent to Parliament by
October/November 2007, but not later than February 2008.            Crop Boards are also
preparing strategic plans which would reflect the implementation arrangement of reforms
and serve as the basis for budget submissions. Strategic crop development plans which
are consistent with and integrated into the ASDP will be prepared by April 2007. Also in
this sector, Special Studies on Strategic Grain Reserves have been completed and will be
made available to the public. The process to establish a Strategic Grain Reserve Agency,
which is part of the findings, has started.        Studies on fertiliser subsidy, and the
Agricultural Inputs Trust Fund will be finalised by [October, 20071 and the reports will be
made available to the public,


    D.      MACROECON0MI C STABILITY

23.     Tanzania's sustained economic reforms have generated strong macroeconomic
performance over the past decade, characterised by accelerating GDP growth and low
inflation. The Government continues to emphasize fiscal and debt sustainability as key
areas of economic reforms. In view of the adverse effects of weather on agriculture and
hydropower generation and increasing petroleum prices, economic growth for 2006 is
estimated to have been 5.8 percent, close to the earlier projected level of 5.9 percent but


                                             59

below the previous year's performance of 6.8 percent. Notwithstanding the drought
related decline in the growth of the agriculture sector, it remained the highest overall
contributor to GDP, while the electricity sub-sector is expected to have registered
negative growth; in effect pulling down the growth of the Water and Electricity sector
along the same direction. With the completion of heavy investments in the mining sector,
production is stabilising with a tapering-off in the growth rate of the sector projected at
11.0 percent in 2006 compared to 15.7 percent attained in 2005. Growth of the
manufacturing sector is expected to have been adversely affected by the power load
shedding experienced during most of 2006, and projected at 6.5 percent compared with
actual growth of 9.0 percent registered in 2005.

24.    Ongoing reforms in the area of domestic resource mobilisation have continued to
generate encouraging results, with domestic revenue rising from 13.3 percent of GDP in
2004/5 to a projected 14.5 percent of GDP for 2006/07. However, the level of domestic
revenue is still very low relative to financing needs (reflecting in part the unfavourable
structure of the economy), and the Government will continue to pursue prudent policies
to sustain recent performance in this area. To address the significant financing gap for
implementing MKUKUTA, and reduce aid dependency in the medium to long term, both
domestic and external resources need to be increased. In this regard, priority is given to
implementation of TRA's Second Corporate Plan that seeks to achieve a modern tax
administration by 2008; through the multi-donor supported Tax Modernization
Programme (TMP). Recent initiatives include building an integrated approach to the
management of taxes, harmonisation of tax laws and enhancement of taxpayer services.
The potential for increasing revenue collection from non-tax revenue sources will also be
explored during 2007 through a PER study for this purpose. Arrangements for co-
operation with Local Government Authorities (LGAs) to assist them in the administration
of taxes and identify areas of co-operation such as common tax bases, unification of
taxes, and the elimination of nuisance taxes have been initiated following the joint
development of the terms of reference for areas of cooperation.

25.    Total national debt stock at end December 2006 stood at TSh 9,069.6 billion. TSh
7,252.9 billion was external and TSh 1,816.7 billion was domestic. The MDRI relief from
the multilateral financial institutions will enhance further Tanzania's debt sustainability
position. Nevertheless, the Government will continue to seek debt relief on the HIPC
terms from the remaining creditors. Our actions in debt management will continue to be
guided by the National Debt Strategy (1999) and the Loan, Guarantees and Grants Act,
1974 as amended in 2003, which include, among others, centralisation of borrowing
powers in the Minister for Finance and a requirement for all new external borrowing to be
from concessional sources only. Considering the potential borrowing by private sector,
Tanzania will adhere to prudent debt management and will ensure that it does not over
borrow and return to debt trap. Guarantees will be critically scrutinized and private sector
borrowing will be monitored.

    E.     IMPROVEMENT I N THE EFFECTIVENESS OF DELIVERY OF PUBLIC
           SERVICES

26.    The Government has continued with its effort to maintain macroeconomic stability,
improving the quality of budget formulation, budget implementation as well as the overall


                                             60

expenditure discipline. The underlying processes for this area include the Budget
Guidelines, the Public Expenditure and Financial Accountability Review (PEFAR), Poverty
Reduction and Growth Facility (PRGF) reviews, Annual MKUKUTA Progress Report, and
quarterly Budget Execution Reports; with the main avenue for discussing issues from
these underlying processes being the PER Macro Group.

27.     Budget execution in 2005/06 was broadly in line with the budget targets.
Domestic revenues to the end of June 2006 were 3 percent above projections, mainly due
to better than expected performance of import duties, corporation tax, and domestic
excises. The impressive performance in domestic revenue continued in the first half of
fiscal year 2006/07, with actual performance 10 percent above estimates for the period
and 31 percent higher compared to collections for the corresponding period of last year,
with import duties, income tax and domestic VAT continuing to demonstrate strong
performance  .

    PRSC-5 PriorAction:
    Expenditureoutturn for W06consistentwithapprovedbudget.

    PRSC-6 Trigger:
    Expenditure outturn for FY07 consistent with approved budget.

28.     Government expenditure in 2005/06 was 96 percent of the estimated level due to
a cautious stance adopted in budget execution, and delays in the hiring of new staff and
in the implementation of some development projects. Expenditureon the goods, services
and transfers category was also contained within programme targets. Expenditure for
priority social services and for activities linked to the achievement of the Millennium
Development Goals (MDGs) was protected despite the drought and power load shedding.
Domestic financing of the budget for the year was TShs 349 billion, compared with the
budget of TShs 373.9 billion. Budget implementation as marked by budget outturn
showed an improvement on the baselinefigure.

29.     In the approved budget for 2006/07, revenues are expected to be 14.4 percent of
GDP; reflecting a 16 percent annual increase primarily on account of policy measures
announced in the budget for 2006/07 and continued tax and customs administration
reforms, and including a cautionary discount for a possible effect of power load shedding.
The revenue measures announced in the budget for 2006/07 are expected to contribute
0.7 percent of GDP to revenues. In undertaking initiatives to increase domestic revenue,
the Government recognisesthe need to maintain the hard earned stability of the business
climate.

    PRSC-5PriorAction:
    Approved budget for W07in fine withpolicy objectives(MKUKUTA)

    PRSC-6 Trigger:
    Approved budget for FY08 in line with policy objectives (MKUKUTA, sector policies).

30.     The Government has further strengthened the link between budgetary resource
allocation and MKUKUTA priorities through cluster-based expenditure programming, and


                                            61

rolling out the Strategic Budget Allocation System (SBAS) to the Regional Secretariats in
addition to MDAs that were already using the system. The completion of the ongoing
MKUKUTA costing exercise will strengthen further the Government's effort to prioritise
and link the resource allocation with MKUKUTA. The costing of MKUKUTA interventions
for roads, water, health and agriculture is already at an advanced stage and are providing
input to the 2007/08 budget cycle.

31.     The Government has put great effort into linking resource allocation with
MKUKUTA at the Budget Guidelines and the budget preparation stages through SBAS.
The Government is work towards including SBAS based analysis in the Budget Execution
Reports. The MKUKUTA Implementation Reports will include the growth and poverty
trends as well as an update on MKUKUTA monitoring. An initiative has been undertaken
to provide electronic reporting by MDAs through the use of a software known as `Ripoti
ya Uteke/ezaji wa MKUKUTA" (RIMKU). RIMKU is compatible with SBAS and will obtain
financial inputs from SBAS, while other non-financial MKUKUTA implementation status will
be entered in the system by MDAs themselves. Further, the Government is in the process
of establishing an ExpenditureTracking and Monitoring Unit at the Ministry of Finance, in
order tO enhance the efficiency in resource utilisation. Meanwhile LGAs are being
increasingly involved in the budget formulation process in order to strengthen ownership
at the local level. The initiatives implemented for this purpose include the representation
of LGAs in the Budget Guidelines Committee through PMO-R4LG.

32.     The Budget Guidelines for 2006/07 were issued in March 2006 instead of January
2006, owing to the need to incorporate the requirements of a new Government structure
that was to be brought on board following the 2005 General Elections. In order to have
an effective MTEF planning framework, there is a need to improve the medium term
predictability of the external financing component of the budget. Recent analysis of the
planning and budgeting system and the PER (PEFAR 2006) has highlighted both the
differing quality of PER analysis across the sectors and the clusters. The Budget
Guidelines process for FY 2007/8 has involved capacity building of MDAs in using MTEF
and in developing mechanismsfor feedback of budget execution into planning processes.
The PER/GBS structuresare also being strengthenedto provide more analytical input.


     F.     IMPROVED PERFORMANCE OF THE PUBLIC SECTOR AND THE
            OVERALL INCENTIVE ENVIRONMENT

33.     The Government had made substantial progress in the area of public sector
reform by implementing a comprehensive Public Service Reform Programme (PSRP) with
the aim of improving public service delivery and contribute to economic growth and
poverty reduction. The reform strategy has continued to focus on attracting and retaining
better qualified staff, strengthening human resource capacity and sustaining the overall
Government reform agenda, consistent with MKUKUTA implementation priorities.
Consistent with the Medium Term Pay Reform targets, and new recruitment requirements
to implement MKUKUTA priorities, outlays for wages during 2006/07 were increased by
1.6 percent of GDP, to a projected 5.9 percent of GDP. The additional resources are to
finance a 15 to 50 percent general wage increase for Government employees in
professional grades with scarce skills in order to improve retention of staff in critical areas


                                             62

of the public service. In addition, the increase is to be in part applied towards recruitment
of 24,689 new staff in priority areas, including front line health workers, teachers,
lawyers, accountants and internal auditors. By end 2006 about 10,595 new employees
have been enrolled. The overall impact of this measure to the budget is partially
mitigated by reductions in the allocations for employment allowances that are not part of
the wage bill, as well as some other allocations for the running of the Government,
including motor vehicle running expenses. A comprehensive review of the MTPP has been
undertaken by the Presidential Commission on Public Service Pay, with a view to examine
options to accelerate the implementation of the pay policy by implementing the policy in
relation to the employment levels for each MDA and their budgetary implications. The
recommendations for an improved implementation plan in the longer term would be
considered in the 2007/08 budget.


    G.      MINIMISATION OF RESOURCE LEAKAGESAND THE STRENGTHENING
            OF ACCOUNTABILITY

34.     Adequate resources have been provided during 2006/07 to ensure efficient and
effective implementation of the governance agenda, and will also be reflected in the
2007/08 and subsequent budgets for this purpose. In addition, this effort to minimise
resource leakages and strengthen accountability will be assisted by implantation of the
proposedAnti-Corruption and the Audit legislations.

        PRSC-6 Trigger:
        SubmissionofAnti-corruptionBill to Parliament

35.     The Bill was tabled for first reading in Parliament in February 2007. Meanwhilethe
Government has pursued vigorously its anticorruption agenda with declarations against
corruption continuing to be a common feature of political statements. During a two-day
workshop on integrity, stakeholders were invited to discuss the issue of corruption openly
with the Minister of State for Good Governance and other organs of Government. The
second National Anti-Corruption Strategy and Action Plan (NACSAP 11) outlining
interventions at both the Central and Local Government levels as well as the private
sector has been finalised and launched. MDAs are already preparing their Anti-Corruption
Plans in line with the thrust of the new NACSAP. With the support of the World Bank and
other development partners, the Government is also scaling up its efforts to implement its
comprehensive Legal Sector Reform Programme and NACSAP. In addition, the
Government is building the capacity of oversight and watchdog institutions as well as the
media and civic society as part of its efforts to increase their effectiveness in ensuring
Government accountability and therefore act as a deterrent to corruption. Other actions
against corruption include strengthening of the procurement function in public institutions
by developing the procurement cadre, and capacity building in the Public Procurement
Regulatory Authority (PPRA), spending agencies, and civil society especially the media to
report corruption incidents professionally and accurately.

36.     An Anti-Money Laundering Bill, submitted to Parliament for first reading in July
2006, was approved in November 2006 and implementation initiated. One of the key




                                              63

elements of this legislation is the establishment of a Financial Intelligence Unit (FIU) to
monitor and report questionable financial transactions to relevant authorities.

37.     Progress has been made in ensuring that the National Audit Office (NAO) has
suitable qualified staff and equipment in order to meet NAO's important objective of
developing appropriate capacity to carry out computer based audits of Government
accounts maintained in the IFMS. In this regard, staff has already been trained and
procurement and installation of computer equipment at the NAO completed for auditing
computerised accounts of revenue collection at TRA. With regard to the audit of
Government expenditure accounts, staff training was undertaken in April and May 2006,
and computers have been procured and installed. This is expected to facilitate transition
to an audit through IFMS in the near future. The NAO has also continued with its staff
training program to strengthen audit capacity.

         PRSC-5PriorAction:
         NationalAudit Ofice GeneralReportfor Fy05issuedby April 2006.

         PRSC-6 Trigger:
        Audit Bill Submittedto Parliamentby November2007

38.      Reflecting the impact of recent capacity building initiatives, the National Audit
Office (NAO) produced the 2004/05 Annual Audit Report in a timely manner (March
2006), and had the report tabled for discussion in Parliament before the June 2006
budget session. In order to further strengthen the the autonomy and effectiveness of
NAO, the Government is preparing for submission to Parliament by November 2007 a new
Audit Bill.
39.      Implementation of the Public Financial Management Reform Programme was
improved by remedying previous procurement planning bottlenecks. Improvements in the
production of audit and accounts reports were also noted. An external review of the
PFMRP was carried out, and the Government is revising the PFMRP Strategic Plan to
reflect the key findings. While this review is underway, a short term results focused PFM
action plan is being developed for the next financial year.            The Programme is
incorporated in the strategic plans of the Ministry of Finance (MOF) and reporting will be
improved.

40.      Overall progress within the Local Government Reform Programme is satisfactory,
although there are differences between the different outcome areas. Restructuring and
implementation of Decentralisation by Devolution is underway, and being accelerated
through planned further devolution of resources to LGAs in the 2007/08 budget. Recent
progress includes the continued successful roll-out of IFMS and the accompanying
training to LGAs. More and more LGAs are qualifying to benefit from capital development
grants. This improvement is also reflected in the number of LGAs receiving clean audits
from the Auditor General. The recent posting of 489 accountants and auditors to LGAs
will further enhance these developments. In June 2006, a road map to guide the `Dby D'
process was developed and addresses mainly attitudinal, institutional and legal aspects.
An evaluation of the assessment process will be carried out this year to ensure that




                                             64

appropriate standards for assessment are being maintained. LGAs are still faced with
multiple reporting systems on the Development Partner side.

41.     In relation to Zanzibar, an assessment of the public financial management
situation was made and suggestions for improvements formulated. The Revolutionary
Government of Zanzibar (RGoZ) reacted very positively to the recommendations and is
now in the process of preparing a detailed Action Plan.


     H.    ENVIRONMENTALSUSTAINABILITY

42.     Implementation of the Environment Management Act commenced with the
translation of the legislation into Kiswahili to facilitate dissemination and public education,
issuance of two sets of Regulations, and initiating the drafting of a further six. In
addition, MDAs' capacity needs assessment has been undertaken and six MDAs have
established Environmental Sections. A draft State of Environment Report was produced in
December 2006, and a strategy for urgent action on land degradation and water
catchment areas is under implegentation. Planned next steps in this area include building
awareness in MDAs and LGAs in relation to the Environment Management Act and
selection of an appropriate national outcome indicator for environment.


     I. SECTORREVIEWS

43.     Health
The seventh joint Health Sector Review took place in 2006, as did the costing exercise for
this sector of MKUKUTA. The review highlighted impressive gains in addressing infant
mortality, under-five mortality, and malaria. In contrast, progress in maternal health,
nutrition and reproductive health over the period has been limited. Increasing the
demand for and access to contraceptives, investing in emergency obstetric care, and
strengthening multi-sectoral action in nutrition (health, education and agriculture) will be
critical to realizing progress. Despite the adoption of the broad-based MKUKUTA,
budgetary allocations to the health sector continue to be protected, in order to protect
the gains already achieved. The financing of health services, however, faces several
challenges including inadequate resources and heavy reliance on large earmarked
external (often vertical) funds.

        PRSC-5PriorAction:
        Satisfactoryhealth sector review carriedout (in2006)

        PRSC-6 Trigger:
        Satisfactoryhealthsector review carriedout (in2007)

        Education
44.     The Education Sector Review took place in early 2006 and informed plans of
Ministries, Departments and Agencies (MDAs) for the sub-sectors of primary, secondary,
vocational and tertiary education within this sector.




                                               65

       PRSC-5PriorAction:
       Satisfactoryeducationsector review carriedout (in 2006)

       PRSC-6 Trigger:
       Satisfactoryeducationsector review carriedout (in 2007)

45.    Remarkably good performance continues to be observed in relation to overall
enrolment of both girls and boys at primary level. This review noted a number of
challenges, including the shortage of qualified teachers (especially in rural areas), and the
need to improve quality of primary education through provision of teaching and learning
materials. Drop-out rates in primary education also remain a challenge and there is need
for a multi-sector approach to this particular issue. The review also highlighted the
increasing demand for secondary education, and the need to expand gross enrolment in
higher education institutions in the near future. The sector is currently implementing the
recommendations from the review which were agreed through the Education Sector
ReviewAide Memoire approved at a high-level meeting in June 2006.

       Water
46.    The first joint Water Sector Review was conducted in mid 2006, and steps were
taken towards revised legislationand improving the sector strategy with stakeholders.

       PRSC-5PriorAction:
       Satisfactorywatersector review carriedout (in 2006)

       PRSC-6 Trigger:
       Satisfactorywatersector review carried out (in 2007)

47.    This review led to the formation of Thematic Groups in Performance Monitoring,
Planning and Finance; Institutional Development and Capacity Building; and Sanitation
and Hygiene. The review was supplemented by a draft National Water Sector
Development Strategy and Programme, preparation of revised legislation (to be tabled in
Parliament in early 2007) for the sector, and costing of the water sector within the
MKUKUTA. The sector review highlighted the need to improve coordination to include
allied Ministries such as PMO-RALG and Ministry of Health, as well as the civil society and
the private sector. Steps are needed to accelerate capacity building in local authorities
and private sector suppliers. The Water Sector Development Programme financing plan is
also being aligned with the MTEF. In view of the varied frequency of some indicators, the
stakeholders will address the choice of the most appropriate indicators for monitoring
progress in this area. National surveys are consideredthe most reliable source of data in
relation to household access to safe and clean water. The Ministry's routine data system,
implemented in collaboration with local authorities is based on service points (not
households) and requires improvements. The baseline in relation to household access
comes from the routine data source, yet the next figures from national surveys will come
from the ILFS 2006 (data available by mid 2007) and the HBS 2007 (data available in mid
2008).




                                             66

        HIV/AIDS
48.     The second bi-annual HIV/AIDS programme review took place in March 2006,e
with a strong concensus among stakeholders on the satisfactory progress against
milestones in HIV/AIDS control programme, including increased public awareness, high
level commitment, mobilisation of the private sector, and mainstreaming into the national
budget. During the past year, Council Multi-Sectoral Aids Committees (CMAC) and
Regional Facilitating Agencies were established in LGAs and Regional Secretariats
respectively

        Social Protection Framework
49      In consultation with a wide range of diverse stakeholders working in this area, we
have initiated a process towards an action plan for developing a national social protection
framework. The next steps in this regard will include agreement on institutional
coordination, and defining social protection in the local context


   J.       MILESTONESFORTHE ADVANCEMENTOF MKUKUTA

the implementation of MKUKUTA are reflected in the PRSC-6 triggers which include:

   ix.      Satisfactory education sector review;

   X.       Satisfactory water sector review;

   xi.      Submission of Anti-corruption Bill to Parliament;




                                             67

111. CONCLUSION

51.     The Government is committed to continue to implement sound policies to sustain
good performance, continue reform processes and consolidate the achievements under
the PRSC programme to date. The Government reaffirms its commitment to the
integrated implementation of the three cluster strategies of MKUKUTA, which sets
economic growth, social well being of all Tanzanians and good governance and
accountability as the pillars of the development agenda in Tanzania. The Government
respects the imperative of pursuing consistent and appropriate monetary and fiscal
policies, complementing key structural reforms essential for achieving the MKUKUTA
goals. The progress in implementing policy actions laid in the PAF for PRSC 5 Prior
Actions, and other policy actions in PAF for the coming year, will continue to guide the
Government in policy reforms. It is against this background that the Government is
requesting the International Development Association to approve the provision of US$190
million to support the Government's efforts in sustaining and continuing to implement the
reforms.

52.     Thank you for your cooperation and timely support.

                                      Yours sincerely,
                                        n
                                    / Grays,Mgonja

CLUSTERI GROWTHAND REDUCTIONOF POVERTY
               -

Broad outcome:
Broad based and equitable growth is achievedand sustained

Goal 1:Ensuringsound economic management
1.1 Maintained macro-economic stability: inflation rate at maximum of 4 percent, maintaining
official reserves of at least six months of imports, achieving debt sustainability (containing the
external debt to GDP ratio at 50 percent or less).
1.2 Reduced unemployment from 12.9 percent in 2000/01 to 6.9 percent by 2010 and address
underemployment inrural areas.

Goal 2: Promotingsustainableand broad-basedgrowth
2.1 Accelerated GDP growth rate to attain a growth rate of 6-8 percent per annum by 2010.
2.2 Scaled up participationof the informal sector and SMEs (includingcooperatives).
2.3 Increased growth of manufacturingsector from 8.6 percent to 15 percent by 2010.
2.4 Increased agricultural growth from 5 percent in2002/03 to 10percent by 2010.
2.5 Increased growth rate for livestock sub sector from 2.7 percent in2000/01to 9 percent by
2010.
2.6 Increased technologicalinnovation, upgradingand use of technologies.
2.7 Promoted regional equity in development and capacity for growth and infrastructure that
enables investment and livelihoods diversification.
2.8 Repaired 15,000 kmof rural roads annually by 2010 from 4,500 kmin2003.
2.9 Reducednegative impacts on environment and peoples' livelihoods.
2.10 Reducedland degradation and loss of biodiversity.
2.11 Increased export proportion of value added minerals from the current 0.5 percent to 3.0
percent by 2010.

Goal 3: Improving food availability and accessibility inurban and rural areas
3.1 Increased food crops production from 9 million in2003/04 tons to 12million in2010.
3.2 Maintained Strategic Grain Reserveof at least 4 month of national food requirement.

Goal 4: Reducingincome povertyof menand women inrural areas

from 38.6% in2000101to 24% in2010.

2010.

4.5 Securedand facilitated marketingof agricultural products.

6.6%.
