                                        1.INTRODUCTION

The Government of Tanzania, in close collaboration with education stakeholders including Civil
Society Organizations, Non-State Actors and Development Partners supporting education, resolved to
carry out the Education Sector Review in 2005. Following the political and democratic processes o f
the general elections, the appointment o f new Government Ministers and changes inMinistries' Chief
Executives, the review was postponed to 23rd - 25th February 2006. The review processes were
coordinated by the Prime Ministers Office (PMO) under the leadership o f the Education Sector Inter-
Ministerial Steering Committee (IMSC). The IMSC was technically advised by the Education Sector
Advisory Committee (ESAC). ESAC considered inputs fiom the Basic Education Development
Committee (BEDC), the Vocational Education and Folk Development Committee (VEFDC), Tertiary
and Higher Education Education Development Committee (THEDC) and the Public Expenditure
Review (PER) Working Group. The Education Sector Review (ESR) also built on earlier reviews o f
PEDP (2003 and 2004) and the appraisal o f SEDP (2004) and other policy development work carried
out inrecent years for other education sub-sectors.


The general objective o f the ESR i s to assess the overall performance o f the sector in the context o f
the National Strategy for Growth and Reduction o f Poverty (NSGRPNKUKUTA), Education for All
(EFA) targets, Millennium Development Goals (MDGs) and other sectors' policies related to
education. The importance o f education in national development i s seen as a strategic agent for
knowledge and skills acquisition, and for mindset transformation, as reflected inthe priority given to
the sector in the Tanzania Development Vision 2025. This Education Sector Review was the first o f
i t s hnd as far as the holistic including all sub sectors approach i s concerned. This Review therefore
                                -                         -
could not yet provide an assessment o f overall education performance on the basis o f `agreed results
and targets', one o f the criteria for a `satisfactory review' within the Partnership Framework
Memorandum (PFM) governing General Budget Support (GBS) but provided an opportunity to set the
stage for the development o f a full sector strategy. The Review made an attempt to provide some first
strategic benchmarks in order to be able to measure satisfactory progress inthe sector as a whole at a
later stage.




                                           2. OBJECTIVES

The objective o f the Aide-Memoire i s to provide key education sector stakeholders with a synopsis o f
the situation obtaining and issues arising fiom the four preparatory studies and discussions inthematic
groups. Four discussion papers (see chapter 3) were the basis o f the discussions and recommendations
adopted at the review. Itwill define the sector's strengths and challenges and form an agenda that will
provide a work plan and a basis for dialogue and cooperation between DPs, CSOs and NSAs and
Government. Finally, the Aide-Memoire includes a work plan towards the realisation o f a
comprehensive Education Sector DevelopmentProgramme.




                                                    186

                               2.1 THESPECIFICOBJECTIVESOF THE ESR

                  THESPECIFICOBJECTIVESOF THEESRINCLUDE THEFOLLOWING.'

o To provide a deeper understanding o f the critical linkages between the various sub-sectors'
    policies, strategies, plans, laws and regulations
o To identify major achievements and gaps, issues and challenges that pose a threat to bringing
    about desired changes inthe sector
o To focus on the problems and implications o f planning and budgeting for the sector as a whole
    and to formulate recommendations
o To discuss how all stakeholders can contributeto effective policy dialogue with government
o To discuss how progress in the sector can be more effectively monitored in line with
    MKUKUTA's outcomes-based approach
o To provide inputs that will guide the process towards an integrated education sector policy
    guideline and strategic plan
o To produce an Aide-Memoire, including a work plan(with milestones) towards revision o fESDP,
    to enhance a full education sector strategic plan, short, medium and long-term.




       3. SUMMARY OFISSUESRAISEDBY THEREVIEWSTUDIESAND
                   THEMATICGROUPDISCUSSIONSAND REPORTS

Four studies were carried out to provide necessary background for the review discussions.
These are:-
    @   The Education Sector Situation Analysis: This explains the situation obtaining and identifies
        gaps as well as critical issues both across the sector as a whole and at sub-sector level. It
        makes specific recommendations on key challenges.

        The Education Sector Public Expenditure Review: This assesses the sector's costing,
        prioritization, budget formulation, financing, execution and monitoring, and the
        relationship with strategic plans and needs o fthe sector.

    0   The Education Sector Performance Assessment Framework: This looks at ways o f assessing
        the sector in all its aspects: financing, quality, management, etc - so that progress can
        be measured over time to enhance value for money and accountability.

        Exploring more Effective Dialogue in the Education Sector: This considers structured
        engagement for effective and efficient partnership among the education sector stakeholders,
        capacity, how accountability and the communication with the Government o f Tanzania
        could be enhanced.
Key issues identified inthe four major studies, insubsequent thematic group work and papers
presented, and are provided hereunder:-
                          3.1. THEEDUCATION      SECTOR SITUATION      ANALYSIS

This study notes the very significantprogress made inthe last five years, notably inthe primary sub-
sector, which has been the major emphasis under PEDP. There has been increased fundingresulting
inimpressive gains inconstruction anddistribution ofschools, enrolment andteacher supply.

A few key statistics on the sector at the time o fthis review are as follows:

                                                   187

        Pre-primary:
        The estimated population o f 5-6 year olds in2005 was 2,191,300.
        The number o f children enrolled inpre-primary schools was 638,591, Gross Enrolment Rate was 29.1%
        and the Net Enrolment Rate 27.7%.
        The Education and Training Policy 1995 states that pre-school is for 5-6 year olds,
        The Day-care Centres Act 1981provides for 2-6 year old children;

        Primary:
        In2005, there were 14,257 primary schools with7,541,208 students; aGross Enrolment Rate of 109.9%
        and a Net Enrolment Rate o f 94.8%; National average o f Book-Pupil Ratio o f 1:3 and Pass Rate o f 62%.

        Secondary:
        In 2005 there were 489,942 students in 0 level Secondary schools with a Gross Enrolment Rate of
        15.9% and Net Enrolment Rate o f 10.3%. Secondary education provision in Tanzania i s one o f the
        lowest inSub-SaharanAfrica and constitutes only about 7.6% o f the Secondary school age group. There
        i s considerable shortage o f diploma and graduate teachers.

        NonFormalandAdult Education:
        About 480,000 school-age children are enrolled in NFE centres, and about 500,000 students are
        enrolled in community based adult education. A variety o f programmes have been developed and
        pilotedbut have not been scaled up.
        There are many small programmes which are not focused to the MKUKUTA target of ensuring that at
        least 80% o f adults, especially women in rural areas, are literate and that there i s a reduction o f
        illiterates from 3.8 million to 1.5 millionby 2007/8.

        FolkandVocationalEducation andTraining:
        The VET sub-sector inprinciple covers all forms oforganised vocational education andtraining. It has a
        diverse set o f providers and institutions. There are 764 centres (of which 21 RVTSC, owned by VETA),
        with a total of 77,051 students and 58 colleges with 24,708 students in2004. Recently VET has been
        merged within the Ministry o f Education and Vocational Training (MoEVT). Another Ministry o f
        Community Development Gender and Children (MCDGC) manages Folk Education with 58 Folk
        Development Colleges (FDC's) and 4 special Community Development Training Institutes (CDTI's).
        Both Folk-education and VET have inadequate capacity and capital to address all interested students,
        butmany institutes show low utilisation rates andneedamore market oriented approach.

        Tertiary and Higher Education:
        Higher education i s offered by 8 public and 13 private universities. Tertiary education is offered by 4
        technical colleges and 18 tertiary education institutions. There are 160 institutions providing certificate
        level o f instruction, with low enrolment, scattered all over the country, spread over 15 different
        ministries.
        The total enrolment is 48,236. Overall, the number o f students inUniversities and tertiary institutions is
        very small with a Gross Enrolment Rate o f 0.7.


3.1.1. General Challenges

Despite improvements in the primary education sub-sector, the Situation Analysis study identifies
some priority challenges across a range o f areas. Although sub-sector variations can be identified in
the nature and magnitude o f sector challenges, at all levels and in all sub sectors there i s a need for
long-term resource mobilization, awareness-raising, improved institutional linkages, more systematic
capacity building, policy consultations and operational actions in order to enable progress in the
sector as a whole. The challenges are recognized and need to be addressed as they continue to
undermine full realization o f sector goals and objectives. The key challenges include:

        A need for integrated, more coherent full sector planning to avoid the fragmentation of the
        sub-sectors. Sector-wide planning and budgeting need a                       strong relationship with
        MKUKUTA, a rationalization and adaptation of institutional arrangements, the development

                                                        188

        o f a sector Performance Assessment Framework (PAF) and the revitalization o f the ESDP
        communication structure.
        The development, revision or updating o f sub sector planning and prioritizing (including
        costing) within this global reform o f the education sector.

3.1.2. ChallengesinAccess and Equity

In order to achieve the Education for All and pro-poor MKUKUTA goals these challenges are
                                             -                              -
considered o f great concern and would need constant attention. The critical challenges mentioned are:

        The provision o f childcare, pre-school and pre primary services

        Access for children from poor families, orphans, children with disabilities (including
        development and use o f Tanzanian Sign Language (TSL) for deaf children as well as training
        and use o f TSL teachers and interpreters) and other vulnerable children

        Achieving a sufficient number o f classrooms, toilets (with adequate water supply), teachers'
        houses, staff rooms and desks, especially at pre-primary level, primary, secondary schools and
        colleges,

        Improving living and working conditions for teachers, especially inremote areas,

        Recruiting and retaining teachers for inclusive education (special needs) and vocational
        colleges,

        Addressing the acute shortage o f teachers at all levels of education

        ,Retainingand motivating competent teachers in (public) schools, rural and A-level

        Rationalizing tertiary education and reassessing small institutions that are uneconomical to
        run

        Obtaining reliable and consistent data on student enrolments, staffing, examination results,
        repetition and dropout outside the universities

        Usingmore efficiently spare capacity insome tertiary and higher learning institutions

        Improving mechanisms for students' loans and bursaries

        Providing financial support to needy students at all levels.

3.1.3. Challengesin Quality

Enrolled students have a right to good quality and relevant education. Quality o f education forms an
inseparable part o f Education for All. Quality has many aspects; teaching and learning, learning
environment and learning outcomes. The sector analysis identified several challenges:

        Improving the learning environment inmost education institutions at all levels

        Upgrading the skills o f untrained and under-qualified teachers at pre-school and pre-primary
        level and in primary and secondary schools and teachers' colleges and folk and vocational
        colleges (especially inlanguages, arithmetic and sciences) and ensuring appropriate support,

        Procuring appropriate teaching and learning materials at all levels including relevant
        textbooks, reference and supplementary books,

        Expanding use o f I C T ineducation institutions,

        Helping new teachers by fitting their (in-service) training to new curriculum demands, and by
        improved supervision and mentorship duringteaching practice and probation


                                                   189

          Matching howledge and skills to the economic needs o f the country (in particular for
          vocational and folk education), with suitable quality assurance

3.1.4 ChallengesinManagement andInstitutionalArrangements

The Education sector is complex due to the number o f Ministries involvedas well as the number o f training
institutes that are widespread and not all coordinated. Improvement on coordination and interlinkages
between the sub sectors is considered important. At the core o f these challenges identified:
          Develop comprehensive policy and supportive institutional, legal framework for ECD,
          including decisions on supervision and co-ordination o f sub-sector

          Establishing and maintaining an effective monitoring and evaluation system in education
          (sector PAF)

          Coordinating sub-sectors to optimize linkages and reduce overlaps in functions by some
          institutions
          Consolidating Adult and Non-Formal Education programmes to meet EFA - and MKUKUTA
          -targets

          Rationalizing numerous and un-coordinated small scale projects within a sector-wide
          approach

          Addressing the split o f Vocational, Folk and Technical Education between different
          Ministries, Departments and Agencies

          Ensuringtertiary institutions do not resort to intermittent closures

          Developing an explicit national and prioritized strategy on capacity building

          Accelerating the full implementation o f key Government reforms (e.g. decentralization, public
          service reform and finance management, mainstreamingo f ongoing projects/programmes),

          Optimizing the use o fwell established Teachers Resource Centres (TRCs).

3.1.5. Cross CuttingIssues

The sector analysis underlined the challenges for CCI in particular to transform policies, strategies
into actions and behaviour change

          Assessiflg and addressing the impact o f HIV/AIDS on the education system (deaths of
          teachers and pupils and absenteeism due to illness)

          Developing a gender strategy for education and helping girls to fulfil their potential and
          perform equally with boys at all levels

          Ensuringthat education plays a positiverole inenvironmental management

          Ensuringthat education plays a role inaddressing HIV/AIDs.


        3.2.     THEPUBLICEXPENDITUREREVIEW(PER)ANDTHEMATICWORK ONFINANCIAL ISSUES

The Public Expenditure Review and the thematic discussion isolated a number o f priorities in
improving the functioning o f the planning, budgeting, disbursement and monitoring processes,
notably:




                                                   190

        Development and costing o f outcome oriented, full education sector planning including
        prioritisation according to MKUKUTA goals and expected outputs (including for pre-
        primary, special needs and AE/NFE)
        The need for budgeting to be based on well-researched unit costs, with attention to
        procurement and the supply o f materials

        The need to address mismatches between approved estimates and disbursements, as well as
        partial and unpredictable releases
        The challenge o f exploiting more fully existing sources o f funds

         Securing a more efficient financial transfer system to local government authorities

                        3.3. PERFORMANCEASSESSMENT         FRAMEWORK       (PAF)

The Performance Assessment Framework set o f f from the premise that, within a government-led
reform process where multiple stakeholders are involved, both accountability and management
objectives would be served by a transparent, reliable and agreed system o f criteria, indicators and
benchmarks with which to assess achievements and evolution within the education sector. Key issues
identified were:

        A Sector PAF is needed; monitoring is fragmented and incomplete
        Institutional arrangements should be agreed upon to develop and operationalise the Sector
        PAF (co-ordination, management o f data collection, processing, analysis, dissemination and
        reporting)

        Harmonization o f existing M I S in the education sector, and revision and integration into
        ESMIS should be given a highpriority

        Key performance indicators should be identified for the whole sector (for ESMIS). At the
        same time,     sub-sector M I S should be developed, all in line with the Poverty Monitoring
         System (PMS) for MKUKUTAand the PAF for General Budget Support.

                                  3.3. EDUCATIONSECTOR DIALOGUE

The Education Sector Dialogue provided insight for improving the quality o f dialogue on sector
policies, budgets, expenditures and performance in the Tanzanian education sector. As a reference it
assisted the thematic group inlooking at the type o f problems which appear to exist at the sector level
across much o f the Tanzanian civil service and to think through, inpreparationfor the dialogue theme
at the Review, how sector dialogue might be improved to generate more efficient outcomes in ways
consistent with the principles o f the JAS. The dialogue should serve as a tool to promote education
sector objectives and achieve the MKUKUTA goals. The thematic group discussions led to a
proposed framework that focuses on the improvement o f the dialogue between all relevant
stakeholders inthe education sector inline with the new initiatives o f MKUKUTAand JAS.
Key issues include:

                                                   191

     e  Effective Government leadership o f the dialogue and linking the dialogue to Government
        structures.

        Development o f a full sector education strategic plan

        Improving policy choices, prioritisation, planning and budgeting

        Information based decision making. Facilitating monitoring and reporting o f sector
        performance (sector PAF/ESMIS)

     e   Strengthening partnership with all stakeholders including Ministries Departments and
        Agencies (MDAs), Local Government Authorities(LGAs), CSOs, Non-State Actors(NSAs)
        and Development Partners (DPs),

        Undertakeresearch and pilot new ways o f working.

        Minimizing transaction costs

        Improved information sharing, transparency and informationreliability

         Alignment and timing o f dialogue with core national processes (of planninghudgeting
        PEIUMTEF,LGRP,PSRP, PFMRP)




   4. SUMMARY OF FINDINGSFROMTHE REVIEW PRESENTATIONSAND
                                            DISCUSSIONS

Inpreliminary discussions at the launch of the review, participants noted that with well-defined
MKUKUTAoperational targets inplace, the time had come to focus on the transformational power o f
education. The concept o f lifelong learning extended this process beyond the traditionally recognized
school years. Education should include life skills, values and attitudes to help learners contribute to
poverty reduction. International standards should be balanced with local needs and skills for daily life.
Participants called for sufficient places for learning at all levels, noting for example that as the
primary sector i s expanded also plans need to be made for the secondary, vocational and higher
sectors. Education should also encompass the design o f alternative pathways for young people out o f
school.

                       4.1     OVERALLEDUCATIONSECTOR COORDINATION

The coordination o f the ESDP i s done by the PMO which chairs the Inter Ministerial Steering
Committee (IMSC) comprising Permanent Secretaries o f Sub Sector ministries and the Advisory
Committee (AC), which i s composed by Directors o f Policy and Planning o f Sub Sector Ministries
and representatives from stakeholder DPs and CSOs. Below the IMSC are the Sub-sector
Development Committees, which are responsible for over-seeing development o f the Sub Sectors in
line with National policies. The first i s the Basic Education Development Committee (BEDC) which
i s responsible for the development o f the basic education sub sector that encompasses pre-primary,
primary, secondary, non formal and teacher education (certificate and diploma levels). The second i s
the Tertiary and Higher Education Development Committee (THEDC) which i s responsible for
development o f tertiary, higher and technical education. The third i s the Folk and Vocation Education
Development Committee (FVEDC). The overall review observation i s that the sub sector planning
happens in isolation o f one another. This urges for an integrated strategic planning process for the
education sector explaining the role o f all MDA's which actors are. The role o f IMSC has been to:-



                                                   192

    0   Oversee implementation, development and execution o f decisions o f national education
        policies and their inter linkages with other sector policies;

    0   Monitor ESDP

         Provide higher level inter ministerial coordination and;

    0    Guarantee ESDP consistency with broad government policies. Indeed it i s through its
         guidance that the Education Sector Review has come into h i t i o n ;


Recognizing that ESDP i s at the heart o f Education Reformthere i s the needto develop
    0    An effective coordinationmechanismthat entails clear linksand defines roles o fall actors

    0    An explicit holistic capacity buildingfor the whole sector andthe agencies involved
         Strengthening linkages between districts and the centre as well as between PER, PAF, PMS
         and Sector dialogue structures to enhance their contributions to MKUKUTA wide sector
         approach andjointly determine the education outcomes.

                                     4.2     CROSSCUTTINGISSUES

The Review noted in various discussions the importance o f CCI for inclusion in the programmes of
the various sub-sectors. Apart from the traditional composition within CCI, namely HIV-AIDS,
Gender and Environment, the Review referred regularly to other cross-cutting and cross-sectoral
issues like an Institutional and Organisational Analysis o f the education sector, Integrated Human
Resources Development o f teachers, instructors, facilitators and the sector PAF as important areas for
attention inthe development o f a full education sector plan.


Although specific strategic plans and policy guidelines on HIV/AIDS have been developed for the
education sector, implementation generally i s slow and not well-coordinated. Many NGOs and CSOs
are strongly involved inthe awareness raising, capacity buildingand monitoring.
Also national guidelines and policies for environmental conservation and management have been
issued and capacity building in the education sector has been organised in limited scale, mainly for
primary education, but ideas on Education for Sustainable Development (ESD) have not yet received
much national and cross education-level attention in Tanzania by which for instance the concept o f
`eco-schools' could take roots.

The national strategy on gender i s in a finalising phase, which will encourage the education sector to
react with a more specific strategy. It was noted that to bring about desired changes, guidelines and
policies should be distributed amongst all stakeholders and users. In addition, practical follow up by
management in the education (sub) sector(s) need to be given adequate attention. Most MDAs have
now their gender-focal persons. Effective follow up o f policies and strategies in place require
practical, workplace oriented interventions and close collaboration, partnership with key players in
the sector.
One o f the tools to assist in monitoring CCI would be the sector PAF, which has proposed
disaggregated data on gender and supports for explicit monitoring o f CCI. Also the PMS for
MKUKUTA has linked gender differentiation to most o f its indicators. Mainstreaming o f the cross-
cutting issues into the budgetingand planning (MTEF-PER)process i s o f similar, great importance.
An important link was made with Sector dialogue where it was noted that the collaboration on CCI
with other stakeholders (other relevant ministries, NGOs, CBOs, partnerships with business (PPP),
development partners) need to be improved. Partnership will grow through more extensive dialogue,
dynamic information exchange on CCI and introduction o f innovative methods like coaching through
peer group systems and action-research.

                                                   193

                                      4.3     FINDINGSONQUALITY

The Review noted insufficient progress for all types and levels o f educationon quality and efficiency.
Quality was discussed in the various sessions for all sub sectors. This chapter i s included to stress the
importance o f qualitative aspects o f education in general but should not distract from sub sector
specific quality issues as mentioned inthe SituationAnalysis.
A number o f challenges continue to hinder smooth implementation o f sub sectoral programmes and
plans and achievements in improved quality o f which the acute shortage o f teachers at all levels was
pointed out with great concern. A core reason i s the absence o f a full sector coherent plan which
strengthens the linkages between the different types o f education, with clear goals and targets for all
sub sectors, with clear definitions and strategies for cross-sectoral issues such as quality, efficiency,
access and equity. Furthermore a sector PAF/ESMIS i s not yet operational and an agreement on what
`quality education' entails , make identifying qualitative indicators and monitoring o f education
performance difficult.


Improved quality o f teaching and learningwill have a positive impact on learning outcomes. There are
many factors and actors that determine the quality o f this complex process o f teaching and learning
like training o f teachers, adequate numbers and quality o f human resources that manage, teach,
monitor etc. in the education sector, books both with good quality and inadequate quantities,
relevance o f curricula etc. Communities and in particular parents should be in the confident position
to trust that their children are in the hands o f capable, well trained and responsible teachers. The
Review noted therefore the cross-sectoral need for Human Resource Development for teachers,
trainers, managers and lecturers.

The urgency o f the improved quality for all was stressed at the Review as rampant poverty and
persistent gender inequities have adversely impacted on attendance and performance and in general
threaten the full realization o f the sector goals and objectives. Inorder to achieve qualitative reforms
government needs to play a strong leadingrole, based on a sound strategic plan with proper resources.


The Review mentionedkey challenges for urgent attention. These include:

         Quality o f teaching and learning process

         Relevance o f curricula and examinations (including stronger incorporationo f CCI)

         Choice o f instruction language (including TSL) and in particular the quality o f language
         teaching and use o f bothKiswahili and Englishby teachers

        More systematic teacher (management) training, development, motivation and deployment

         Equal access also for girls, disabled, poor and vulnerable pupils and students

        Improved school facilities (classrooms, teacher houses, staff rooms, desks, teaching and
         learningmaterials including ICT, toilets, water)

         Increasedattentionto life skills, reproductivehealth, hygiene HIV/AIDS awareness

        Decentralisation ineducation management

         Strengthening inspectorate

        Development o f ESMIS




                                                    194

                  4.4       FINDINGS EDUCATION
                                        ON                SECTOR SITUATIONANALYSIS


4.4.0    Introduction

The Review recognised concerted efforts to develop the education sector for the last five years.
Increased number o f institutions, immediate beneficiaries and budget at all levels i s one o f clear
indications o f commitment towards improvement o f education provision inthe country. Despite these
efforts, there are still some challenges that are supposed to be resolved in order to realise effective
sector development as a way o f leading to realizing MKUKUTA targets. These are presented by sub-
sector specific to facilitate clear follow-up o f issues raisedby this review.

4.4.1    BasicEducation

The basic education i s divided into four sub-sectors namely pre-primary and primary, secondary
education, teacher education (Certificate and Diploma level teachers) and the adult and non-formal
education. The review noted positive developments in the basic education, especially due to the
implementation o f PEDP and SEDP but called for the need to sustain the two reforms and
concentrating more on quality improvement and qualified teacher supply. Specific Review
recommendations are:

A. Pre-PrimaryandPrimaryEducationlevel

The review noted significant developments at primary level, especially duringthe period o f PEDP but
noted that pre-primary level has not been given due attention. It was felt that the positive
developments o f PEDP need to be sustained beyond 2006 in line with EFA targets and MDGs
addressing the challenges encountered during implementation o f the current programme. Yet, the
review came out with the following specific recommendations.

0   Curriculum reforms to include teacher development and availability o f better teaching and
    learning materials, and pilot testing methods;
0   Sustain positive PEDP developments and put more attention on disadvantaged learners, those with
    special needs and out o f school children;
    Ensure adequate teacher supply and differentiate primary and pre-primary more clearly to ensure
    teachers are allocated correctly between the two
    and enhance more Government commitment to pre-primary;
    Improve the budgeting process for pre-primary and primary, to create all-inclusive education
    development plans for Ministries to use by usinginformation from all LGAs;
0   Conduct effective monitoring and supervision at the sub-sector.

B. SecondaryEducation

The Review noted the efforts made to improve performance at secondary education level through
implementation o f SEDP (2005 - 2009). Through SEDP the Government i s implementinga number o f
reforms geared towards realizing access, equity, quality, management reforms and crosscutting issues
targets throughout the duration o f SEDP.
Yet there are several challenges on the basis o f which recommendations are made, specifically for
secondary education. These include the following.

0   Improve projections o f teacher numbers fi-om universities and TTCs;
    Increaseuse o f distance education to meet expanded demand;
    Revisit the cost o f the sub-sector inrelation to the expansion;
0   Secondary education to reflect the challenges of regional initiatives -EAC, SADC, AU, including
    educational aspects.


                                                      195

C.Adult andNonformal Education

Regarding the Adult and Non-Formal Education, the Review noted some form o f developments
especially with regard to provision o f basic education to out-of-school children and youth and adults
through COBET (Complementary Basice Education and Training) and ICBAE (Integrated
Community Based Adult Education) initiatives. Inspite o f this there are a number o f issues that were
raised at the review workshop and participantsrecommended the need for:
    Improved coordination o f CSO-activities and increase the AE and NFEto reach at least 3% o f the
    national education sector budget
    considering an independent sub-vote for AE/NFE and elevate the status o f the AE and NFEunit
    Concentrating more on vulnerable youth and adults, improve access in disadvantaged areas and
    also exploit benefits o fpast policies on adult education -primary schools as a focal point.
4.4.2 FolkDevelopmentand VocationalTraining

The level o f education and training has been improving slowly across the country. In each region
there i s at least one vocational training institution and a folk development college. Both are producing
some skilled individuals who are expected to employ themselves after the training. However there are
challenges that came out o f the review. Participants and members recommended the following actions:
    Establishand strengthen demand driven training in folk and vocational training;
    Create stronger links around planning and budgeting, monitoring and evaluation, and a National
    Qualification Framework;
    Include Folk Educationinthe structure under BEDC;
    Establisha sub-sector reformprogramme for VET and FDE including sub-sector MIS;
    Upgrade obsolete VET and FDC infrastructure.
    The use andpercentage o fthe Skill Development Levy (SDL) shouldbe reassessed
4.4.3 Tertiary and Higher Education Science and Technology

This level o f education has developed relatively fast with liberalisation policies o f the mid to late
1990s. The number o f Tertiary, Higher and Technical Education Institutions has increased together
with increased enrolment o f both boys and girls. Yet a number o f challenges remain un-resolved,
which led Review-participants to recommendthe following:
    Establish Reform programmes for post-secondary technical and higher education and sub sector
    MIS
    Identifying some quick wins inenhancing science and technology at all levels.
    Increase fundingfor the teaching, research and application o f science and technology
    Expand strategically to meet labour market and national development demands
    Strengthening quality assurance bodies for technical tertiary and higher education.
    Analyse and strengthen the student loans system for Higher Education and improve the loan
    recovery service


                         4.5 FINDINGS THE PUBLICEXPENDITURE
                                         ON                              REVIEW

The Review noted the importance for continuation to develop a costed planning process for the full
sector with MDAspreparing effective and realistic submissions. The subsectors continue to have their
own priorities, but there is a need to interlink and to achieve some regular form o fjoint planning and
budgeting in the education sector under the co-ordination o f MoF and MPEE. The PER-Education
Sector Report focused on three main areas: budget formulation process, performance (monitoring) and
costing o f priority sector interventions.



                                                     196

The need for clarity from the relevant ministries on what information and analysis would be useful for
planning purposes was expressed. The link with the PMS and sector PAF and specific indicators,
which could help to provide that information, i s clear. The information should be preferably carried
forward by MDAs rather than by public expenditure analysis from external consultants. The
development o f a sector MTEFPERprocess with maximumparticipation from stakeholders would be
very helpful in this regard. The DPs are expected to improve on financial predictability. The roles and
responsibilities for different stakeholders and specific forums should urgently be clearly defined and
agreed upon in order to realize more coherent planning process and could be one o f the main tools in
establishing a full education sector development fkamework. Joint planning and budgeting would also
assist inidentifying priorities inthe (sub)sector (s) and more precisely incalculating the funding gaps.

The Review reiterated earlier discussions on commitment to fundingthe education sector as a whole
via general and/or sector budget support.        It also strongly supported efforts to co-ordinate on
budgeting and planning across various ministries and local governments. One o f the reasons for
moving away from fundingsub-sectors to the sector as a whole i s to allow for a real process o f budget
contestation allowing GOT(in collaboration with its partners) to determine how best to allocate
limited funds to help translate its policy priorities into expenditure priorities. However, the current
institutional arrangements with several line ministries (4), PMO-RALG and LGAs are not conducive
to this. A strong co-ordinating role i s required by PMO, M o F and MPEE. The Review stressed the
need to put in place institutional arrangements to ensure a mechanism for prioritisation across and
within sub-sectors.

It is difficult at present to get a complete picture o f the Government o f Tanzania's expenditure on
education - covering all line ministries, PMO-RALG, LGAs and the present off-budget project
support in the sector managed by different sub departments at national and/or LGAs, NGOs at
community level. Efforts to work to provide budget information on an annual basis i s needed to
ensure transparency, more efficiency and to allow stakeholders to track trends in spending. This
should include alignment to fiscal decentralisation; simplify transfer systems to LG level including
harmonisation o f PEDP transfers (development and capitation grants) with developments for
education sector block grant and LG Capital Development Grant.

The Review established important links with the Sector Dialogue. Whilst the government i s in the
lead role it i s important that agreement i s reached on timing and consultation processes by all
stakeholders around budget formulation and execution.        Development o f the budget should be a
collaborative process firstly internally within one MDA and with improved communication and
coherence among different MDAs and LGAs in the education sector and members o f parliament.
Indeed, civil society, private sector and DPs have a right to be informed as they could provide
important inputs into the process. Public-private partnerships could assist in more resources and
efficiency, outcome oriented planning e.g. by encouraging tax privileges and VAT exemptions for
increased private investment ineducation development.

Recommendations made at the Review

    Develop a costed planning process for the full sector with clear and timely (October) budget
    guidelines and MDA's effective submissions.
    Develop full sector MTEFPER-process including Public Expenditure Financial Accounting and
    ReportingProcess (PEFARP)
0   Increase awareness and capacity building amongst MDAs and LGAs on MKUKUTA and its
    impact on coherent, cluster-oriented planning and budgeting.
    DP's improve onpredictability o f funding for GBS and SBS
    EstablishPublic Private Partnerships

                   4.6    FINDINGS    ONPERFORMANCEASSESSMENT               FRAMEWORK

The Review noted that the importance for an education sector PAF/ESMIS i s connected to the
NSGRP/MKUKUTA which urges for an outcome oriented approach in the fight against poverty. In
order to measure progress towards Cluster Two goals o f `equitable access to quality primary and
secondary education for all, universal literacy and expansion o f higher, technical and vocational
education' the sector requires a coherent and reliable monitoring tool.

Due to the fact that full sector ESDP developments only currently started, the sector Ministries have
till recently concentrated (E)MIS on their own sub sectors or departments and therefore general
progress in the sector i s difficult to measure. The Review noted that there i s need for a monitoring
system which assists education planners and policy makers with information based decision making
and provides a clear image o f the performance and reformprocess inthe education sector.
The PAF for monitoring GBS with 3 core indicators and the PMS with 1426 core indicators are
considered useful tools, but insufficient to measure more detailed progress in the sector and
inadequate for (sub) sector planning purposes. EMIS development within the former MoEC had a
strong sub sector (primary school) bias.
The advantages o f a full sector PAF are many. The sector PAF could encourage stronger
harmonisation o f different sub sector MIS, could be utilised as important source o f infomation in
joint planninghdgeting and collaboration among MDAs and could stimulate deeper reflection about
monitoring in general, its use for planning, its operationalisation and dissemination o f information
(including to communities). A thematic group on monitoring produced a final draft PAF document
and small paper for discussion at the review.

Recommendationsmade at the Review
     Continue the work in the thematic group on the PAF with an increased number o f participants in
     order to raise awareness on the PAF and encourage further development and operationalisation
     (including costing and capacity building) o f PAF/ESMIS.
0    PMO should coordinate the PAF-development andbe the central point for assembling data.
                                4.7      FINDINGSONSECTORDIALOGUE

The Review noted that the dialogue in the Education sector has not been satisfactory because it has
focused primarily on the sub sector for primary education instead o f the whole sector. In addition, it
has been closely linked to funding decision making which has to some degree led to micro
management and has diverted the dialogue away fiom discussions on core strategic issues and overall
policy directions.

A proposal to revisit and improve the dialogue to serve as a tool to promote education sector
objectives and achieve the MKUKUTA goals was presented at the Review. The framework focuses
on the improvement o f dialogue between all relevant stakeholders in the education sector in line with
the new initiatives o f MKUKUTA and JAS. It mentions specific objectives for the education sector
dialogue with a core focus on outcomes for learners which entail the following aspects: improved
quality o f education service delivery by informed education policy choices, enhanced government
planning, budgeting, monitoring and reporting based on a sector assessment framework. The policy
choices take in consideration cross cutting issues, alignment with government reforms, holistic
capacity building strategies, equity and equal access. Furthermore other important general objectives
o f the sector dialogue are the promoting o f Government ownership, strengthening partnership around
common goals and enhancing mutual accountability and timely and open information sharing between
the Government and other stakeholders.



26According to last proposals presented at Cluster 11-meetingat MPEE, 7th of March 2006
                                                      198

It is important to follow certainprinciplesto encourage an effective dialoguebothat centralas well as
local government level The following principles were presented: Government leadership, involvement
o f all relevant stakeholders , openness and frankness, mutual accountability, trust and respect,
availability o f transparent, complete information, facilitation o f contestability, cost effectiveness,
linkages to established Government structures and procedures and good timing in relation to the
annual planning-budgeting-executioncycle.

Effectiveness o f the dialogue would be assisted by an agreement on the different functions o f the
dialogue, i)information provision, ii)structured reporting, iii)consultation or iv) participatory
decision making and v) cross sectoral learning. This would then help to clarify roles and
responsibilities o f different stakeholders in the dialogue. Restructuring, revision and revitalisation o f
the existing, but limited ESDP-dialogue i s needed. For each dialogue forum, Terns o f References
including objectives, functions o f dialogue and definition o f roles and responsibilities among the
actors must be defined and agreed uponby all stakeholders.

Recommendationsmade at the Review
     To further develop the proposed institutional structure composed o f forums like an Annual Sector

     PERREFARDialogue, GBS dialogue structure
     Continue the work o f the thematic group on sector dialogue under guidance o f the Ministry o f
     Finance and continue with PAF discussion Working Group under the PMO.

     To revisit the existing structure for a clear and more robust horizontal communication as well as
     internal vertical communication.

     relation to annual planning - budgeting - execution cycle.
     function, participation, roles, responsibilities, main tasks, timing and frequency o f meeting) in


     Proposed fora for dialogue were: the annual sector review and its high level meeting that should
     take place four weeks after the sector review; a budgeting and planning forum; the PERREFAR
     and the GBS dialogue.

     An important role for PMO to maintain and put `new life' to the existing ESDP structure
     including its widening it to allow full sector dialogue.
     The statement o f TENMET (reasoning the non-participation at the Review) stressed the
     importance o f inclusiveness, effective engagement, full participation and the importance o f
     sharing information between all stakeholders and the public.

INTRODUCTION

In light of sector reform developments and a changing aid environment, the
Ministry of Water (MOW)as the Government of Tanzania's (GOT)lead agency
on the water sector, and its Development Partners (DPs) have embarked on a
new mechanism of cooperationand dialogue.This new mechanismis basedon
the principles of the new Joint Assistance Strategy (JAS) and the emerging
sector development and planning framework. The new arrangements shall
accommodate "co-ordination, collaborationand dialogue" in one single efficient
and sustainable working mechanism.

In this sense, the first Joint Water Sector Review (JWSR) has established the
basis of a joint process for future performance monitoringand priority setting in
the water sector based on a mechanism of continuous partnership dialogue
between all stakeholders, including GOT, DPs, civil society and the private
sector.

The following objectives have been achieved during the first JWSR:
    a to assess the status and performanceof the sector against previous
       monitoringresultsand based on establishedmonitoringsystems,
    ~j to set performancedefinitions and benchmarksfor the coming year,
    3  to highlightfocal issues to be tackled in the coming year,
    rj to review stakeholder consultationand dialogue processes and establish
       an agreed dialogue and co-ordinationmechanism.

The JWSR included all sub-sectors, namely Water Resources Management
(WRM), Urban Water Supply and Sewwage (commercialisedWSS) and Rural
Water Supply and Sanitation (community-ownedWSS). In addition, the policy,
planning and monitoring processes as well as dialogue and eo-ordination
issues have been reviewed and discussed.

The main outputs of the JWSR are:
   3   consensus on the current situation of the water sector,
   3   a performance monitoring framework, which takes into account
       requirementsof MKUKUTA cluster indicatorsand MDG targets,
   zs an agreed mechanism for sector co-ordination and dialogue between
       GOTand DPs as well as for the involvement of other stakeholders,
       agreed priority undertakings for the period until the next JWSR in
       September 2007.

       consultations beween the MOW and the DPs in a series of meetings,
       group work and field visits. Inaddition and in order to guide the process,
       ajoint Task Force had been established.

The main and concluding event was the Stakeholders Meeting on September
19and 20, 2006, which brought together all water sector stakeholders, namely
GOT Ministries, Development Partners, NGOs, Private Sector, Academic
Institutions, Water User Entities, Basin Water offices, Water Utilities, the
nationalregulator EWURA and the media.

Some 180 participants were asked to review and comment on progress in the
water sector and on the priorityactions identifiedfor the following year.


CURRENTSITUATION OF THE WATER SECTOR

Tanzania's Development Vision 2O2fi2 aims at achieving an absence of abject
poverty and attaining a high quality of life for all people by 2025. Water supply,
sanitation and water resource management feature prominently in the
Development Vision. Intrinsic to these overall targets, are the objectives of
equity of access, water management capacity, and proper maintenance of
water and sanitation systems, use of environmentally sound technologies, and
effectivewater tariffs, billingand revenue collection mechanisms.

In the medium term, MKUKUTA commits Tanzania to achieving the M!llennium
Development Goals (MDGs) for access to safe water, sanitation and a
sustainable environment, while also setting targets for 2010. The MKUKUTA
targets for WRM are: to reduce water related environmental pollution tevels
from 20% in 2003 to 10% in 2010 and put into place an operational integrated
water resources management in all basins by 2010. The MKUKUTA, MDGs
and Vision 2025 targets for water services are to increase the proportion of the
rural populationwith access to clean and safe water from 53% in 2003 to 65%
by 2010, 79% by 2015 and to 90% by 2025 (respectively) for the rural
population. They also call for increasedaccess to clean and safe water to the
urban population to rise from 73% in 2003 to 90% by 2010, to 95% by 2015;
and to 100%by 2025 inthe same order.



* United RepubfgcofTanzania (1999),"DevelopmentVision 2025 for Tanzania" Oar es Salaam.




                                           202

Based on routine data collection conducted by MOW,to monitor water service
coverage as of June 2006, the populationwith access to clean and safe water
was 54.7% for rural areas and 74% for urban areas, During the same period,
the sector maintained access to sewerage service at 17% but the service is
available only in parts of Dar es Salaam, Mwanra, Arusha, Moshi, Tanga,
Morogoro, Dodoma, Tabora and lringa. This coverage enables about 25% of
the wastewater generated daily to be collected and disposed of through
seweragesystems.

Sased on the Household Budget Surveys, (2001), basic sanitation coverage
(as measured by access to latrines) is as high as 90%. However, most latrines
fail to meet MDG defined standards for sanitation. By MDG standards,
coverage would be closer to 50%.

Inorder to achieve the above listedtargets, the sector has been movingtoward
a Sector Wide Approach to Planning(SWAP). The main elements of the SWAP
have either been established beforehand (such as the national water policy
NAWAPO); have been prepared to be launched during this JWSR (such as the
dialogue mechanism and the performance monitoring framework); or are
expected to be confirmed shortly. The latter include the National Water Sector
Development Strategy (NWSDS); the water legislation; the Water Sector
Development Programme (WSDP) as well as the joint funding mechanism
("Water Basket")which will be used to fund significant portions of the WSDP.


KEY ISSUES ARISINQ FROM THE REVIEW PROCESS

During the preparation of the JWSR, consensus was reached to highlight three
areas in the sector situation analysis: planning, financing, and performance
monitoring. In addition, a Sector Dialogue and Coordination mechanism was
proposed as an importantarea for adoption at the JWSR.

CONCLUSION

The JWSR 2006 is considered as highly useful in moving the sector dialogue
forward and agreeing on the joint agenda for the coming year.

Germany and the World Bank will co-chair the DPG-Water for the period until
the JWSR 2007.

AH parties agreed to contribute to implementing the undertakings and tackling
the emerging issues. This will be taken forward within the established Water
Sector Working Group and the thematic working groups. Detailed work plans
for implementing the undertakings should be provided before the next WSWG
meeting based on the action points documented in the JWSR proceedings.

ANNEX 9. DRAFT"A PROPOSEDDEFINITION OF A SATISFACTORYSECTORREVIEW IN
TERMS OF CONTENTSAND PROCESS"
                                                   DRAFT



A ProposedDefinition of a Satisfactory Sector Review interms of Contents
                                                and Process


    (i)INTRODUCTION

Sector reviews are expected to carry out three key functions

         As integral components o f the national planning and budgeting system, and in combination
         with cluster level integration processes, sector reviews are expected to include feedback on
         sector performance and broad dialogue on sector policy and expenditure plans, providing
         essential inputsto national policy, plans and budgets

         As public processes, based on publicly available information and allowing broad based
         domestic feedback, they are potentially a key instrument for strengthening domestic
         accountability, contributing to a broad public awareness o f sector issues that can strengthen
         the role o f formal institutions o f accountability, especially Parliament

    0    Less centrally, but very importantly for aid strategy, they are expected to provide the base for
         performance monitoring and policy dialogue for DPs, allowing accountability to DPs to be
         fulfilled ina way that i s supportive o f broad accountability to all stakeholders.


These objectives apply across all areas o f government, notjust those identified in the PAF matrix. For
many if not all sectors, full realization o f these objectives remains some way off. Getting them to
function as envisaged above i s a major challenge for GOT,DPs and domestic stakeholders. Sector
reviews are not equivalent to large public meetings on a particular sector. Effective sector reviews
depend on effective processes o f M&E, sector analysis, planning and budget systems, and sector
dialogue that continue through the year. Public meetings between Government DPs and other
stakeholders can provide a focal point for bring together the outcome o f these underlying processes,
but cannot substitute for suchprocesses where absent.

Thisnote sets out criteria for judgingthe quality o f the review process and a process for raising issues
that arise from the dialogue to the GBS Review.


    (ii)JUDGINGTHEQUALITYOFTHEREVIEWPROCESS

The implementation o f MKUKUTA centers on the alignment, update and regular review o f sector
strategies and local plans. The reference inthe PAF matrix to `satisfactory sector reviews' implies
that:

    (i)    Sector reviews are heldregularly as input to the budget preparation process. They also feed
           into the MKUKUTA annual progress reporting. This includes:
                    No requirement for one annual formal event, but rather opportunities for dialogue
                    over the course o f the year
                    Quality and frequency o f formal dialogue i s crucial
                                                     209

               0   Clear links between budget and dialogue process

    (ii)  They involve a wide range o f stakeholders under the leadership o f Government. This
                 includes:
               0   Timely circulation o f information, adequate time for consultation, and timely
                   communication o f government response
               0   Agreement on categories o f stakeholders (including how cross cutting2' issues are
                   addressed)
               0   Clear co-ordination mechanism, especially where a sector cuts across several
                   ministries
               0   Each category of stakeholders should choose its own representatives
               0   Agreement on balance o f allocation o f seats between different stakeholders where
                   there i s a public meeting

    (iii)Theirpurposeistoreviewdevelopmentsinthesector andtoassessperformanceagainst
          agreed benchmarks and targets. This includes:
               0   A clear view of financing, where financing hasbeentargeted (area,level, trend), and
                   what the performance has been;
               0   Reporting on progress related to outputs and outcomes
               0   Reporting on progress related to reformprocesses
               0   Identification o f priorities going forward to inform the the budget

    (iv)  They are based on reliable information and data from either National Bureau o f Statistics or
          relevant Government Institution (MDAs or sectors) implementing a specific project or
          programme. This includes:
                   Functioning MIS system that can provide reliable, timely data
                   Existence o f an agreed performance assessment framework and key indicators

    (v)   Dialogue covers a broad range o f issues including:

               (a)      Review o f action on previous year recommendations;
               (b)      Progress towards agreed targets and results;
               (c)      Consistency o fbudget and resource allocation decisions; and
               (d)      Identification o fpriorities and focal actions for the coming year

The PRBS assessment will draw on the sector review conclusions inthose sectors which have met the
necessary conditions for review processes to take place, based on an agreed timetable as to which
sectors will be identified for PRBS assessment inthe coming period. Interim measures/indicators will
be needed for sectors not currently covered by adequate review processes, with the intention that such
processes should be developed as soon as possible.

    (iii)PROCESSFORJUDGINGTHEQUALITYOFTHEREVIEWPROCESS

The proposed process or modality for judging the quality o f a review process:

a) Using the criteria above, DPs involved in a sector jointly reflect back over the year and write a
short report indicating their view on the quality o f the review processes.

b) This note is then shared with the sector lead agency on the government side for discussion and
agreement on priorities. Normally, issues arising from sector processes will be taken up by


27Includinggender, HIV/AIDS andenvironment
                                                     210

stakeholders in the sector over the coming year, but some may be raised up to the GBS Annual
Review proper (see point d below).

c) The joint DP/government note will then be forwarded to the Joint Thematic Working Group
(TWG) for the relevant cluster, where is will be discussedalongwith inputsfrom other stakeholders.

d) Key issues across the sector will thenbe consolidated and synthesized. These will thenbe proposed
by the TWG for further discussion (and possible action) at the GBS Annual Review proper.

d) In exceptional cases, DPs in a sector may apply the critieria in section iiand conclude that an
annual review process i s unsatisfactory. In these exceptional cases, this opinion will be discussed at
the HOClevel. Ifthey agree with the conclusion, then the process for dealing with this i s outlined in
section iv below.


    (iv) WHAT IFA REVIEW PROCESSI S UNSATISFACTORY?

Ifit is concluded that there are specific issues inthe annual sector review processthat preventit from
fulfillingthe satisfactory review criteria o f the GBS as outlined above, then the following steps will
be applied:

    J The identifiedconcerns will be discussed by the TWGand a summary note outliningthe key
         issues and suggested actions, i.e, temporary process actions, that need to be undertaken by the
         Sector over the coming year will be developed and agreed.

    J This note will then be presented at a meeting between PEWGBS Secretariat and TWG for
         further discussion. At this stage the note may be further modified taking on board the views
         arising from this meeting.

    J Thenotewill thenbepresentedat the annualGBSReview elaborating the following:

         (a) Specific issues that have led to an unsatisfactory review process inthe sector concerned;

         (b) Identified temporary process actions which have to be undertaken over the coming year
             by the sector to address the weaknesses identified in sector performance and the review
             process.

    J Inthe case of a lack of progress in addressing these temporary actions over the year by the
         sector, this may lead to a possible reduction infinancing by some GBS Partners




                                                   211

ANNEX 22. FUNDRELATIONSNOTE
IMFExecutive Board Completes Sixth Review Under Tanzania's PRGFArrangement and
Approves a Three-Year Policy Support Instrument
Press Release No. 07/26
February 16,2007
The Executive Board o f the International Monetary Fund (IMF) today completed the sixth and last
review o f Tanzania's economic performance under a three-year Poverty Reduction and Growth
Facility (PRGF) arrangement (see Press Release No. 03/128). The completion o f the review enables a
further release o f SDR 2.8 million (about US$4.2 million) and will bring the total disbursements
under the arrangement to SDR 19.6 million (about US$29.4 million).

The Executive Board also approved a three-year Policy Support Instrument (PSI) for Tanzania to
support the country's economic efforts. The PSI seeks high and sustainable growth and more rapid
poverty reduction based on enhancing public resource mobilization and efficiency o f spending,
increasing the financial sector's contribution to growth and the effectiveness o f the monetary policy,
and improving the business climate. Approval of a PSI for Tanzania signifies IMF endorsement o f
the policies outlined in the program.

The IMF's framework for PSIs i s designed for low-income countries that may not need, or want, IMF
financial assistance, but still seek IMF advice, monitoring and endorsement o f their policies. PSIs are
voluntary and demand driven. PSI-supported programs are based on country-owned poverty reduction
strategies adopted in a participatory process involving civil society and development partners and
articulated in a Poverty Reduction Strategy Paper (PRSP). This i s intended to ensure that PSI-
supported programs are consistent with a comprehensive framework for macroeconomic, structural
and social policies to foster growth and reduce poverty. Members' performance under a PSI i s
normally reviewed semi-annually, irrespective o f the status o f the program. (see Public Information
Notice No. 0.51145).

Following the Board's discussion on Tanzania, Mr.Murilo Portugal, Deputy Managing Director and
Acting Chairman, stated:

"Tanzania has achieved sustained strong economic performance through market-oriented policies
within an appropriate macroeconomic framework. To consolidate recent successes, Tanzania will
need to maintain sound policies and steadily pursue key structural reforms, including in the critical
energy sector, and capacity building to remove key impediments to growth and achieve lasting
inroads against poverty.

"The Tanzanian authorities are undertaking actions and commitments to address the allegations o f an
impropriety in the management o f the government external payment arrears account managed by the
Bank o f Tanzania. The authorities remain committed to transparency and to consult with the Fundin
adoptingand implementingappropriate remedial measures.

"The Policy Support Instrument i s viewed as the appropriate next step in the Fund's relations with
Tanzania. Tanzania's macroeconomic framework and structural reform agenda are consistent with the
government's objectives o f high and sustained broad-based economic growth and poverty reduction
within a stable macroeconomic environment.

"Tanzania i s undertahng structural reforms in three key areas. First, tax, customs, and capacity-
building reforms are expected to further. mobilize public resources, improve the efficiency o f
government spending, and support economic and social objectives. Second, financial sector reforms,
including improvements in credit information, pension investment guidelines, and liquidity
management, will help enhance this sector's contribution to growth and the effectiveness o f monetary


                                                  226

policy. Third, improvements in the business environment, including anti-corruption efforts, are
expected to facilitate productive private sector activity and investment.

"Fiscal policy should remain focused on economic and social objectives while limitingnet domestic
financing to avoid crowding out private sector credit. The monetary framework remains appropriate,
with the envisaged deceleration o f broad money growth consistent with inflation objectives while
still facilitating adequate credit to the private sector.

"Recent energy production shortfalls have been damaging. Resolving energy supply issues on a
timely basis, including through appropriate increases in generation capacity and tariffs as envisioned
under the PSI, will be critical to maintaining strong economic performance and puttingthe finances
o f the energy parastatal, TANESCO, on a sound and sustainable basis,'' Mr.Portugal said.
ANNEX
Recent Economic Developments

Inthe last three years Tanzania has achieved strong economic performance, securing high growth,
low inflation, adequate reserves, and a sustainable external debt position. Due to an energy crisis
caused notably by drought-related reductions in hydropower and higher fuel prices, real GDP growth
i s expected to slow to 5.9 percent in 2006, from 6.8 percent in 2005. The impact o f the energy crisis
on inflation has been limited so far, as businesses largely absorbed higher costs. The current account
deficit, however, widened to 9.3 percent in 2006 from 5.3 percent in 2005, as rapid growth inexports
was offset by higher oil an drought-related imports. Nonetheless, the overall balance o f payments
position remainedpositive due to continued strong inflows o f donor assistance and debt relief o f $3.8
billion receivedunder the Multilateral Debt ReliefInitiative (MRDI) in2006.

Program Summary

Tanzania's program under the PSI aims to achieve sustainable broad-based high growth and poverty
reduction, while maintaining macroeconomic stability. The program includes three core objectives:

  Enhancing public resource mobilization and efficiency of spending. Boosting Tanzania's revenues
would facilitate higher expenditures on key government priorities, particularly infrastructure, and
reduce the country's aid dependency over the long term. One goal o f the program i s therefore to raise
central government revenue to about 15.5 percent o f GDP through further tax customs administrative
reforms and tax policy measures. In addition, the government intends to improve efficiency in
spending by better aligning spending with poverty-reducing and growth-enhancing measures.
